                        FOREWORD

                           (First Edition)



        It has always been my earnest desire to preserve
every word of Shri Gurudev and to offer it to the world of
scholarship. |am exceedingly happy that the present work
is being    published   under the auspices of the Sahitya
Samskriti Mandal, Maharashtra State. My thanks are due

to them.

        The work, though partly in the form of Notes, will be
found    highly   useful    by   students    of   philosophy   and

research scholars.

        It is with great satisfaction that | place this scholarly

treatise in the hands of all those interested in Philosophy

and Religion.



Bombay                                  (Mrs.) VJAYA V. APTE
20th March 1986.
                           PREFACE
                           (First Edition)

      It is our great privilege to publish ‘Studies in Indian
Philosophy’, by late Prof. R. D. Ranade, a doyen of Indian
Philosophy.
      The history of philosophy is its own critique. The historical
process involves development, evaluation, attempt                at
cancellation and rehabilitation of doctrines.  Different Darsanas
of Indian philosophy have their own basic assumptions which
they sometimes share with others. Nyaya, Vaisesika, Samkhya,
Yoga or Dvaita Vedanta all share the doctrine of the reality of
the world. Similarly, Buddhism and Jainism, though very
different in approach from Advaita Vedanta, share with the latter
several epistemological doctrines. The Advaita system itself, to
which Shri Ranade belongs, has developed on account of the
intellectual warfare with other philosophical systems and the
conflicts arising within the system itself. Comparisons and
criticisms have always helped development of philosophical
thought. This is borne out by the present work also, though
briefly. We wish we had a fuller exposition of such comparison
 from this great master.
       We are happy that we are able to publish this yet
 unpublished work during Professor Ranade’s Birth Centenary
 Year. The Board deems it an honour that Shri Gurudev २. D.
 Ranade Samadhi Trust, Nimbal (R. S.), has asked the Board to
undertake the publication of this Volume. What more befitting
 tribute can be paid to late Professor R. D. Ranade, than bringing
 to light his hitherto unpublished works?


Bombay :                                      S. 9. BARLINGAY
Gudi Padava                                             Chairman,
Chaitra Shuddha Pratipada             Maharashtra State Board for
Shake 1908                         Literature and Culture, Bombay
10th April 1986.
                  EDITORIAL NOTE
                         (First Edition)
      The preservation of the thought of the Master throws on
the disciple a specia! responsibility of propagating his philosophy
in all its pristine purity. What a disciple or an Editor can do is
to add a foot-note here or a foot-note there.
      The Notes left by Gurudev 1२. 1). Ranade are valuable and
constitute a rich heritage. They contain original suggestions and
judgments and thus make a contribution to world-scholarship in
general and to Indian Philosophy in particular. They are highly
critical in value; though succinct, they are very lucid couched
that they are in a simple style. The logical and developmental
manner of arrangement of topics reflects his method of study
and presentation. It is our confidence that many a research scholar
would be benefited by a study of these Notes as they give a new
vision to look at Indian Philosophy. Particular mention may be
made of two sections of Mimamsa System, namely the Doctrine
of Sabda and the Doctrine of Sphota. The topic "Contribution
to the Science of Interpretation", will be very valuable for those
who are interested in the study of Linguistics, and by those who
want to specialise in the interpretation of the Hindu Law. In fact,
the science of interpretation is a basic discipline. These days
there are very few who study and appreciate the value of
Mimamsa      Philosophy.    Unfortunately,      it   is   dubbed   as
meaningless ritualism. The two sections treated in the present
brochure are an epitome of the fundamental Mimamsa teachings.
In our opinion, students of Linguistics cannot afford to disregard
Mimamsa doctrines.

       The Notes are unbiased and do not advocate any particular
‘ism’ of philosophy. Both the orthodox and heterodox schools
are subjected to a severe criticism whenever their theory is not
in conformity with logical reasoning. Neither the thrill of poetry
nor an emotional out-burst is allowed to have an edge over cool
philosophical reasoning.
Studies indndian Philosophy


      Under Six Systems (Sad-darsanas), Gurudev has included:
(i) Sankhya, (ii) Yoga, (iii) Buddhism,       (iv) Jainism,    (v)
Nydya-Vaisesika and (vi) Purva-Mimamsa. This classification
is different from the traditional one which (a) does not include
Buddhism and Jainism, (b) treats Nyaya and Vaisesika as
separate systems and (©) includes Vedanta or Uttara~-Mimainsa.
His new classification seems to be oriented towards grouping
non-Vedantic    Schools    together   and    treating   them    as
Purva-paksas, and as such Vedanta, constituting Uttara-paksa,
is taken out of the traditional fold. By the by the stand-point of
the Uttara-paksa, namely Vedanta, has already been elucidated
 in his volume, ‘Vedanta the Culmination of Indian Thought’
 published by Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay in 1970. A few
 of the Notes with some exposition appear in the above-mentioned
 volume.
       Gurudey Ranade’s interpretation of any doctrine is most
 natural. While scholars — both eastern and western — have
 laboured   hard to   understand correctly   the   import   of the
 Buddhistic doctrine of Pratitya-samutpada, Gurudevy takes the
 term in its most natural sense. He splits the word into two parts
 — Pratiti + Asamutpada which means that in Pratiti or intuition,
 there is Asamutpada i.e. non-creation of the world (P. 20). Only
 a God-realiser can give such an interpretation.
       It is really wonderful how he would translate a technical
 term from Indian Philosophy into a technical term from Western
 Philosophy instead of just explaining its meaning. Thus the
 technical term Nayabhasa (opposing standpoint) found in Jaina
 epistemology is rendered into the Kantian term Antinomy.
       His mathematical brain would always incline towards
 graphic presentation of the data wherever possible. Thus he
 would divide a page into two sections to present side by side
 the views of Prabhakara and Kumarila on all topics so that the
 similarities and differences between the two philosophical heavy
 weights (Pp. 68 ff) become at once obvious. The symbolic

                               Six
                                         Editorial Note (First Edition)


representation of systems expressing varying views on validity
of knowledge (P. 53) makes the whole discussion precise and
clear.
         The salient features of his writings— systematisation,
comparative approach, clarity of thought and                lucidity of
exposition,    chaste   language,    employment       of apt    western
terminology and doctrines to display ancient Indian thought in
the modern out-fit to make it more appealing — are all present
in these Notes though many of which are just jottings.
         Mostly Gurudey Ranade has not — barring one or two
places (e.g. P. 46) — specifically mentioned his own judgments
regarding a 1091० dealt with. But the brief outline of a topic is
interspersed with his own comments which can be easily noted
by a discerning reader. At times there are brief summaries of
standard works on a particular philosopher or a topic kept ready
for critical exposition.
         Sometimes. two drafts of a section are given — one rough
and one fair. If there is a specific instruction to omit the rough
draft, it is omitted. If, however, there is no such specific
instruction, the rough draft too is retained. In the latter case a
comparative study of both the drafts shows how the topics were
rearranged, the rearrangement showing that Prof. Ranade was
always thinking of the subject in order to put it in a still more
systematic way. In this connection, we may refer to two drafts
ofthe subject-headings of hisVedanta and Western Thought one
of which was prepared as carly as 1927.         =
         Sometimes the sub-sections are indicated as 1, 2, 3...;
sometimes as i, ii, ili....; or A, 3, C....; or evena, 0, c....; bracketed
or unbracketed. As every system was prepared independently
and as these are but Notes, his usual rigour of maintaining
uniformity is not found here. Again, we do not know whether
he wanted to develop each system into a separate book or prepare
one single volume comprising all the Six Systems.



                                 Seven
Studies in Indian Philosophy


         Notes on one section ‘Santaraksita and Kamalasila’ place
for which is indicated in Chapter III as well as Chapter V, are
not available. It is mentioned that this section is typed separately,
but nowhere could it be found. So too "Analysis" of the Yoga
Sutras referred to on Pp. 13 and 16 could not be traced.
    We have also included here his plan of the Pathway to
God bearing the sub-title: A Study in the Philosophy of
Beatificism. He wanted to write a volume on the Pathway to
God (see P. 109) for which his works on the Upanisads, the
Bhagavad-gita, Vedanta or the Three Pathways (Pathway to God
in Marathi Literature, Pathway to God in Hindi Literature and
Pathway to God in Kannada Literature) would constitute the
basis.
         Some of these Notes were taught by Shri Gurudev to his
M. A. students.
         Gurudev’s Bibliography given at the end of each of the
Six Systems would be.found useful by those who want to
undertake an intensive study of a system. The bibliography is
not just a list of books; it gives an analysis of the important
contents thereof. There are also thought-provoking comments
which would help a student to think independently and form his
 own judgment. In a couple of places, Gurudev’s Bibliography
 refers to the Call Number of a book ofthe Allahabad University
 Library.
          1 must express my gratitude to Mrs. Vijayatai V. Apte
 (daughter of Shri Gurudev) for having made available the
 material for publication. It is very kind of her to have written a
Foreword to this volume.
          My heartfelt thanks   ar due to Dr. S.      S.   Barlingay,
 Chairman, Shri S. D. Deshmukh, Secretary, of Sahitya Sanskriti
 Mandal, Maharashtra State, as well as Members of the Mandal
 for the keen interest they took in the publication of this work.
But for their kind gesture the work would not have seen the light
of day. The indefatiguable efforts which Shri Vidyadhar Gokhale

                                Eight
                                      Editorial Note (First Edition)

made in the furtherance of this sacred cause are beyond all praise.
Shri R. B. Alva, Director; Shri G. D. Dhond, Dy. Director; Shri
P. S. More, Manager, Shri A. C. Sayyed, Dy. Manager and the
concerned staff of Government Central Press, Bombay have laid
us under great obligation by their valuable help in the printing
of the volume.
      The MS was very old and in some places difficult to
decipher. It goes to the credit of Shri S. D. Shintre who took the
trouble of making a clean typed copy ready for the Press. Shri
V. G. Nitsure typed the Bibliography appended to the Six
Systems. Shri Vyankatesh Apte and Dr. P. M. Upadhye rendered
whatever help was sought from them. To all these earnest
devotees who have done the job in a spirit of sacrifice our
gratitude is due.

                               Nine
                    EDITORIAL NOTE

                        (Second Edition)

       History of Philosophy has recorded creative souls whose
strength lies in illuminating flashes — like the Vedic Rsis.
Similarly, William James. Time the destroyer, says T. S. Eliot,
is the Time preserver. It has also obliged us by producing
systematic thinkers like Panini or Kant who coilected manifold
data and arranged them in a magnificent super-structure. Nature
has not been chary, but has gifted to the world, giants like
Patafijali   or Aristotle   who combined        both   inspiration    and
system-building.    Professor रि .     1).   Ranade    belongs   to   this
illustrious syncretic group.
       Professor Ranade’s Birth Centenary was celebrated in July
1986. There was a plan to bring to light his unpublished writings.
As part of the celebration programme, his Notes on Indian
Philosophy were published under the title ‘Studies in Indian
Philosophy’. The Notes were published as they were. They show
Professor Ranade’s characteristic power of concentrating his
meaning in pithy sentences winged with mystical vision and
profound philosophic reflection which ‘strike the mark like a
cannon ball’. The cryptic statements, however, needed a detailed
elucidation, and hence the present attempt.
     _ An annotated edition of the entire text of            ‘Studies in
Indian Philosophy’ is contemplated. What is being published
today is Part I of the project and covers the first six chapters of
the first edition. The remaining chapters (Seven to Fourteen)
would be covered in Part II.
       Professor Ranade refers in his Notes to very many authors
— Eastern and Westem. His vast studies covered the ancient
Vedic period as well as the early Greek period and took note of
the works of his contemporaries as well. Dr. ए. V. Kane’s History
of Dharma-sastras      or   E.   छे.   Holt’s   articles   dealing    with
physiolog
       sm did not escape his attention. We have tried to locate
                                     Editoria! Note (Second Edition)


as many books as we could. Not that all books scrutinised by
Professor Ranade were available, much less the editions he had
used. We are keenly aware of these limitations.
      In the haste to pubish the notes during the Birth Centenary
celebrations, some glaring errors remained uncorrected in the
first edition. They have now been rectified.
       Sometimes the criticism in the notes appears to be a bit
too severe. After all, the notes or jottings were lecture-points for
teaching Post-graduate students of the Allahabad University. To
drive home a point, over-emphasis might have been laid on a
lacuna in 8 particular philosophic doctrine. Again, suggestions
from any quarter were welcomed, and these were added to the
Notes as marginalia for consideration. Some of them contained
sarcastic remarks. Perhaps Professor Ranade might have deleted
them in the final draft. It might also be mentioned here that the
marginalia in the original notes have been fitted in the text
without omitting any point. Occasionally, their exact place was
not indicated in the text of the Notes leaving room for conjecture.
       We are grateful to the Maharashtra State Board for
Literature and Culture for granting permission to bring out the
annotated edition. We record our heart-felt thanks to many
scholars without whose esteemed help the annotation-work
would not have been completed. Dr. (Mrs.) Shailaja Karandikar
whole-heartedly    supplied   all    information   asked   for. It   is
unfortunate that she could not see the publication of the book
as death snatched her away in 1994. Dr. P. G. Lalye, Bhandarkar
Oriental Research Institute, Pune, made available translation of
many an abstruse Sanskrit passage. Sanskrit grammar created
stumbling blocks in our way. Dr. G. B. Palsule, Bhandarkar
Oriental Research Institute, Pune, cleared many doubts in our
mind and provided detailed clarification of technical terms,
especially grammatical, in addition to running to our rescue
whenever we faced difficulties. Dr. P. D. Nawathe, Bhandarkar
Oriental   Research   Institute,    Pune,   explained   many a   terse

                               Eleven
Studies in Indian Philosophy


Mimamsa doctrine. Dr. रि . L. Joshi, Solapur, obliged us by
explaining    original    Sanskrit     passages     and _ supplying
documentation of Sanskrit quotations. Shri M. N. Deshpande,
New Delhi, sent valuable information about Asoka Edicts as also
 about some topics in Jainism. Shri V. K. Wagadarikar, Bombay,
 threw light on some scientific concepts like Atomism. We were
 always welcome to Prof. H. L. Auluck’s house, which itself is
 a veritable library. Shri G. G. Devan Dixit, Dharwad, and Dr.
 K.B. Dabade, Udupi, were ever ready to help us. Dr. S. G.
  Kulkami,   Hyderabad,     supplied    documentation      of    many
  philosophical classics. We could make profuse use of the
  Fergusson College library, Pune, and we have great pleasure in
  recording our indebtedness to Principal S. V. Bokil, Dr. S. M.
  Paralikar and Principal V. M. Bachal, who extended all heip in
  this behalf. Special mention must be made of the authorities of
  Dr. Babasaheb    Ambedkar       Marathwada      University    Library
  Aurangabad, and of D.A.V. College Library, Solapur, where we
  could see valuable texts. Our profound gratitude to them. The
  whole credit of preparing a neat and clean Press Copy goes to
  Shri S. D. Shintre, Solapur. Shri N. S. Kulkarni, Solapur and
  Shri S. K. Joshi, Solapur helped us in checking the text. The

  Solapur and to Sou. Sushama Agnihotri, Pune. The disciples of

  anonymous. We express our gratitude to Navadurga Lasertech,
  Udupi, for accurate type-setting and to Vinand off-set Printers,
  Solapur for elegant printing.
        There is no end to annotations. They can never be fully
  exhaustive. There always remains enough scope for a student to
  study fresh books and for the teacher to give a new orientation
  to a topic. Hence sufficient unto the day

                                Fourteen
                          CHAPTER I

     A CRITICAL EXAMINATION OF SANKHYA
If Introduction!
1. A Scientific World-view: Sankhya is very old, going back to
the days of the Upanisads, and tries to give a scientific?

1.   Sections I to X of this chapter cover the entire Sankhya thought
     and are mostly evaluative. Professor Ranade had also an
     alternative scheme of presenting Sankhya Philosophy under
     three heads, namely,
     (i) Cosmology (Categories and Causality)
     (ii) Psychology (Buddhi, Linga, Gunas)
     (iii) Ontology (Prakrti and Purusa)
     Perhaps the ten Sections treated in the present text would have
     been re-arranged under these three major heads in his final
     exposition. Section I (Introduction), Section II (Criticism of
     Sankhya Categories) and Section 1५ (Sankhya Doctrine of
     Causality) would have gone under Cosmology. Section V (The
     Schematic Function of Buddhi), Section VI (Doctrine of Linga-
     §arira) and Section VII (Relation of the Gunas to Prakrti) would
     have been covered by Psychology. Finally, Ontology would have
     included   Section   वा   (Evaluation   of Arguments    for   the
     Existence of Prakrti and Purusa), Section VIII (Criticism ofthe
     Nature of Prakrti), Section IX (Criticism of the Nature of
     Purusa) and Section X (The Sankhya Ideal).
     The Karikas referred to in the subsequent pages are from
     Isvarakrsna’s ‘Sankhyakarika’.
2    Science depends upon observation and experiment. Sankhya
     world-view is naturalistic and based on observation. Sankhya
     does not put forward any non-observable or non-inferable
     cause of the world like God. The Sankhya Prakrti which is the
     ultimate cause of the world, though not observable, is inferable.
Studies in Indian Philosophy


explanation of Reality; cf. Homer3 and Thales+. Evolutionism>.
Realistic6 atheistic’ spiritualism® .

     The proofs for the existence of Prakrti depend on inference
     1.९., from the actual to the potential or from effect to cause.
     Sankhya is thus scientific.

 3   Homer, the great epic poet of Greece and author of ‘Iliad’ and
     ‘Odyssey’. For him, all events are governed by natural laws; he
     does not allow any groundless beliefs or superstitions. An
     intellectual approach to nature is also traceable in him. As E.
     Zeller points out, ‘Beneath the surface of the heroic poetry and
     its myths the Logos begins to stir, soon to grow bold and raise
     its head’. (Outlines of the History of Greek Philosophy, Kegan
     Paul Trench Trubner and Co. Ltd., London, 1931, P. 10).
 4   Thales (about 7th century B.C.), a thinker from Greece, was a
     philosopher of the physical school. He sought to find within
     Nature an explanation of everything. "The Principle of all things
     is water’, he says, "all comes from water and to water allreturns".
      Eslewhere Prof. Ranade calls Thales the inaugurator of a
      philosophy of nature in Greece. (See Philosophical and Other
      Essays, Part I, Shri Gurudev Ranade Satkar Samiti, Jamkhandi,
      District Bijapur, Karnatak, 1956, P. 79).
 5    Evolution means actualisation of the potential. From Avyakta
      (non-manifest) Prakrti, the Vyakta (manifest) world evolves or
      comes out.

 6    Sankhya is realistic because according to it, the world is real
      and not illusory. Again, the existence of Prakrti or matter is not

      cognitive relation).

13     प्रधानमल्लनि बर्हण न्याय is a maxim of defeating a formidable foe. It

       conquered all the lesser ones. It refers to the game of wrestling

3. Heterodox System: Sankhya, Vaisesika and Mimamsa should
all be excluded from the ‘orthodox’ systems;14             Sankhya,
because NiriSvara; VaiSesika because no              Aptavakya       and
Miméamsa, because no necessity of God. It is curious that in the
three doublets — Sankhya-Yoga, Vaisesika-Nyaya, Mimamsa-
Vedanta, the first is heterodox and the second orthodox; probably
a movement of thought.

14    There are two Schools of Indian Philosophy — orthodox
      (Astika) and heterodox (Nastika). Traditionally the term Astika
      refers to those who believe in the authority of the Vedas.
      Nistikas are those who do not accept Vedic authority (नास्ति    को
     वेदर्नि दक:). On this count, Vaisesika is excluded from the
      orthodox systems. The terms Astika and Nastika also mean
      those who believe in the existence of God and those who deny
      the   existence of God       respectively. Hence Sankhya       and
     Mim4msa have no claim to be orthodox systems. Professor
     Ranade’s standpoint seems to combine both the meanings. So
     Sankhya,    Vaisesika   and    Mimamsa    are   characterised    as
     heterodox or Nastika.

      १.... also the five Pranas are here specified" (See analytical survey

      shall see that he has not made any mention of five Pranas. The
      five Jnanendriyas are there; the five Karmendriyas are there;
      the five Mahabhitas are there; the five Tanmatras are there;

Na-prakrti-na-vikrti; cf.   Aristotle,   Moved,   Mover, Neither,
Both;16 also cf. Yoga : Sukla, Krsna, Sukla-krsna and
Asuklakrsna.17

2. Mahat, the cosmic principle, cannot be identified with Buddhi,
the psychic principle. (a) Implicit parallelism in the equation of
Mahat and Buddhi, the cosmic and the psychic as in the attributes
of Spinoza. (b) Not even parallelism but an identitat philosophy
in the huddling up of mind and matter.18              $


16    InAristotle, form and matter are two principles. Form is always
      the mover and matter always the moved. God is pure form and
      is the mover. Pure matter is the moved (though pure matter
      does not exist), All things in the world are combinations of
      form and matter, and as such are both mover and moved.
      Corresponding to the category of ‘neither mover nor moved’,
      there is nothing in Aristotle. So in-Aristotle, the class, ‘both
      mover and moved’ is the widest because all things except pure
      form and pure matter are included therein; ‘mover’ is a
      unimember class with God as the only member; while ‘moved’
      as well as ‘neither mover nor moved’ are null-classes as they
      have no members.

 17   For explanation, see below, Chapter II Section IV 8 (१).

 18   In Spinoza’s philosophy, thought and extension are attributes

      of the Substance and are parallel. The same thought and
      extension when referred to the individual, become mind and
      body. In Sankhyan parlance, thought is cosmic (Mahat) and
      mind is individual (Buddhi). In Spinoza, thought is spiritual
      while extension is material. But in Sankhya, Mahat and Buddhi,
      being evolutes of Prakrti, are identical as they are material
      (Jada). So the Spinozistic distinction between thought and
      extension does not hold in Sankhya. The parallelism is true as
      regards the cosmic and the psychic, but not as regards thought
                                                                       Sankhya


3. Violation of the law of Satkaryavada, the vaunted Sankhya
doctrine of causality. There must not be more in the effect than
in the cause. It is wonderful how from the unintelligent Prakrti,
the   intelligent   Buddhi       could    spring.   This        means     that
non-intelligence is superior to intelligence.

4. (a) Ahankara, a superfluous category, sometimes dropped even
by the Sankhya philosophers in the concept of Linga-Sarira
(ससदशैकं लि ंगम्‌ 17 or 18); 17 + 1, महदादि सूक्ष्मपर्यन्तम्‌(Karika 40),
dropping Ahankara it is 17.19 (b) The ego, according to the
scheme, becomes a product of nature. Contrast, however, the
-Purusa.20


      and extension, for both mind and matter are covered by what
      Spinoza calls extension.
19    Sankara in his Paficikarana speaks of Liiga-Sarira as comprising
      of 17 elements : 5 Pranas + 10 Indriyas + Manas + Buddhi.
      Here Ahankara is excluded. It is surprising how there can be
      a Linga-Sarira without Ahankara. Apart from metaphysical
      quibblings, it sounds unconvincing both psychologically and
      ethically that there can be Linga-Sarira without Ahankara. In
      fact, Ahankara seems to be both necessary and sufficient to
      compel a man to take re-birth; traces of past                     actions
      (Samskaras) and unfulfilled desires lead to the cycles of birth
      and death. With the destruction of Ahankara, the very root of
      transmigration    is   destroyed.   Egolessness      is    the    ethical
      characteristic of a Jivan-mukta. For a Jivan-mukt&, there is
      AnAvrtti (no return to mundane life) mainly because he is free
      from Ahankara.
20    In Sankhya metaphysics Purusa is independent of Ahankara,
      though in ordinary parlance, Purusa is invariably associated
      with the principle of ‘self-assertion (Ahankara). Ahankara is
      an eyolute of Prakrti.

21   Biologically, Functionalism explains the process of adaptation
     of the organism to the environment. It is a basic operative
     principle of evolutionism. From the practical and_ utilitarian
     point of view, priority of functions is a more acceptable
     principle. Function is primary and for that purpose, structure
     is evolved, e.g. the function of seeing is fundamental, and for
     its realisation, the eye is evolved.
22   The Tanmitras or subtle elements are the essences of the gross
     elements and as such precede the latter in existence. They are
     not directly known, but are inferred through the gross elements
     which are first known.
23   Form is the fundamental reality, and hence, is first to. exist.
     Matter without form is mere potentiality and does not exist in
     itself. It realises form and becomes actual. And through this
     process, form is known (See E. Zeller, Outlines of the History
     of Greek Philosophy, Pp. 174 ff.).

24   In idealism there is priority of ideas or thought as against
     naturalism where structure or matter is prior. As the Tanmatra-
     Mahabhita relation is functionalistic, it is idealism and not
     naturalism (See Foot Note 21 above).

IV. Sankhya Doctrine of Causality
 1. The Sankhya philosopher speaks not merely about Parinama
but even about Sarga (Karika 21) and Prasava (Karika 11), which
is creation. The mere presence of Purusa (Purusa-sannidhi) is
the cause of creation. Parinama vs. Sarga. Transformism vs.
Creationism. Loose terminology.33 "Purusa-sannidhi" reminds

      Sankhya without a ruling God, and the "system of selves’

      it is curious to find that while McTaggart’s philosophy is non-
      theistic, Sankhya is also nirisvara (God-less)" (R.D. Ranade,
      Essays and Reflections, Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Chowpatty
      Road, Bombay—400 007, 1964, P. 10).

      teleological argument for proving the existence of God are
      famous in the history of Western Philosophy. Rene Descartes
      and St. Anselm are fore-runners of these arguments. However,
      the arguments have been ruthlessly criticised by Immanuel
      Kant. The point to be noted is that so ancient a System like
      Sankhya could put forward these arguments. This itself is
      admirable.

Evolutionism or Transformism. Satkarya-vada                   in    Sankhya

Karana with God? In Sankhya, Karana is Prakrti; in VaiSesika,
atoms; in Vedanta, God. All these schools join hands                       in

V. The Schematic Function of Buddhi
1. Nature of Buddhi: Buddhi is the unifier, the mediator, the
Janus-faced43 interpreter (Karikas 36, 37); Time in Kant.44 A
new schematism required for any pair of schematiser and
schematised.

2. Infinite regress involved in all schematism.45
3. Fictitious function. Why then, take exception to Semblance?
Through Buddhi, Prakrti becomes intelligent or sentient as it

42   See Foot Note 1 above:
43   Janus is an ancient Roman deity who presided over different
     epochs of time. He had two faces one of which looked behind
     into the past and the other before into the future. He is thus
     Trikala-dargi, 1.6. knower-: of past, present and future. This
     power was given to Janus by Saturn for being given a shelter
     when the latter was driven out of heaven.
44   According to Kant, time brings together categories and
     sensations. This mediation is possible because time is
     homogeneous with both categories and sensations. Similarly
     Buddhi brings together Purusa and Prakrti.
45   A schema brings together two things, say, A and B. But we
     must have another schema, namely, Al to bring together the
     schema itself and A, and then again a third one, namely, A2 to
     bring together A and 31 and so on ad infinitum.

‘VI. The Doctrine of Linga-Sarira
1. The Linga-Sarira, a good hypothesis, but incapable of proof
(Karikas 40, 41, 42).
          पूर्वो त्पन्नमसक्त नि यतं महदादि सृष्ष्मपर्यन्तम्‌|
          संसरति नि रुपभोगं भावैरधि वासि तं लि ंगम्‌ ।। 40 ।।

46.   Read on this head Prof. Ranade’s article: "Yajnavalkya and the
      Philosophy of Fictions" included in his book — Philosophical
      and Other Essays, Part I.
47    Professor Ranade takes the reading अवि शेषै: instead of वि शेषैः
      in Karika 41. The expression महदादि सूक्ष्मपर्यन्तम्‌ in Karika 40
      definitely states that the Linga-sarira consisis of 18 elements
      (Mahat (Buddhi), Ahankara, Manas, 5 sense-organs, 5 motor
      organs and 5 Tanmiatras). Visesa means the five gross elements.
      To say that the Linga-Sarira can not live without five gross
      elements or Sthila-Sarira, is to assert that the Linga-Sarira after
      leaving the body on death, has to take, at that very moment,
      another gross body. This contradicts the Brhadaranyaka view
      (VI. 2. 14-15) that the Linga-sarira passes on from one region
      to another. Hence the support which the Linga-Sarira has, is
      not that of Sthila-Sarira (Visesa) but that of Tanmatras
      (Avigesa). Read: Lokamanya Tilak’s गीतारहस्य, Pp. 176-179.

49   It is difficult to explain how the soul transmigrates or takes on
     another body. There must be some determining factor behind
     this process. The Vasanas (desires) and Samskaras (traces)
     contribute this determining factor. As Vasanas and Samskaras
     need some carrier, the hypothesis of Linga-Sarira is brought in.
     The Linga-Sarira
                    is a connecting link between the previous birth —
     and the subsequent birth. In the absence of the hypothesis of
     Linga-Sarira, the process of transmigration falls apart.

VII. Relation of the Gunas 10 Prakrfti. Examination ofthe
    Doctrine of Qualities

1. Is Prakrti an aggregate or a whole? Are Gunas components or
only subsidiary? Members or parts? (strands of a rope). Are they
dependent    or   independent,    adjectival   or   substantival?   If


    Points prepared in 1934-35 when he taught Contemporary
    Schools of Psychology, Prof. Ranade noted: "James Ward’s
    Theory of Psycho-plasm corresponds to Vedantic Linga-Sarira.
     (1) Proto-plasm, (ii) Bio-plasm, (iii) Neuro-plasm".
    Plasm means fluid substance of an animal or vegetable cell.
     Hence psycho-plasm would imply fluid substance comprising
    of Manas, Buddhi and Ahankara.
    These seem to be the psychological bases of Linga-Sarira. `
    According to James Ward the ultimate concept in biology (at
   + the time when he wrote) is bio-plasm, i.e., life force and not a
   : conglomeration of atoms. So too in psychology, psychical force,
     and not the manifold of sensations, is the explanatory concept.
     Psycho-plasm is the psychical force corresponding to bio-plasm
     or the biological force and is inheritable from parents. Psycho-
     plasm develops into a psychical organism. Law of heredity or
     inheriting characteristics from parents, implies a tendency to
     pass on or transmit though not actual transmission. What is
     inherited is the tendency to develop certain characteristics, or
    Anlage (See James Ward, Psychological Principles, Cam-
     bridge, at the Clarendon Press, 1933, Pp. 408 ff.).
     Psycho-plasm or Anlage is then a foundation of further
     development and is the same as Linga-Sarira. It is the carrier
     of tendencies or 808095 or Samskaras and, as Ward contends,
     though the individual is unaware of these tendencies, his life is
     determined by them. The modern theories of bio-plasm and
     psycho-plasm were. anticipated by Sankhya philosophers ages
     ago in their concept of Liiga-Sarira.      ;

60      Cardinal Humours: Blood, phlegm, लागल (yellow bile) and
        melancholy (black bile). Humour is any fluid of an animal
        considered responsible for one’s health and disposition. The
        related types of personality are: Sanguine (hopeful), Phlegmatic
        (sluggish), choleric (angry) and melancholy (depressed).
61      Tridosas: Vitiation or derangement of the three humours of the

62      This is based on लातलाट imagery. Eidetic means unusually vivid
        mental images. It refers to memory images that are percept-like
        and are ‘seen’. A child is reported to have counted buttons on
        the coat of a person whom he had seen and who was not before
        him at the moment. He was counting from the memory image,
        but that memory image was as vivid as actual perception. The
        name ‘eidetic’ has been used by Jaensch and his associates who
        related the phenomenon to personality traits (see Jaensch E.R.,
        Eidetic Imagery and Typological Methods of Investigation,
        1930). The types according to Jaensch are : Form-type, Light-
        type, Colour-type, Sound-type or Word-type.

imagery. She is also very tender सुकुमार. She is rainbow coloured.

5. Relativism. The Prakrti retires for the released soul, but
continues to exercise her function for the others. This makes
Prakrti subjective. She is, and is not. Why not Prakrtiasemblance
or an Avarana? Why not equate with Maya? Subjective and
illusory. The vaunted Sankhya realism does not hold much water.

IX. Criticism of the Nature of Purusa
 1. The Purusa not entirely Udasina (Karika 65) or Upeksaka
 (Karika 66). No doubt, he is described as looking at Prakrti
 unconcerned. He has a purpose (Purusartha), which is the motive
 to action. The Indriyas, the Gunas, the Linga and the Prakrti are
 all described as contributing to this Purusartha; cf.

                 (एते गुणवि शेषाः, namely गुणाः)

 2. The Purusa is described as Saksi, Kevala, Drasta.etc.
           तस्माच्च वि पर्या सात्‌सि द्धं साक्षि त्वमस्य पुरुषस्य |

Purusa is said to be neither Prakrti, nor Vikrti nor Prakrti-vikrti;
and Buddhi is Prakrtika. Saksitva might involve intuition and
not intelligence.

3. Moral responsibility entirely shelved. The vicarious suffering
of Purusa. Wife purchases articles and husband has to pay. The
Prakrti sows, and the Purusa reaps. Cf. The vicarious suffering
of both the Purusa and Paramatman in the Bhagavad-gita:
          कार्यकारणकर्तृत्वे हेतुः प्रकृति रुच्यते |

69   According to Bergson, intellect analyses Reality into static
     stereo-types and arranges them. In this endeavour, the living
     Reality escapes from its grasp. Likewise the Sankhya intellect
     can not be a characteristic of Purusa. Reality can not be grasped
     by mediate intellect, but by immediate experience or intuition.
     In this sense Saksitva may imply intuition. In his Lecture on
     Bergson’s Creative Evolution, Professor Ranade notes: Life =
     Change = Intuition = Duration.

X. The Sankhya Ideal
1. Poetic and not philosophic presentation. The dancing girl
retires. The Linga-Sarira breaks; the Gunas commit suicide: their
truc intent is all for the delight or satisfaction of Purusa. Aristotle
on Plato. A poetical fancy.74

75    See Vacaspati’s justification of reference to दु:ख at the very

3. Intellectualism: Isolation, an intellectual state.76 The driest

83    Prof. Ranade refers to the same point in his essay ‘Evolution
      of My Own Thought’ incorporated in ‘Contemporary Indian
      Philosophy’, (George Allen and Unwin Ltd., Museum street,
      London, Second Edition, 1952, P. 556): "When Canon Rashdall
      says that the self is realised already, he is speaking about a
      metaphysical fact. On the other hand, when it is said that the
      Self is to be realised, we are asked to take into account the
      whole ethical and mystical process by which the human being

      is gradually weaned from the allurements of the not-self, and
      the Self to be realised in its native purity and grandeur".

 84   Not-ness is expressed in two ways (i) नाहम्‌ = 1 am not or नास्मि
      = I do not exist. In these the subject is denied. (ii) नमे =
      nothing is mine, 1.6. I exist but nothing belongs to me. Here the
      predicate is denied. Buddhistic interpretation means denial of
      the subject (412). Vedantic interpretation means denial of
      the predicate (the self is devoid of all qualities).

प्राप्ते शरीरभेदे might mean also catastrophic liberation. Prototypes
of Jivan-mukti and Vidcha-mukti. ISvara-krsna goes to the length
of positing both Jivan-mukti and Videha-mukti. Running with
hare and hunting with hound.85
7. The ultimate aim, emancipation of every Purusa.
                  इत्येष प्रकृति कृतौ महदादि वि शेषभूतपर्यन्तः |

Cf. the Vedantic doctrine of Sarva-mukti. Doctrine of Universal
salvation.86

85   Jivan-mukti means liberation while living and though living.
     Videha-mukti means liberation only at death and not during
     life. Hence the two can not go hand in hand. The Sankhya
     philosopher advocating both Jivan-mukti and Videha-mukti 15
     inconsistent in his stand.
86   As liberation is destruction of ignorance of one’s own nature,
     सर्वमुक्ति      would require the destruction of everybody’s ignorance.
     Towards the end of his treatise Vedantaparibhasa, Dharma-
     rajadhvarindra discusses this point (Tr. Swami Madhavananda,
     The Ramakrishna Mission Saradapitha, Belur Math, Howrah,
      1972, Pp. 226-229). It is no use objecting that Nescience (अवि द्या)
     or ignorance is only one and its destruction
                                                in one single
     person’s case is tantamount to its total destruction resulting in
     universal salvation. Nescience may be one but its covering
      power           for   different    individuals    (आवरणभेद)        is   different,
      depending on individual प्रारब्ध. Even he who has realised
      Brahman has te live on till he finishes the enjoyment of the past
      deeds that have begun fructifying. Final मुक्ति               is deferred till
      then. As against this, it is claimed that God’s grace destroys
     प्रारब्ध and a person in that case need not enjoy it. But does God’s



                                             29
Studies in Indian Philosophy


      An Analytical Survey of Sankhya-karikas87
1. The removal of three kinds of suffering: Adhyatmika,
Adhidaivika, Adhibhautika, like the Buddhistic ideal, a negative
idea. Contrast the positive ideal of beatification.

2. Knowledge of Vyakta, Avyakta and Jia, the manifest, the



     grace lie at our doorstep? Have we not to resort to moral purity
     and one-pointed meditation? This itself is enjoying प्रारब्ध. This
     also explains why persons take longer or shorter time to reach
     the final goal. प्रारब्ध is an obstacle that does not allow complete
     destruction of ignorance. Dharmarajadhvarindra calls it
     प्रारब्धकर्मरूप प्रति बंधकदशा. God’s grace, when it pours, may be far
     in excess of our desert (who knows?); but it is bonus and not
     stipulated interest. Our spiritual practice. which takes          us
     towards grace, goes on cutting out प्रारब्ध on the way. There is
     no contradiction in insisting that we have to square it up with
      our कर्म and in hoping that God’s grace annihilates it. Both are
      the same phenomenon looked at from opposite ends. God
      evaluates man’s work and then smiles on him. Prof. Ranade
      used to give grace marks to students — but that was after going
      through the whole of the answer-book, and after assessing the
      performance. Grace marks would differ from candidate to
      candidate — +1, +2... upto +5, some not qualifying at all.
      Solely to depend on grace to wipe out प्रारब्ध, is an idler’s
      philosophy. It has neither philosophic, nor ethical justification.
      Reap as you sow is the dictum. Dharmarajadhvarindra quotes
      Vacaspati Misra to state that only after finishing one’s own
      work that has begun to yield fruit, can a man be liberated. Far

      less it is true that liberation of one individual can lead to
     liberation of all.
87   This is Professor Ranade’s analytical survey of Karikas mostly
     used in the preceding discussion.

                                                                   Sankhya


unmanifest and the knower. For Jia, cf. Vedanta-siitra ज्ञोऽत एव
(1.3.18).

3. One + seven + sixteen + one; cf. mover, moved, neither mover
nor moved, both mover and moved, in Aristotle. While the
Aristotelean God is mover, the Sankhya Purusa is na-prakrtih,
na-vikrtih.

4. Aptavakya is recognized by Sankhya.
5. This
      is the same as इण.
6. The supersensuous according to Sankhya philosophy is
established by inference. No intuition is recognized.

7. One of the causes of non-perception is subtlety.

8. We cannot perccive Nature because it is subtle. It is, however,
to be inferred from its effects such as the great principle Buddhi
and the rest.

9. The effect is in fact identical with the cause कार्य सत्‌ कारणभावात्‌।
The effect cannot be greater than the cause nor less than it (शक्तस्य
शक्यकरणात्‌). There must be compatibility between cause and
effect. Causation, in fact, is referred to the sphere of Nature: It
cannot be ascribed to Purusa.

11. Prasavadharmi is a talk about creation and not simply
evolution.

12. A fine description of the Gunas or qualities. Pleasure, pain
and gloom; illumination, activity and stupefaction; mutual
conquest;     mutual   dependence;          mutual   generation;   mutual
concert.                                ;
 13. They cooperate towards one purpose; they supplement and
do not supplant one another प्रदीपवत्‌चार्थतोवृत्ति ः, the wick, the oil
and the flame give out light; wind, bile and phlegm maintain a
constitution.

15. Arguments for the existence of Nature (a) From the limit to

                                   31
Studies in Indian Philosophy


the unlimited, (b) from a primary causal energy, (c) from effect
implying acause कारणकार्यवि भाग. Contrast Karika 9 where identity
of cause and effect is suggested. कारणवि भाग and कारणभाव are
antithetical.

 17. Proofs for the existence of spirit:
 (i)     81 aggregates imply an end. Teleology embedded in
         the nature of the composite.

 (ii)    Qualities imply the qualitiless.

 (iii)   Necessity ofa substratum for all things. This incident-
         ally does away with the necessity of postulating Nature.

 (iv)    The enjoyed implies the enjoyer (note that it is not
         doer but enjoyer).

  (v)    The will to absolution, abstraction of spirit from
         matter, is seen in all human beings.

  18. Arguments for the plurality of spirits:

  Differential incidence of births, deaths, actions, and mental
  equipment. (Types of character such as Sattvika, Rajasa and
  Tamasa). The Sankhya believes in re-incarnation (Linga-Sarira).

  19. Attributes of Purusa:

  The spirit as Saksi, Kevala, Udasina, Madhyastha, Drasta and
  Akarta. The Drasta is the same as Saksi.

  20. On account of mediation of Buddhi, the Pradhana appears
   intelligent and the Pumusa active.

  21. Combination like that of the lame and the blind for the spirit’s
  contemplation of Prakrti as well as for Prakrti’s emancipation
   कैवल्यार्थं प्रधानस्य). Sarga or creation is also referred to.

   22. Evolution from Prakrti successively of Mahat or Buddhi,
   Ahankara, and then of Manas, Pafica-karmendriyas, Pafica-
  jfianendriyas and Pafica-tanmatras (Five primary elements from
   which last proceed the five gross elements or Pafica-bhiutas).

                                      32
                                                             Sankhya


24. From Ahankara or self-consciousness proceed on the one
hand the eleven organs and on the other the five Tanmatras.

27. Mind partakes of the nature of both Karmendriyas and
‘Jhanendriays, i.e. it is both active and intellectual in its nature.

29. The three intemal organs are here specified. Buddhi,
Ahankara, and Manas, i.e. determination, self-consciousness and
imagination, also the five Pranas are here specified.

31. The spirit’s purpose is the motive to action.

33. The difference between the three internal organs Buddhi,
Ahankara and Manas and the ten external organs, viz. the
Pafica-karmendriyas and the Pajica-jnanendriyas is that while
the external organs refer only to the present, the internal organs
may refer to the past, present or future. The element of time
introduced. There must have been rain (past) because the river
has risen; there must be fire in the mountain (present) because
there is smoke; we shall have rain (future) because the ants are
carrying eggs.

35. The ten external organs are gates (Dwarani); the three internal
organs are gate-keepers (Dwarins).

36. Manas and the ten Indriyas all of which are modifications
of the   three qualities   resemble a    lamp   in action.   Having
manifested the purpose of the Purusa, they present it in its entirety
to the Buddhi. After all, the Purusa has a purpose! Cf. the
expression Purusartha in Karika 31.

37. The Buddhi ministers to the entire satisfaction (Bhoga) of
Purusa. The Purusa then enjoys!

38. From the five Tanm§atras (primary elements, Sabda, Sparsa,
Rupa, Rasa, Gandha) proceed the Pafica-mahabhttas. They are
also subject to qualities and thus become Santa, Ghora and
Mugdha (calm, turbulent and stupefying).

39. Difference between the subtle body and the physical body.
                                 33
Studies in Indian Philosophy


The physical body is derived from the parents and is perishable.
The subtle body is underived and is imperishing.
40, 41, 42. A famous description of the Linga-Sarira.

40 Its origin untraceable, its nature imperishable, consisting of
18 elements (महदादि सूक्ष्मपर्यन्तम) subject to transmigration,
containing traces of actions, in the shape of motives or
dispositions.

41 It must have a body becausé a picture cannot stand without
a background, because there can be no shade without pillars. It
 cannot be supportless.

 42 It fulfils the purpose of the Purusa by playing its part like a
 dramatic actor under the all-pervading influence of Prakrti.

 44. By merit the Linga goes upward, by demerit downward, by
 knowledge it reaches absolution [ज्ञानेन अपवर्गः), by the reverse it
 suffers bondage.

 45. Dispassion leads to the destruction of Prakrti, passion to
 transmigration.

 52. The Linga cannot exist without Bhavas or dispositions; the
 2118५25 cannot exist without the Linga. The reciprocal causation:
  among them can be compared to the seed and the sprout.

  54. Cf. The Bhagavad-gita—
            ऊर्ध्व गच्छन्ति   सत्त्वस्थाः मध्ये ति ष्ठन्ति   राजसाः |
            20101             4 अधो गच्छन्ति                 तामसाः |
 Isvara-krsna later.
 55. Pain is natural so long as the Linga is not dissolved,
 Avinivrttih; pain of old age and death is bound to occur.

 56. The ultimate aim as the emancipation of (every) Purusa
 though it appears as if the Prakrti itself is emancipated.

 57. The unintelligent milk ministers to the development of the
 calf,   similarly      the    unintelligent           Prakrti   ministers   to   the

                                             34
                                                             Sankhya


emancipation of Purusa. The cow, however, is neglected!
Personality, cf. Karika 21.

59. As a dancing girl retires, so does Prakrti retire after having
shown herself to Purusa. The subtle sexuality in ISvara-krsna.
60. Prakrti 15 Upak@rini, the Purusa is Krta-upakarin; the one is
generous, the other is ungrateful. Nature is Gunavati, Purusa is
Aguna.

61. Prakrti is most delicate or modest; having but been seen, she
vanishes forever.

62.   It is not Purusa who is bound or is emancipated or
transmigrates; it is Prakrti, who is bound or is emancipated or
transmigrates.

63. (अकृति वि कृतयः सप्त) Rainbow coloured Prakrti binds herself.
Seven coloured, she emancipates Purusa, cf. the natural love of
women for colours.

64. Characteristics of Kevala-jfiana: Neither do I exist, nor does
anything belong to me. Knowledge of ‘not-ness’, cf. Buddhism;
see commentary for negative and positive interpretations. (In its
negative aspect, the Sankhya ideal corresponds with the
Buddhistic,   in   its positive aspect,   it corresponds with     the
Vedantic). Three things: see Radhakrishnan नास्मि    , न मे, नाहम्|‌

65. The Purusa is Preksaka or Saksi or Upeksaka. He sees the
Prakrti   unconcerned;    Prakrti    whose   seven-fold   variegated
creation has ceased. But can the Purusa be entirely without
emotions? (He that hath not committed sin in his heart). The
emotionless Purusa of the Sankhya is like an abstract universal
not a concrete universal; or much rather an abstract individual
because there are many Purusas.

 as the सांख्यसूत्र. It gives a good account of Sankhya philosophy
 as found in the सांख्यसूत्रऽ. Criticism by Sankhya of all the other
 systems of philosophy in this work is important. (Cf. Vedantic

                        CHAPTER II

                              YOGA

Introductory?
1. Two Patafijalis: Patafijali, the grammarian and Patafjali,
                                                          the
Yogin.2 Keith on wrong grammar. We should read प्राति भाद्वासर्वम्‌


1    English translation of every Sutra quoted in the Text is not
     given. Readers are requested to refer to the excellent book -
     Rajayoga by Swami Vivekananda (Advaita Ashram, 5 Dehi
     Entally Road, Calcutta—14).
2    (€ date ofPatanjali, the Yogin (author of Yoga-sitras) is fixed
     at 2nd century B.C. on the hypothesis that he is the same as
     Patanjali, the grammarian. "The view, however, cannot stand
     examination. It is clear that in his philosophic views as to the
     nature of substance and quality, the grammarian stands on a
     lower plane of development than the philosopher, and on the
     other hand, the philosopher violates one at least of the
     grammarian’s laws of grammar." (A. ए. Keith, The Sankhya
     System, Heritage of India Series, Y.M.C.A. Publishing House,
     1949, P. 69). The grammatical discrepancy may be explained
     in two ways — either as an archaic form or slip of hand.
     The question whether there are two Patanjalis is considered
     from a very different angle by Dr. K.K. Kolhatkar. He has
     absolutely no doubt about the one-ness of Patanjali, the
     grammarian and Patafjali, the Yogin. He tackles the question
     of two Patanjalis by questioning a widely held assumption. He
     counters the supposition that the Vedanta-sitras have criticised
     Astanga-yoga of Patanjaliin II. 1.3, The fact is that Astanga-yoga
     is not different from Vedanta, much less hostile to it. He further
     argues that Vedantais theoretical in the sense that, it propounds
     the ideal of self-realisation; Yoga is its practical aspect as it
     provides the means to achieve the Vedantic goal of self-
Studies in Indian Philosophy


(Yoga-sitra I]. 33)        instead of      प्रति भाद्वा सर्वम्‌ | आर्षप्रयोग


     realisation. The Vedanta-sitras, according to Dr. Kolhatkar,
     can not attack the application of their own theory. Their
     criticism is directed to an older Yoga-Sastra which is theistic
     Sankhya (सेश्वर सांख्यशास्त्र). Sesvara Sankhya is dualistic and is
     entirely different from Astanga-yoga-sastra of Patanjali. How-
     ever, continues Dr. Kolhatkar, if people want to maintain that
     the name of the founder of the older school also was Patanjali,
     they may do so. In that case, we may have two ९89] 2115 — the
      one of the older Yoga and the other of Astanga-yoga. While
      the former system has almost disappeared from the philosophic
      scene, the latter is very much in vogue. The conjecture of two
      Pataiijalis is only a working hypothesis. There is no proof to
      say that the name of the founder of the older Yoga-sastra is
      2150 Patanjali (See भारतीय मानसशाख अथवा पातंजल-योग-दर्शन,
      Publishers, Shri D. 8. Dhavale, ShriSamartha Sadan, First Bhat
      Wadi, Girgaum, Bombay 4, 1951, भूमि का, Pp. 111-112).
      Professor J. H. Woods is of the opinion that Patanjali the
      grammarian and Patanjali the Yogin can not be identified as
      they hold divergent views about the nature of substance
       (Dravya). Prof. $. N. Das Gupta holds that this view is
       untenable. He argues that there is no internal evidence in पातंजल
      महाभाष्य (the celebrated commentary on Panini’s Astadhyayi),
       which would go against the identification of the two Pataiijalis.
       Moreover, the महाभाष्य agrees with the योग view as regards
       स्फोटवाद. No other school of Indian Philosophy accepts स्फोटवाद.
       (See 5. N. Das Gupta, A History of Indian Philosophy, Vol. 1,
       Cambridge University Press, Reprint 1957, Pp. 231-232). Prof.
       Das Gupta concludes, "Since there is no evidence which can
       stand in the way of identifying the grammarian Patafjali with
       the Yoga writer, I believe, we may take them as being identical."
       (Ibid. p. 238).

                                                                           Yoga


or हस्तदोष. Ahigah.3
           पातंजल-महाभाष्य चरकप्रति संस्कृतैः |
           मनोवाक्कायदोषाणाम्‌हंत्रेऽहि पतये नमः ।14


3   The word Ahisah occurs in the following benedictory verse of
    Vyasa-bhasya on the Yoga-sttras. The verse is applicable both
     to Sesa, the serpent-God and to the sage Pataiijali.
    यस्त्यक्त्वा रूपमां प्रभवति जगतोऽनेकधानुग्रहाय |
    भरक्षीणङ्केशराशि ः वि षमवि षधरोऽनेकवक्तरः सुभोगी ||
    सर्वज्ञानप्रसूति र्भुजगपरि करः प्रीतये यस्य नि त्सम्‌।
    देवोश्ी शः स वोऽव्यात्‌सि तवि मलतनुः योगदो योगयुक्तः ||
     (May Ahisa, the divine Lord of serpents (Patanjali) who appears
     on the world scene by discarding his original form and confers
     infinite benefactions on the world, who reduces to ashes heaps

     of affliction, who embodies in himself immortal nectar, who
     has many faces (who is a multifaceted author), who sports a
     beautiful hood (who enjoys ecstatic bliss of Brahman), who is
     the source >of all knowledge and whose girdle of attendant
     snakes is ever ready for his pleasure (whose attendant disciples
     are   ever willing for his service), who                possesses a white,
     immaculate body and who, himself merged in contemplation,
     bestows concentration (the science of Yoga) on humanity,
     protect you).
4    The verse means: "Obeisance to Patafjali, the Lord of serpents,
     who authored immortal sacred texts on Yoga (Yoga-sitras),
     grammar (Mahabhisya), and medicine (Caraka-samhita), and

1 Metaphysics

2. The conflict of Self and God in Yoga: which of them is the
primary reality? तदा द्रष्टुः स्वरूपेऽवस्थानम्‌(४.३. 1. 3), ईश्वर प्रणि घानाद्वा
                                                             १



 5    "शाह form of the serpent fell into the folded hands of his father,
      (Anjalau patan) and hence the name Patanjali" (R. D. Ranade,
      Pathway to God in Kannada Literature, Bharatiya Vidya
      Bhavan, Chowpatty Road, Bombay 7, 1960, p. 93).
 6    The aim of Yoga is attainment of Kaivalya which implies
      freedom or emancipation. Thus it would not be proper to
      maintain that the word Yoga has its analogue in the English
      word ‘Yoke’ which is a symbol of bondage, the very anti-thesis
      of freedom. J. H. Woods says: "Yoga... is etymologically derived
      from the stem Yuj-a (Dhatupatha IV. 68)                in the sense of
      concentration and not from the stem Yuj-a (VII. 7) in the sense
      of conjunction". (The Yoga System of Pataiijali, ए. 5). In fact
      Yoga is religious concentration while its compeer system
      Sankhya is theoretical investigation.

      The word Swarupa occuring in Y.S. 1.3 means the unmodified
      state of Purusa or one’s own original pure nature. The word
      Saripya,   in Y.S. 1.4 means identification with mental
      modifications, which leads to misery. In the one, there is
      sorrowlessness while the other is the very root-cause of all
      sorrow and suffering. सारूप्य corresponds to the Vedantic
      Adhyasa.

                                                                Yoga


(Y.S. 1.23). God from the back-door. Could be resalved only in
Vediantic fashion by identifying Self and God.8 Yoga, a half-way
house between Sankhya and Vedanta.?
3. Transcendence of God; cf. the deistic God of Aristotle.10 God

     In Y.S. 1.23 which says that the state of Samadhi can be attained
     also by surrendering oneself to God, God becomes the primary
     reality in as much as the self has to surrender to God for the
     attainment of the highest ideal. The self here becomes a
     secondary reality. In 5013 1.3 stating that at the time of one-
     pointed concentration the seer or Purusa abides in his own
     unmodified state, the self becomes the primary reality. This
     Sitra dispenses with God. The Yoga-sitras lay stress on the
     Yogic practice for the attainment of Samadhi. Sutra 1.23 states
     that one can attain to Samadhi, also (वा) by surrendering to
     God. The Avyaya (indeclinable) वा implies that devotion or
     surredering to God is relegated to a lower position while ४०९20
     practice is assigned a higher position. The conflict between self
     and God disappears when they are regarded as identical. In
     that case Sutra 1.3 would mean that in the highest state the Self
     abides in God; the same would be the upshot of Sutra 1.23 as
     well.                                             न                 र
     In Sankhya there is no God. In Vedanta, God is the only reality.
     In Yoga, God is recognised, but he is only the first among equals
     and is not assigned the highest position. Thus Yoga is a
     stepping-stone to Vedanta.
10    Deism, is a doctrine that God creates the world, but takes no
     further interest in it. Zeller summarises the view of Aristotle
     as follows: "Aristotle did not assume the action of the divine
     will on the world or any creative activity or interference of the
     deity in the course of the world". (E. Zeller, Outlines of the
     History of Greek Philosophy, Pp. 177-178). See 315670०1 Note
     17 below.

       or mistaking a piece of mother-of-pearl for a piece of silver.
        On this hypothesis the world or Prakrti becomes unreal. 5018
        Ill. 49 specifically recommends that we should discriminate
        between Prakrti (सत्त्व) and Purusa. This is realism because
        here both Purusa and Prakrti are real. So while the former Sutra
        regards Prakrti as unreal, the latter holds that it is real, and
        hence the inconsistency.

self and mind, दग्दर्शनशक्त्योरेकात्मतैव अस्मि ता (Y.S. I. 6),22 Aham

ग्रहीतू, ग्रहण and ग्राह्य.24 Ananda which 005111८5 संप्रज्ञातसमाधि


22   In Yoga there is unity of self (दक्शक्ति   or पुरुष) and mind (दर्शनशक्ति
     or बुद्धि ). This is called अस्मि    ता. The very fact that we have
     experience proves that there is such a unity, argues Vyasa-
     bhasya on ४.७. 1.6. In the Vedantic expression अहंब्रह्मास्मि        , the
     Brahm‘asmita’ is unity of self and Brahman. This necessarily
     means that the self can not be identified with the senses, mind
     or intellect, in a word, with body. But, proceeds Vyasa-bhasya,
     if there is realisation of the separateness between self and mind
     (or पुरुष and प्रकृति as Yoga would put it), Kaivalya would be the
     result and no experience whatsoever would be possible.
     Therefore Vyasa-bhasya would insist that अस्मि            ता, which is a
      pre-condition of experience, is unity of self and mind and not
      unity of self and Brahman.
      As a matter of fact, both Yoga and Vedanta agree that for the
      sake of empirical experience the identification of self and mind
      is necessary and that it has to be ultimately done away with.
      The only difference is that Yoga uses the word अस्मि           ता for the
      identification on the empirical level whereas Vedanta would
      reserve the term अस्मि     णि ः the transcendental level. Yoga itself
      comes very near such identification in the Sitra 1.41, See Foot
      Notes 24 and 87 below.
23    The   famous utterance of Mansir, the absolutistic mystic-
      philosopher of Sufism. The expression means, ¶ am the Reality’.
      This is in consonance with the Advaita Vedantic stand.

24    The Yogic unification ९ ग्रहीतृ, ग्रहण and ग्राह्य in Y.S. 1.41 comes
      very near the Vedantic idea of त्रि पुटि लय (unity of knower, known
      and knowledge) and therefore, can be contrasted with the Yogic
      conception of अस्मि   ता, namely, unity of Self and mind. See Foot
      Notes 22 above 970 87 below.

27   Berkeley gave proof for the existence of one’s own self as
     perceiver of the external world. He proved the existence of
     other selves by analogy: I have a body and also mental life;
     hence, others who have bodies must have also mental lives. The
     existence of God was proved as the ultimate Knower.

IV. Ethics
8. (a) Parallelism of the ten virtues included under Yama and
Niyama with the Ten Commandments of the Old Testament,
Exodus and Deuteronomy,37 the latter of which gives an older.


     likewise in the above instance, space and time have no
     independent existen::c apart from the knowing subject. Again,
     दुःखि तस्य नि शाः कल्प   सुखि तस्यैव क्षण: (गा. 60.22). (The same
     period of night is experienced as a small eternity by the
     miserable and as a single moment by the happy). William James
     expresses the point in his own inimitable style. Tracts of time
     "shorten in passing whenever we are so fully occupied with their
     content as not to note the actual time itself. A day full of
     excitement, with no pause, is said to pass ‘ere we know it’. On
     the contrary, a day full of waiting, of unsatisfied desire for
     change,   will   seem    a   small   eternity’   (The   Principles   of
     Psychology, Vol. 1, Dover Publications, Inc. 920 Broadway, New
     York 10, N.Y. 1950, P. 626).

37   The Bible, a collection of sacred Writings, is divided into two
     parts — the Old Testament and the New Testament. The Old
     Testament is written in Hebrew and deals, among others, with
     the religion, history, institutes and manners of the Jews. The
     New Testament is written in Greek and comprises, among
     others, the Four Gospels, the Acts of the Apostles and the
     Epistles. Exodus (i.e. going out), the book of Old Testament,
     refers to the departure ofthe people of Israel from Egypt where
     they had been slaves. It also deals with the institution of the
     moral and ceremonial laws. Deuteronomy (1.6. the second law)
     is the fifth book of Pentateuch (the first five books of the
     OldTestament). Deuteronomy is a series of addresses given by
     Moses to the people of Israel. One of the most important items
     in these addresses is Moses’ review of the Ten Commandments.
     Moses emphasizes the meaning of the First Commandment

scheme and the former a newer one, which is the one now usually

38   The classification of virtues into positive and negative can not
     be exclusive. Satya can be understood both positively (truth)
     and negatively (non-falsehood) and can be included in either
     class. Similarly, the classification into self-regarding and other-
     regarding virtues. Santosa or contentment 500115 to be an
     individual virtue. But its implication is to rule out covetousness.

 48    Dr. Thomas Brown (1778-1820) was a Scottish psychologist.
      ‘He taught in the University of Edinburgh. His lectures were
       published in a book form under the title, ‘Lectures on the
       Philosophy of the Human Mind’. He says that human mind has
       a tendency to perceive the relations between objects that are
      perceived together. This he calls the feeling of relation. Without
      this feeling of relation, our consciousness would be limited to
      a single point. He further holds that consciousness does not
      consist of independent or disparate states, but is one continuous
      stream. The implication of these two laws is that our meditation
      will not be confined to one single moment, but will have a
      tendency     to    carry   the    process   forward.   Again,   if   the
      consciousness were limited to one single moment, no habit can -
      be formed, because repetition or Abhyasa of any act will

प्रति पक्षभावनम्
             (५.‌ 5. 17. 33).      Schiller
                                          53 Aristotle’s            Wand.54


       Upanisadic description anticipates the conception of Sankhya
      ` Prakrti.
53     In the centre of J.C.F. Schiller’s (1759-1805)           aesthetics,
       ".....stand the Kantian antithesis of sensibility and reason and
       the reconciliation of the two sides of human nature brought
       about by its occupation with the beautiful". (R. Falckenburg,
       History of Modern Philosophy, P. 417). The aesthetical activity
        avoids the two extremes of reason and sensibility. Y.S. II.33
        states that to combat the upsurge of an inimical emotion coming
        in the way of observance of a virtue, one has to think of an
        opposite emotion. For example, to contain anger, one has to
        think of love. This, however, is highly difficult. The practical
        way would suggest the dilution ofthe inimical emotion. Extreme ,
        vice is undesirable; extreme virtue is unattainable; therefore,
        in practice a golden mean should be struck.
 54     Aristotle gives an example of a crooked stick (wand) when he
        suggests the way to arrive at virtuous life. If we suffer from a
        vice to which we are habitually inclined, we should try to
        practise the virtue that lies at the other extreme. This is painful,
        and the pain indicates how deeply the vice is rooted in us. Now
        we have to practise the opposite virtue with determination. The
        force of the vice and the determined effort to practise the
        opposite virtue will bring us to the mean. Aristotle says, "But
        it is necessary to drag ourselves away towards the opposite
        extreme; for by bringing ourselves far from the side of error,
        we shall arrive at the mean; as people do with crooked sticks
        to make them straight". (The Nicomachean Ethics of Aristotle,
       Tr. R.W. Browne, London, George Bell and Sons, 1889, Book
       II, Chapter IX, Section 4). Prof. Ranade has referred to the

                                                                        Yoga

जाति देशकालसमयानवच्छि   न्नाः सार्वभौमा महात्रतम्‌(४.5. II. 31); birth, place,



     concept of Aristotle’s wand in his work "A Constructive Survey

     Chowpatty Road, Bombay, 400 007, 1968, P.241.
55   Howis it that, inspite of constant flux in the world, things appear
     relatively stable? The answer of Herakleitos is that it is owing
     to the observance of the "measures", in virtue of which the
     agercgate bulk of each form of matter in the long run remains
     the same, though its substance is constantly changing. Certain
     "measures" of the “ever-living fire" are always being kindled,
     while like "measures" are always going out (Fraction 20). All
     things are "exchanged" for fire and fire for all things (Fr. 22);
     and this implies that for every thing it takes, fire will give as
     much. "The sun will not exceed his measures" (Fr. 29). (J.
     Burnet, Early Greek Philosophy, Adam and Charles Black,
     London, 4th edition, 1958, P. 150).
     "It is clear at any rate that the great contribution of Pythagoras
     to science was his discovery that the concordant intervals could
     be expressed by simple numerical ratios. In principle at least,
     that suggests an entirely new view of the relation between the
     traditional "opposites". If a perfect attunement (@’puovia) of
     the high and the low can be attained by observing these ratios,
     it is clear that other opposites may be similarly harmonised.
     The hot and the cold,     the wet and the dry,      may be united in
     ajust blend   (xp4ots) an idea to which one word "temperature"
     still bears witness. The medical doctrine of the "temperaments"
     is derived from the same source. Moreover, the famous doctrine
     of the Mean is only an application of the same idea to the
     problem of conduct. It is not too much to say that Greek
     philosophy was hence-forward to be dominated by the notion
     of the perfectly tuned string” (Ibid., P.112).

V. Occultism?®
10. The different powers :



56    Kant’s moral law is a Categorical Imperative and not a
      hypothetical imperative, because its observance is demanded
      unconditionally and of every one. "..we are under moral
      obligation, not in order to reputation here below and happiness
      above, but without all "15" and "in order to’s". Thou shalt
      unconditionally, whatever be the outcome" (R. Falckenberg,
      History of Modern Philosophy, P.384).
57    Jainism talks of two Vratas or Codes of conduct— one for the
      layman (अणुव्रत) and one for the monk (महात्रत). "Like Buddhism,
      Jainism also admits a two-fold training — that of lay life and
      that of the monk, and places the latter above the former.
      Naturally the precepts for the ascetic are more rigid and the
      vows for the layman are therefore called the ‘lesser vows’ or
      anu—vrata, to contrast them with the former known as the
      mahavrata"” (M. Hiriyanna, Outlines of Indian Philosophy,
      George Allen and Unwin Ltd. Museum Street, London, Eighth
      Impression, 1970, P. 167). We may note that Yoga uses the
      word महाव्रत to mean the great vows applicable to each and
      every one, whereas Jainism uses the word महात्रत
                                                     to mean stricter
      virtues to be observed by the monks.
 58   The word means belief in forces that lie beyond man’s
      understanding. Yoga philosophy talks of some mysterious
      powers which a Yogin attains. (For a description of these
      powers, see Foot Note 59 below).

61     The Siddhis or occult powers are obstacles to Samadhi because
       if we become entangled in them, no progress in our spiritual
       life is possible. On the other hand, in the post-ecstatic state
       (व्युत्थानदशा or reversion to mundane life), they are powers.
       However, entanglement in them during the ९८518116 state and
       their misuse in the post-ecstatic state are alike condemned.
62     It seems that the super-natural powers (see Foot Note 59 above)

       moral; ईशि त्व (over-lordliness) is mystical. The last (ईशि त्व) may
       characterise the released souls (sages) and the Lord (Self).

VI. Psychology of Mysticism
11. Dharana, Dhyana, Samadhi, stages ofone continuous process.
(a) Dharana, local — nose, heart, navel, brain or external object
(Y. $. 17.     1); Dhyana, ideological. (७) तदेव अर्थमात्रनि र्भा सं
स्वरूपशून्यमि व समाधि : (५.&..3). तदेव = ध्यानम्‌. Dhyana, nisus to
continuity of experience; Samadhi, attainment of continuity.
Dhyana is defined 88 प्रत्ययैकतानता (Y.S.U.2), but in this way it
could hardly be distinguished from Samadhi. Hence it is better
 to understand Dhy4na as effort to attain to continuity without
 reaching
        it,which is Samadhi.65 (©) Trayamekatra ‘sam’yamah



 63   Knowledge is the gradual attainment of the knowable. As

 65    Dharani, is concentration of Citta on one object; the object
      may be some part of the body (like the tip of the nose) or an
      external object (like the sun). Though any part of the body can
      serve as an object for concentration, it is specifically mentioned
      that concentration on the Sat-cakras is more beneficial as it is
      more health-giving. Unbroken or continuous concentration
      results in Dhyana. Here the concentration is on the idea, or
      quality or import of the object rather than on the object itself.
      As Dharané tends to be continuous, the object of meditation
      tends to be subtler. For example, if Dharana is on the sun,

67   It seems that the words, moral, physiological, psycho-physical
     and psychical refer to objects (3144) of meditation, ranging
     from the gross to the subtle, employed in different types of
     संप्रज्ञातसमाधि . In सवि तर्कसमाधि   the object of meditation is gross,
     1.8. the external objects enjoyed by the senses. This is the

72    Help in meditation is also sought from the two types of twilight
      consciousness — the state intermediate between wakefulness
      and sleep (hypnagogic) and the state intermediate between
      sleep and complete awakening (hypnopompic). Ideas that are
      entertained before going to sleep are soaked in the brain and
      are consolidated; and hence the necessity of meditation for a
      साधक before going to sleep. Similarly the freshness of the mind
      after waking from sleep, is another help for meditation with joy
      and    vigour;      and    hence    the   importance   of   meditation
      immediately after waking. A student can exploit this character
      of brain by concentrating on a topic of study before going to

luminosity, वि शोका वा ज्योति ष्मती (Y.S. 1.36),73          ७.९. of the sun,

13    (i)   The   ‘dichotomy’      between     संप्रज्ञात   and   असंग्रज्ञात,


     sleep and revising it, first thing in the morning. Prof. Ranade
     used to say that the last moment before sleep and the first
     moment after waking are crucial for an aspirant. If the aspirant
     remembers God at these two moments, by implication the chain
     of meditation is continuous and suffers no break. This implied
     continuity is explained by William    James: When we go to sleep,
     there is a time gap between the first moment of sleep (last
     moment of wakefulness) and the first moment of wakefulness
     (last moment of sleep). This is measured by the clock. (James
     calls it outward time.) However, for the sleeping person, the
     two ends merge smoothly over the gap and the time gap is not
     felt at all. Thus the stream of thought is continuous. The sleeping
     person does not feel the gap at all, because there is no inner
     experience of that gap. Hence in strict psychological terms,
     sleep, fainting, coma, epilepsy or other unconscious conditions
     (for example, anasthesia), are, as if, non-existent (See W. James,
     The Principles of Psychology, Vol. I, Pp. 237 ff).
73   Meditation on the super-sensuous, effulgent light where not a
     grain of sorrow enters.
74   According to Dr. K.K. Kolhatkar, Patafijala-yoga-Sastra is
     nothing but the practical aspect of Advaita Vedanta which
     advocates that one’s highest ideal is the attainment of identity
     with Brahman — आत्मब्रहौक्य. As such a Yogin is necessarily an
     Adyvaitin in practice and hence can not be an atheist (See also
     Swami Vivekananda’s Raja-yoga, Pp. 136-137).
75   See Foot Notes 68 and 69 above.       `

76     The difference between संप्रज्ञात and असं प्रज्ञात is not that of kind
      but of degrees. As concentration becomes more and more deep,
      the संप्रज्ञातसमाधि culminates in असंप्रज्ञातसमाधि . Hence the two
      can not be cut asunder. In the highest type ०1 संप्रज्ञातसमाधि 1.८.,
      in सास्मि त संप्रज्ञातसमाधि all modifications of the mind cease
      except the subtlest modification                 "I   am"     (अस्मि    ).   In the
      असंप्रज्ञातसमाधि      even this bare awareness ceases. The progress
       from संप्रज्ञातसमाधि      to असंप्रज्ञातसमाधि        is thus a continuous
       approximation to the highest state.
 77    भवप्रत्यय refers to those who have overcome bodily Ahankara
       (वि देह) and who have merged their mind in Prakrti or Nature.
       In his onward progress, from gross to subtle, the Yogin
       transcends the senses and comes to mind, transcends the mind
       and comes to intellect and then to the Mahat or the cosmic
       principle. After this stage the natural tendency is to enter the
       next higher stage, namely Prakrti. And these Yogins do exactly
       that (Prakrti-laya). Thus they remain in the domain or realm
       of Nature. They are still under the solicitations of the Gunas.
       Eventually they come back to Samsara (भवप्रत्यय). There are
       others who by practising virtues go from Mahat (0 प्रत्यगात्मन्‌
       by overcoming the natural tendency of going from महत्
                                                          10 ‌प्रकृति .
       Instead of remaining in the realm of Nature, they go to the
       realm of Ends. This transcendence of Gunas is attained by the
       intense determination of practising the virtues (उपायप्रत्यय) like
       श्रद्धा, वीर्य ७८.
