78     Both    in संप्रज्ञात and असंप्रज्ञात,   there is a continuity of
     consciousness. In संप्रज्ञातसमाधि     there is consciousness of the
     object, however subtle the object may be. In असंप्रज्ञातसमाधि      there

     On the other hand, if a difference of kind between the two
     Samadhis     is   maintained,     संप्रज्ञात would   be   qualified   by
     consciousness and असंप्रज्ञात by unconsciousness, its opposite.
     This would be catalepsy; however, in Yogic Samadhi, there is
     no loss of consciousness. See Foot Note 77 above.
79   This reference is to the Bhagavad-gité, Chapter VI, verses
     3,10,15. Mystical humility rules out the idea of having achieved
     the end once for all (आरूढ). A better way of describing the
     mystic’s attitude is that he is desirous of attaining to Yoga
     (आरुरुक्षु) and is incessantly trying to achieve (युंजान) the end.
     Prof. Ranade refers to the same idea in his book, Pathway to
     God in Hindi Literature: "Dr. R. G. Bhandarkar going a length .
     further used to say that he was not even 8 प्रपन्न, but only प्रपि त्सु,
     not one who succeeded in surrendering himself, but one who
     was Only desirous of surrender" (P. 181).

     duration, frequency and intensity of the Samadhic experience,

                         Bibliographical Note

Ram Prasad, (Tr) :       Patanjali’s    Yoga-sitras    in   "The      Sacred
Books of the Hindus Series" Allahabad 1912, with a "Text and
Translation" of the commentary of Vyasa and the translation
only of Vacaspati’s yioss. In the translation of the Sutras the
author tries to give the meaning of every word. The Introduction
by S.C. Vasu is good.

Ganga Nath Jha:         The Yoga DarSana (Tukaram Tatya, 1907)
contains no text but the translation of Sutras and the Bhasya of
Vyasa. The author also subjoins to the translation of the Bhasya,
notes on the commentaries of Vacaspati Misra, Vijnanabhiksu
and Bhoja. The Preface is good but introduces some Vedantic
arrangement.


J. न. Woods:     The Yoga System of Patanjali (Harward Oriental
Series, Vol. XVI, 1927). Read the Introduction (Pp. xiii to xxiii)
which is historical in nature concerning the date of Patafijali, the
Yoga Sitras, the Yoga Bhasya and Vacaspati Misra. Analysis
of the contents of the Sutras in serial order of the Sutras as well
as Wood’s Adhikaranas thereof might be read; but the language
is quaint. The Bibliography at the end might be referred to. Index
of Quotations in the commentaries might also be referred to.
Index of Words        in the Sutras is valuable which would enable us
to fix the meaning of the words by collation.

5. N. Das Gupta:         Yoga   Philosophy      (In   relation   to    other
systems of Indian Thought, Calcutta, 1930). Doctorate thesis.
Yoga Cosmology, Yoga Physics, Yoga Psychology, Yoga Ethics
etc. valuable.

S. N. Das Gupta:        Yoga as Philosophy and Religion (Kegan
Paul, 1924) should be read. Especially Chapters VII from Book
I   and   Book   II    generally. Das       Gupta relies too much on
commentaries and does not exercise independent interpretation
                                       69
Studies in Indian Philosophy


or philosophic judgement.
5. N. Das Gupta: Study         of Patanjali   (Calcutta    University)
might be read.

Rajendra Lall Mitra: Yoga Aphorisms           of Patanjali    (Asiatic
Society of Bengal, Bibliothica Indica). Text and Translation both
of Y oga-sutras and Bhoja’s "Raja Martanda". Mitra’s reflections
are subjoined in square brackets to the translation of the
commentary. The Index is valuable. But besides reference to
Sanskrit words in the text and the commentary, it contains
references to English words also in his own reflections and no
reference to words in the Preface. The Preface is valuable
philosophically and contains good English but it is somewhat
long.

R. D. Ranade:    “Analysis” of the Sutras seriatim and reflections
thereon   according   to   subject-matter     such   as   Metaphysics,
Epistemology, Ethics, Occultism and Mysticism.

    A CRITICAL EXAMINATION OF BUDDHISM

1. The Problem of the Historical Buddha

(a) The doctrines of the Buddha recently separated from the
doctrines of Canonical Buddhism. Mrs. Rhys Davids (1934)
entirely changes her old opinions as expressed in her book
Buddhism, Home University Library, London, 1912.1 peccavi.2


1    About her first book Mrs. Rhys Davids says, "But the ‘poor
     thing’ has now out-lived its usefulness....the historical study of
     Buddhism is a very new science... J had not discerned that this
     ‘tradition’, as shown in the Pali Canon is but a ‘tradition of the
     elders’, not necessarily the ‘New Word’ brought fresh into
     Indian religion by the founders of ‘Buddhism’... I did not know
    enough to see that the New Word had survived only in a
    fragment here, a fragment there, built over by the later super-
    structure of the ‘teaching of the elders’, which was largely a
     matter of formula and ritual of the mind" (Buddhism, Home
    University Library, London, 1934, Preface Pp. 7-8). By ‘New
    Word’ Mrs. Rhys Davids means the original teaching of the
    Buddha. By the ‘teaching of the elders’, or ‘tradition’, she means
    the doctrine taught by later Buddhist monks, which is known
    as Canonical Buddhism or Pali Canon. Mrs. Rhys Davids
    further observes that her studies of the Pali Canon and
    Commentaries convinced her that the teaching
                                               in the Pali Book¥.
    regarding man’s nature, life and destiny is not the original
    Buddhism (Ibid. ए. 41). She often confesses that she did not
    know the Pitakas well at that time (Ibid. P. 127), or that she
    groundlessly omitted certain things (Ibid. P. 132).
2   Peccavi (Latin word) means, I have sinned. It is a confession
    of sin or guilt. Mrs. Rhys Davids felt guilty about her earlier

Studies in Indian Philosophy


theory, must be regarded as mercly the mouth-picce of the
Canonists. What are the reasons for this disparity between the
teachings of the Buddha and the teachings              of Canonical
Buddhism?    They    are   probably    these:   (i)   The   Canonists
misunderstood the teachings of the Buddha (lapse of (11700
centuries, want of writing, necessity of system, dogma and so
forth). (ii) The Canonists misrepresented the doctrines of the
Buddha in the interest of theirmonkdom. "Monkish hostility".7
The negative side of the Buddha’s teaching thus emphasised by
the monks at the cost of the positive. They retained Buddha’s
Ethics, dropped his Mysticism and stressed the negative® side
of his Metaphysics (viz., Change and Causality).
II. The Teachings ofthe Historical Buddha
Mrs. Rhys Davids cites Fragments to prove that the Buddha
believed in the Self, the reality of the Moral Law, and so on; cf.
expressions like Atmadipa.? ‘Live as they who have the Self for



 7    175. Rhys Davids elsewhere calls it ‘the mionastic distortion’

      (Outlines of Buddhism, Oriental Books Corporation, New
      Delhi 55, First Indian Edition, 1978, P. 105). About Canonical

VIII) : ‘Western Thought is bankrupt’.!7           The whole is very
valuable as being a positive interpretation of the doctrine of
Buddhism. Cf. also Radhakrishnan. E. Holmes probably supplied
an inspiration to Radhakrishnan and Mrs. Rhys Davids.18


       teachers, went back from what was corrupt and degenerate in

17     "The higher thought of the West is bankrupt, in the sense that
       it can no longer meet its obligations” (Ibid. P. 232). Mr. E.
       Holmes describes this bankruptcy in a language through which
       only a student of economics or commerce can wade. The sum
       and substance of his detailed argument is that the function of
       higher thought is to provide philosophical theories to satisfy
       the soul’s spiritual desires. But sometimes the ideas which
       thought provides, become stereotyped and dogmatic. Fresh
       ideas cease to flow and thought with its capital of pale and stale
       material proves incapable of meeting the new challenges of
       widening spiritual desires of the soul. The demands of the
       developing soul grow higher and higher but the spiritual
       philosophy supplied by stagnant thought can not rise to reach
       the mark. It remains totally inadequate. According to Holmes
        western thought was reduced to such bankruptcy. It did not
        take into account new evidence regarding Buddhism and
        continued to play with the rotten idea that Buddhism taught a.
        negative philosophy.

Buddha taught a doctrine of ‘two truths’, the exoteric and the
esoteric,19 one for the layman and the other for the
philosopher(?)20 This is probably an anachronism going later
to the times of Nagarjuna.2!_       Buddha had no philosophical



     he, nor those who with less excuse than he, have studied the
     Pali tradition at first hand, have come to see, that in the roots
     of "Buddhism" we are up against something bigger than "mere
     change". (Mrs. Rhys Davids, Buddhism, 1934 Pp. 106-107).
19   The ultimate Truth can not be knownin        its entirity by reason
     and much less by senses. Ultimate knowledge is really intuitive

     experience and is termed esoteric truth. But most men, for most
     of the time, and for most of the purposes, have to rely on
     knowledge that falls short of the esoteric truth, but is useful for
     practical life. This is termed exoteric truth. The exoteric truth
     is not unreal; it is less real. It will have more and more reality
     as it more and more approaches the esoteric truth. Thus
     exoteric knowledge has degrees. The doctrine of two truths
     means that the highest truth is one and perfect, but the lower
     truth has infinite grades (See also Foot Notes 131 — 137 below).
20   Japanese scholars maintain that the Buddha instructed his
     pupils according to their capacities. Vyakta Upadeéa 1.6.
     exoteric teaching or common-place knowledge was meant for
     the layman while Guhya Upadeéa 1.९. esoteric teaching or
     mystical instruction was reserved for more advanced pupils. It
     is difficult to guess why the question mark is put here. Perhaps
     it may imply that the esoteric truth is beyond the reach of even
     the philosopher. It can be realised by a mystic in his intuitive
     experience.
21   Anachronism comes in when an earlier doctrine is interpreted
     or criticised in terms of a later one. It is like criticising Newton
     from the standpoint of Einstein. An earlier thinker is innocent
     of later developments and cannot be judged from the standpoint

28    This is a description of continuous and gradual progress. Mrs.
      Rhys Davids quotes another saying, "wherefore train yourselves
      in this way, striving from further to further, from good to good,
      let us realize the liberation beyond which naught remains"
      (Outlines of Buddhism, 1978, P. 25).
      The higher one climbs, the wider the range of one’s vision. If
      one reaches the top of a mountain one can have a panoramic
      view of all things which is denied to a man who remains ona -
      lower level. This is also true of moral and spiritual life. The
      more perfect you are, the nearer you are to the goal and the
      farther from lower cupiditics. The more you have attained to
      spiritual progress, the nearer you are to God and the more
      removed from mundane attachments.

30    Taittiriya Upanisad, 1.10. This is the post-ecstatic monologue

31     The meaning is: ‘you can realise God if you stand on the high

 32    Tele = at, over, from or to a distance. Volition = the act of
       using the will. Televolition means using the will to produce
       effects at a distance. The injunction was that a monk should
       sulfuse all quarters with the virtues like amity (मैत्री), pity (करुणा),
       gladness (मुदि ता) or poise (शम). It is necessary that he should
       himself have that virtue in abundance before he can will it to
       others. It is spreading of virtue by thinking well of others.
       Obviously the monk must have vast resourcesof that virtue and
       a heartfelt desire to benefit others.
 33    The word telepathy was coined in 1882 by-F. W. Meyers. It
        means supposed communication between minds by some means
        other than the normal sensory channels. It is transference of
        thought. Mrs. Rhys Davids observes, "The point is, that a man
        abounding in benevolence, should, by what we call telepathy,
        but what ought to be called televolition, ‘will’ another, whom
        he sees abounding in the opposite sentiment or stinting amity,
        to be filled with his own amity. And the same Will-exercise
        should be practised with the sentiment of ‘pity’ of ‘benevolent
        joy and of ‘poise’ or moral stability’ (Buddhism, 1934, Pp.
        142-143),




                                          84
                                                                    Buddhism


(i) Sanghata and Santina, contradictory conceptions.34 Sanghata
and Santana, cardinal points also in Sarvastivada or Vaibhasika



34   Sanghata means an aggregate or putting together. The Self is
     nothing but a body-mind complex — an aggregate of thoughts,
     sensations and body. Self is not an entity to which the states of
     consciousness       belong.    The     states    of   consciousness       are
     themselves the self. Add an element of time to Sanghata and
     you   get    Santina.    Santana      means     incessant   change.       The
     aggregate is always in a flux. Now, a conglomeration of thoughts
     and sensations (Sanghata) without any substance to hold them,
     is inconceivable in the first instance. Secondly, change in time
     cannot be perceived, unless there is something to change. Flux
     (Santana)      is   inconceivable      without    a   substance.    Hence
     Sanghata and Santana taken individually are inconceivable.
     Taken       together,   they   are    contradictory.    Change     will    be
     consistent with something that changes and not with a transient
     bundle of sensations.
     "The conception ofa dharmais the central point of the Buddhist
     doctrine. In the light of this conception Buddhism discloses
     itself as a metaphysical theory developed out of one
     fundamental principle, viz. the idea that existence is an interplay
     of plurality of subtle, ultimate, not further analysable elements
     of Matter, Mind and Forces. Fhese elements are technically
     called dharmas, a meaning which this word has in this system
     alone. Buddhism, accordingly, can be characterised as a system
     of ‘Radical Pluralism’ (sanghata-vada): the elements alone are
     realities, every combination of them is a mere name covering
     a plurality of separate elements" (T. Stcherbatsky, The Central
     Conception of Buddhism, Indian Edition, Motilal Banaras
                                                                  i-
     dass, 1979, P. 73).
     "Buddhist philosophy is an analysis of separate elemen
                                                             ts, or
     forces, which unite in the production of one stream (santan
                                                                 a)



                                      85
Studies in Indian Philosophy


school, that of materialistic pluralism. See Sankara’s criticism
below.35 Incessant change (cf. P. Carus, Gospel of Buddha,
Identity and non-identity, Pp. 131, 140)36 without a permanent


      of events” (Ibid. P. 9).
      Buddhism never denied the existence of a personality, or a soul,
      in the empirical sense, it only maintained that it was no ultimate
      reality (not a dharma). The Buddhist term for an individual, a
      term which is intended to suggest the difference between the
      Buddhist view and other theories, is Santana, 1.8. a ‘stream’,
      viz. of inter-connected facts. It includes the mental elements
      and the physical ones as well, the elements of one’s own body
      and the external objects, as far as they constitute the experience
      of a given personality. The representations of 18 classes (dhatu)
      of elements combine together to produce this inter-connected
      stream. There
                  is a special force, called prapti, which holds these
      elements combined. It operates only within the limits of a single
       stream and not beyond. This stream of elements keeps together,
       and is not limited to present life. Having its roots in past
       existences and its continuation in future ones, it is the Buddhist
       counterpart of the Soul or the Self of other systems (Ibid. Pp.
       25-26). (See also Foot Notes 165, 166 below).
 35    See Section VI below: Badarayana’s Criticism of Buddhism.
 36    Buddha gives two illustrations to explain change. The flame
       which burns in the first watch of the night is not the flame that

       burns in the second or third watch of the night. It is different
       at every moment. Similarly what is called self is a constant
       change. The babe, the boy, the youth and the man are different.
       However, there is identity in a certain sense only. This identity
       consists in the continuation of Samskaras, which are produced
       by man’s past deeds. The man is identical only in the sense that
       he himself reaps the fruit of what he sows, but he is different
       because, besides the combination of Samskaras there is no self

37   According to Kant, in all change of appearances, substance is

      "Seeing thus, Rahula, the instructed disciple of the ariyans
      turned away from the eye;.... from the ear...., In turning away
      he is dispassionate; by dispassion he is freed: in freedom is the
      knowledge that he is freed, and he comprehends : Destroyed
      is birth, brought to a close the Brahma-faring, done is what was
      to be done, there is no more of being such or so".
      "Thus spoke the Lord. The venerable Rahula rejoiced in what
      the Lord had said. While this exposition was being given, the
      venerable Rahula’s mind was freed from the cankers without
      grasping. And to those various thousands of devas there 87086
      the dustless, stainless vision of dhamma that, ‘whatever is liable
      to uprising all that is liable to stopping’ (I. B. Horner, The

Studies in Indian Philosophy


parts; in Upanisads and Plato, the chariot a vehicle drawn by the

      Just as a chariot is a symbol for the assemblage of physical
      complexes like the pole, wheel, axle, seat, roof and so on, so
      too the self is but a symbol for the aggregate of psychial
      complexes like form (Ripa), idea (५117278), feeling (Vedana),
      sensation (Sanjna) or impression (Samskara). The concept of
      chariot or for that matter, the concept ofself is only an aggregate
      without any underlying unity. In the Upanisads as well as in
      Plato, however, the chariot is a single entity or an organic unity.
      So is the body a ‘harmonious embodiment of a sing}e purpose’.
      The members of the body are organically interrelated. The
      body-chariot described both by the Upanisads and by Plato is
      one unit, complete with the horses and the charioteer.
 46    The parable of the chariot in the Kathopanisad is as follows:
       "The body is a chariot.... of which the Soul is the Lord, and
       intellect the charioteer; the mind constitutes the reins, while
       the scnses are the steeds which roam on the pasture-lands of
       worldly objects. A person whose mind is not composed, is taken

       to evil paths by the unruly and wicked steeds, while he whose
       mind is composed is taken to good paths by the noble steeds"
       (S. K. Belvalkar and R. D. Ranade, The Creative Period,
       Bilvakunja Publishing House, at Bilvakunja, 102 A, Bhamburda,
       Poona, 1927, P. 263). Similar is the Phacdrus myth: Our soul
       is a charioteer (reason) riding in a chariot drawn by two winged
       horses, one ofa mortal (appetite), and the other ofan immortal

‘Charioteer’ in Buddhism. Hume: entire disparity of ideas;
billiard balls.47
Buddhism: projection of energy or efficacy.48 James: an ever—
enveloping unity. The Buddhist view, in between the views of
Hume and James.49 The Canonical doctrine of the annihilation


     win victory in the struggle.
     According to Hume, even if one billiard ball hits another and
     the other moves, we can not conclude that the motion in the
     second ball is caused by hitting; because, the two balls are
     entirely disparate and there can not be any.causal connection
     between them. There is time sequence but no causal sequence.
48   This implies transmission of Samskaras or traces of good and
     bad thoughts. Stcherbatsky renders the word energy by
     Samskara (The Central Conception of Buddhism, P. 23). In
     Buddhism elements are really discrete or separate. "A force, it
     must be recalled, should not be regarded as a real influence of
     something extending beyond its own existence in order to
     penetrate into another— this would be upakara — but simply as
     a condition, a fact, upon which another fact arises or becomes
     prominent (utkarsa) by itself— this is Samskara in the Buddhist
     system" (Ibid., Pp. 21-22).
49    According to James, thoughts are not separate or independent;
      on the other hand, the previous thought completely envelopes
      the next one for they both belong to the same self. The thoughts
      which come later inherit all that the previous one had (See
      William James, The Principles of Psychology, Vol. I, Pp.
      339-340). According to Hume the self is a bundle of discrete
      sensations. Buddhism agrees with Hume in saying that thoughts
      are completely disparate having no relation to each other. It
      agrees with James in maintaining that when the first thought
      dies, it bequeaths all that it had, to the succeeding. Buddhism,
      while agreeing with Hume that there is discontinuity, agrees

57    Without an abiding self it is difficult to uphold the categorical
      nature of the moral law that he who sows must reap. On
      Buddhistic metaphysics he who sows disappears the next
      moment and does not remain to reap the consequences of his
      action. This is the implication of their contention that a new
      self is born every moment. Therefore, the real doer escapes the
      responsibility either of his good actions or bad actions. Again,
      it is only on the metaphysical basis of the permanence of the
      Self, that Ethics becomes possible. One of the postulates of
      Ethics is immortality of the soul.
 58    The chain of links is not self-sustaining, because it breaks at
      every link. And therefore to own such states as अवि द्या, वृष्णा etc.
      there must be a permanent self (See Foot Note 59 below).
 59   The argument of Buddhism is that the concatenation of 12 links
      (नि दान) namely, ignorance (अवि द्या), impression or memory
      (संस्कार), consciousness (वि ज्ञान), name and form (नामरूप), five
      senses and mind (षडायतन), contact
                                     or perception (स्पर्श), fecling
      (वेदना), desire (तृष्णा), holding to existence (उपादान), becoming
      (भव), birth (जाति ), old age and death (जरामरण) is sufficient to

namely, “Avidya", has no ‘Vedantic’ significance; Jacobi says

Causation as based on a cosmogonic myth and says it only
implies the creation and’ destruction of the world; Franke says,
on the other hand, that the chain has only an ideal significance.
Burnouf tells us that the Chain implics the emergence of
concrete entity from non-existence; while Kirste says that it tries
to   bring   about    a   relation   between     independent temporary
existences, which are not changes as in an organism. Oltramare
similarly tells us that the Chain does not try to explain the origin
of life, but only points out to the emergence of misery; while
Mrs. Rhys Davids says that the Chain              may imply progress to

miscry.60    [{ need not be a pessimistic doctrine but may be-a

their doctrine       of Santana      to    explain causation;   otherwise
causation to them         would      have meant no more than        mere
continuity. Das Gupta tells us that the Pratitya-samutpada means
dependent emergence which is only another name for collocative
production, assemblant origination or contingent necessity;61
while to us it seems to imply the doctrine of ‘asamutpada’ or
non-creation as experienced in ‘Pratiti’ or intuition (Pratiti +
asamutpada). The Asamutpada doctrine of the Buddhists thus

69   "A regular part of the daily work of the members of the Sangha
     - whether Brethren or Sisters — consisted in the practice of
     certain contemplations. These stations of meditation differ only
     in minor details from those which are regularly practised by
     Indian     ascetics     of   other      orders.    With     characteristic
     systematization, these modes of training heart and mind are
     often spoken of as forty-four in number. How essentially self-
     educationa! is the purpose of these stations of meditation
     appears from the fact that certain ones are appointed for
     persons of one temperament, and certain others for those of
     other temperaments. I have spoken of these                    meditations
     deliberately as ‘work’, because it is important to urderstand
     that we do not speak here of any simple matter such as day-
     dream or reverie, but of a severe system of mental training,
      founded on an elaborate psychology, and well calculated — now
     by auto-suggestion, now by close attention — to produce the
      type of character aimed at....         The first meditations are of an
      ethical character, and in some respects may be compared to
      prayer. They consist in cherishing the moods (bhavanas) of
      loving-kindness, compassion, sympathy and impartiality (meita,
      karuna, mudita and upekkha). These are calied the Four
      Hlimitable Sublime Moods (Brahmavihiras)....

      another (asubha-bhavana), on "Foul things." This very different
      contemplation is appointed for those whose emotional nature

73   "The four Dhyanas spoken of in the Lankavatara seem to be
     different from those which have been described in connection
     with the Theravada Buddhism. These dhyanas are called (1)
      balopacarika, (2) arthapravicaya, (3) tathatalambana and (4)
      tathagata. The first one is said to be that practised by the
      Sravakas and the pratyeka buddha. It consists in concentrating
      upon the doctrine that there is no soul (pudgalanairatmya), and
      that every thing is transitory, miserable and impure. When
      considering all things in this way from beginning to end the
      sage   advances    on    till   all   conceptual   knowing   ceases
      (asamjnanirodhat); we have what is called the Valopacarika
      dhyana (the meditation for beginners).
      "The second is the advanced state where not only there is full
      consciousness that there is no self, but there is also the
      comprehension that neither these nor the doctrines of other
      heretics may be said to exist, and that there is none of the
      dharmas that appears. This is called the arthapravicaya dhyana,
      for the sage concentrates here on the subject thoroughly secking
      out (pravicaya) the nature of all things (artha).
      "The third dhyana, that in which the mind realises that the
      thought that there is no self nor that there are the appearances,
      is itself the result of imagination and thus lapses into thatness
      (tathata). This dhyana is called tathatélambana, because it has

1९. Buddhistic Ethics
The sublimity of empirical or descriptive ethics/4 in Buddhism.
Its strongest point, the message of peace and contentment.
Sanctity of Life. India, the true representative of Buddhism, says


     for its object the tathata or thatness. G97) PARES 6)
     "The last or the fourth dhyana in which the lapse of the mind
     into the state of thatness is such that the nothingness and
     incomprehensibility of all phenomena is perfectly realised" (S.
     N. Das Gupta, History of Indian Philosophy Vol. I, 1932, P.
     150).
     "According to the Lankavatara the attainment of the highest
     knowledge may be accomplished by Bodhisattvas through the
     realisation that things are nothing but mental creation... To aid
     in the attainment of this end a special series of meditations is
     prescribed.... In the third meditation it is realised that the
     thought that there is no self and no appearance is itself an

     outcome of delusion, and the mind then falls back on the
     coneption     of   suchness    (Tathatalambana)"       (A.   B.   Keith,

74   Empirical ethics deals with the actual ‘here’ and not with the
     hypothetical ‘hereafter’. Ultimate good of the soul has no place
     in this ethics, Buddha was mainly concerned with man’s life on

     the earth and taught that one should try for peace to oneself
     and peace to others.

75   "Introductory episode : A well-born girl of Savatthi, recognizing
     the misery of the worldly life, had adopted the homeless state,
        and was one day seated with others of the Sisters, hearing the
        Master’s teaching; and the thought came into heart, ‘Was 1 in
        some    former       life   an   attendant     of his wives?’ Then    she
        remembered that in the time of the elephant Chaddanta, she
        herself had been his wife, and her heart was filled with joy. But
        ‘Was I well or ill-disposed to him?’ she thought, ‘for the greater
        part of women           are ill-disposed to their       1005. Then    she
        remembered that she had borne a grudge against Chaddanta,
        and had sent a hunter with a poisoned arrow to take his tusks.
        Then her grief awoke, and her heart burned, and she burst into
        sobs and wept aloud. On seeing that the Master smiled, and
        being asked by the company of the Brethren, ‘What, Sir, was
        the cause of your smiling’, he said, ‘Brethren, this young sister
        wept for an injury she did me long ago’. And so saying he told
        a story of the past.
        "Once on a time the Bodhisatta was born as the son of the chief
        of a herd of elephants in the Himalayas. He was pure white,
        with red feet and face; when he grew up he became the chief
        of a great herd, and he worshipped private Buddhas. His two
        chief queens were Cullasubhadda and Mahasubhadda. Onc
        year it was reported, ‘The great sal-grove is in flower’; and with
        all his herd he went to take hts pleasure there. As he went along
        he     struck   a     sal   tree    with   his   forehead,   and   because



                                             102
                                                       Buddhism




Cullasubhadda was standing to windward, twigs and dry leaves
and red ants fell on her, while Mahasubhadda stood to leeward,
so that flowers and pollen and green leaves fell on her.
Cullasubhadda thought, ‘He let the flowers and pollen fall on
his favourite wife, and the twigs and red ants on me,’ and she
bore him a grudge. Upon another occasion, when a lotus with
seven shoots had been offered to him, he presented it to
Mahasubhadda.
"Then Cullasubhadda was still more estranged, and she went
to a shrine of private Buddhas and made offering of wild fruits,
and prayed : ‘Hereafter, when I pass away, 1 would be reborn
as the daughter of a king, that I may become the queen of the
King of Banaras. Then shall 1 be dear to him, and may work
my will, and J will have him to send a hunter with a poisoned
arrow to kill this elephant and bring me his sixfold tusks.’ And
in time to come she becomes the chief queen of the King of
Banaras. She remembers her former life and thinks : ‘My prayer
has been fulfilled.’ She feigns sickness, and persuades the king `
to grant her a boon, which alone will restore her health and
spirits; what the boonis she will tell when all the king’s huntsmen
are assembled. It is that some one of them should bring her the
tusks of Chaddanta....
"Of all the huntsmen, one by name Sonuttara, who was a hideous
lout and big and strong, undertook the task, and being furnished
with all needful implements, he set forth on his way. It needed
seven years of weary going to reach Chaddanta’s haunts; but
no sooner come there, than Sonuttara dug a pit and covered it
with logs and grass, and donning the yellow robes of a man of
 religion, and taking his bow and poisoned arrow, he hid himself
 and lay in wait. Presently Chaddanta passed by, and Sonuttara
wounded him with the poisoned arrow. But the elephant,
 subduing his feclings of resentment, asked the hunter, ‘Why

      have you wounded me? Is it for your own ends or to satisfy the

      tusks. Chaddanta reflected, ‘It is not that she wishes for the

      Come now, thou hunter, and before I die
      Saw through my ivory tusks;
      And bid the jealous queen rejoice —
      ‘Here are the tusks, the elephant is dead’.

      "So Chaddanta bowed his head, and Sonuttara began to saw
      the tusks; and when he could not cut them, the great elephant
      took the saw in his trunk and moved it to and fro till the tusks
      were severed. Then he gave up the tusks and said, ‘I do not
      give you these tusks, my friend, because I think them of litile
      value, nor to win the status of a god, but because the tusks of
      omniscience are a thousand times dearer to me than these; and
      may this worthy gift be the cause of my attaining Omniscience.’
      Then the hunter departed with the tusks; and before the other
      elephants reached Chaddanta he had died.
      "The hunter came then before the queen and said:
      Here are his tusks, the beast is dead.
      ‘Do you tell me that he is dead?’ she cried; and he answered,
      ‘Rest assured that he is dead, here are the tusks.’ Then she
      received the six-rayed tusks, and laying them across her lap,
      and thinking, ‘These are the tusks of him who was once my
      lord,’ she was filled with sorrow so great that she could not
      bear it, but there and then her heart broke and she died the
      same day.

76   Yachibana further writes, "Words such as Yanna (४०७)...
     Yaga.... Havya.... are used in Buddhist texts; but they always
     have particular meanings, they simply mean ‘offering’, ‘alms’,
     and ‘almsgiving’, so far as Buddhist custom is concerned,
     whether they refer to something offered or the action of
     offering. It is only when these words refer to Brahmanic
     practices that they mean sacrifice or oblation. The destruction
     of life, whatever the living object may be, is absolutely
     prohibited as an un-Buddhistic action, and on the other hand,
     love, sympathy, liberality, humanity and so on are taught as
     highly commendable virtues; how can such bloody sacrifices be
     consistent with this?" (S. Tachibana, The Ethics of Buddhism,
     first edition, Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1926, London, Curzon
     Press Ltd. and Harper and Pow Publishers, Inc. New York,
     1975, P. 41). It may be observed that Tachibana’s views are
     clearly prejudiced. For him the words ४ ४09, Yaga, Havya mean
     offering or alms when referred to Buddhism, and the same
     words mean killing when referred to Brahmanism! He forgets
     that these words preeminently mean offerings in Brahmanism.
     Right from the Upanisadic times they have a symbolic signi-
     ficance — as when the Atman is regarded as the sacrificer and
     passions like anger, the objects to be sacrificed, (तस्यैवं वि दुषो
     FAG आत्मा यजमानः:...    मन्युः पशुः.   . Narayanopanisad, 80). The

     Bhagavad-gita clearly speaks of the inner sacrifice (1५, 26-27).

778   "Of more enduring interest is the Sutta upon the Fruits of the
      Life ofa Wanderer. Here, morcover, we do not get a purely
      Buddhist, but rather an Indian point of view. The whole Sutta
      constitutes a reply to the question, what advantage is the life
      of a recluse? King Ajatasattu of Magadha points out the gain

      know what corresponding fruit, visible here and now, the

      fruit of the life of the member of an Order may be seen in: "1.
      The honour and respect shown to such men by others in the

      who had formerly been a slave or a servant, if he adopted the
      homeless life. 2. The training in mere morality, as kindness,

80     Devadatta is "brother of Yasodhara and Buddha’s brother-in-

 81     ‘Nataputta means ‘son of Jnata, Patronym of Vardhamana, the
        founder of Jainism’ (Ibid. P. 226).
 82     For   an elucidation   of these    virtues   in   Yoga,      see Swami
        Vivekananda, Rajayoga, Pp. 148-149.
 83     The four noble truths, according to Buddhism are: (i) There
        is suffering, (ii) It has a cause, (iii) It can be removed, (iv) way
        to removal. They can be reduced to one viz., suffering. If there
        is suffering there must be a cause. So the first truth includes
        the second. Now whatever is caused is subject to destruction.
        Hence suffering can be destroyed. So the third truth need not
        be independent. Now if suffering is to be destroyed there must
        be a way. This fourth truth is covered by the third. So suffering,
        considered in all its aspects, can be said to include the other
        three.

88    "Significance of the concept of Madhyama Pratipad — The



                                     109
Studies in Indian Philosophy




     Buddha used to say that Truth did not lie in the extreme
     alternatives but in a middle position (Madhyama Pratipad).
     Hinayanists gencrally use the concept of Madhyama Pratipad
     in the ethical sense, in the sense of neither taking too much
     food nor too little, neither sleeping too much, nor too little etc.
     "The    Madhyamikas interpreted Madhyama pratipad in a
     metaphysical sense also. Says Nagarjuna:
     कात्यायनाववादे चास्तीति नास्तीति चोभयं |
     प्रति षि द्धं भगवता भावाभाववि भावि ना || (मूलमध्यमकारि का XV. 7)
     "In the Katydyanavavada-sitra the Lord who had the right
     insight into both bhava (ens) and abhava (non-ens) rejected
     both the extreme alternatives of ‘is’ and ‘is not’.
     "In commenting on this, Candrakirti has quoted the relevant
     passage in the Katydyanavavada-sitra which is accepted as an
     authority by all the Buddhists. In this, Buddha says to Kasyapa,
     "Oh Kasyapa, ‘is’ is one extreme alternative, ‘not is’ is another
      extreme alternative. That which is the madhyama position is
      intangible, incomparable, without any position, non-appearing,
      incomprehensible. That       is what      is   meant by madhyama-
      pratipad (the middle position), Oh Kasyapa. It is perception of
      Reality,      (bhdta-pratyaveksi)    (Prasannapadaé,      P.      118).
      Nagarjuna takes his stand on this authoritative statement of the
      Buddha. The word madhyama is not to be taken in its literal
      sense of ‘in between’ or a ‘mean between the two’. As is clear
      from    the   adjectives ‘intangible,     incomparable,   incompre-
      hensible’ ctc., madhyama pratipada (the middle position)
      means that Reality is transcendent to the antinomics of Reason,
      the dichotomies of thought, and can not be ‘cavined, caged,
      and confined in the alternatives of ‘is’ and ‘is not’. It is on this
      basis that Nagarjuna called his philosophy madhyamaka i.c.
      ‘pertaining to the transcendent.” (T. Stcherbatsky, The Con-
      ception of Buddhist Nirvana, 1977 Introduction, Pp. 49-50).

89     Puggala means an individual and Pannatti means designation
       or notion. So the phrase Puggala-pannatti indicates designation
       of types of persons.
       ‘Division of human types by one’ gives one quality which the
       individuals    possess.   But    the    gradation            is    not    spiritual.
       Psychological characteristics like perturbable or imperturb-
       able, ethical ones like capable of progress or incapable of
       progress,     metaphysical    like     persons        with        determined      or
       undetermined destiny and spiritual like enlightened or perfectly
       enlightened are all mixed up. There is no gradation as such,
       ‘Division by two’ is mostly psychological. They are classes rather
       than grades. But one class can be said to be superior to another
       e.g.   ‘One   of   perturbable    nature’,       is    inferior      to    ‘one   of
       imperturbable      nature’.   However        a        sort    of    gradation     is
       sometimes found: The person whose attachment to sensual
       pleasures and malevolence has been destroyed is said to be a
       ‘once returner’.....the   person whose            attachment             to sensual
       pleasures and malevolence has been entirely put away is said
       to be a ‘non-returner’.... ‘The person whose attachment to form
       and the formless, to pride, haughtiness and ignorance has been
       entirely put away is said to be an Arahant. (See Designation of
       Human Types (Puggala-Pannatti) Tr. Bimala Charan Law,

       London, Oxford University Press, Amen Corner, E. C. 1922).
90     Paramita means perfection of virtue.

                                       111
Studies in Indian Philosophy


Dana-paramita,         Ksanti-paramita,        Dhyana-paramita,            Sila-
paramita, Virya-paramita and so on.



     must be regarded as the body of the Buddha manifested in
     them. This       is   Relativity, the   climax   of wisdom         (Prajna-
     paramila). (5०८ Stcherbatsky, The Conception of Buddhist
     Nirvana, 1977, ए. 53). By Sunyata is meant relativity or
     contingency (Ibid. ए. 49). The term Stinya in Mahayana, is a
     synonym of dependent existence (Pratitya-samutpada) and
     means      not    something    void     but   something    "devoid"       of
     independent reality (Swabhava-stinya) with the implication that
     nothing short of the whole possesses independent reality (Ibid.
     P. 50). This unique reality although declared to be uncharacter-
     isable (Anirvacaniya), has been variously characterised as the
     “element of the elements” (Dharmanam dharmata) or (Dharma-
     dhitu), as their relativity (Sunyata), as "thisness" (idanta), as
     their relation to "thisness” (idam pratyayata), as "suchness"
      (tathata) as the "suchness of existence" (bhita-tathata), as the
      matrix    of the     Lord   (tathagata-garbha)     and   lastly    as   the
      “cosmical body of the Lord,” as Buddha’s Dharma-kaya" (Ibid.
      P. 55).

      A Foot Note to this runs: The terms Prajad-pdramita and
      abhisamaya when used in an objective sense (Karma-sadhana),
      mean the same, (Ibid. P. 55). We may note that objective sense
      here means accusitive case or Dwitiya Vibhakti as per P. 7 of
      the book under reference. There are six spiritual qualities that
      have to be acquired. Prajna-paramita is a blanket term for all
      these qualities. They are (i) Dana (charity), (ii) Sila (with-
      drawing from evil deeds), (iii) Ksanti (forebearance), (iv) Virya
      (enthusiasm and exertion), (४) Dhyana (concentration), (vi)
      Prajna     (transcendental      insight).    (T.   Stcherbatsky,        The
      Conception of Buddhist Nirvana, 1977, Dr. Jai Dev Singh’s
      Introduction, P. 45).

100   According to Buddhism, Dharma is the unanalysable ultimate
      reality (See Foot Note 34 above). For Vaisesikas, the partless,
      eternal ultimate material causes of things are called atoms
      (Paramianu). There is qualitative difference between an atom
      of one element and an atom of another element. A fire atom
      is qualitatively different from water element. Atoms of one
      element can not produce another element (See M. Hiriyanna,
      Outlines of Indian Philosophy, Pp. 229, 238).

     "Consequent upon the denial of substance is the denial of every
     difference between the categories of substance and quality.
     There is no ‘inherence’ of qualities in substance; in this respect
     all real elements are equally independent. As separate entities
     they then become substances sui generis. "Whatever exists is a
     substance,” says Vasubandhu (Abhidharma 1९0६8 ix, Vidya-
     manam dravyam). Yasomitra adds Svalaksanato vidyamanam
     dravyam. An element is something having an essence ofits own"
      (Svalaksana dharanat dharmah) (४०६0111113, Abhidharma 1९ 088,
      1. 3) is the current definition" (T. Stcherbatsky, The Central
      Conception of Buddhism). "In such judgements as "this is
      Devadatta,” ..."This is moving" etc... the element "this" refers to
      the svalaksana (T. Stcherbatsky, The Conception of Buddhist
      Nirvana, P. 143). According to Dinnaga the particular again is
      not the concrete object usually so designated, but the absolutely
      particular, the unique, the thing which neither has extension
      (desanugata) nor has it duration (kalanugata), it is the thing-
      in-itself,    (svalaksana),   apart   from   anything   else   (Sarvato-
      vyavrtta trailokya-vyavrtta), the point-instant (ksana) (Ibid. ए.
      149). This moment cannot be characterised by something else,
      since this would convert it in a relational existence. It is
      characterised by itself (Svalaksana) (Ibid. P. 154).

      "plastic" mass. We have to grant its existence though we do not

     in his dialogue, Timacus, his only scientific dialogue. Here the
     influence of Pythagoras, the mathematician, is clearly seen.

       Oxford, At the Clarendon Press, 9th impression, 1930, P. 197).
       Reality is being in which there is no division of content from

The first two are realists — realists so far as knowledge is
concerned; objects must be beyond knowledge. The other two
are idealists — idealists so far as knowledge is concerned; objects
must be inside knowledge.

Vaibhasika : Conception of Svalaksana!17, ic. that which
characterises   itself.    It   is   equivalent   to   attribute,   quality,
characteristic, etc. e.g. blueness.

 Vaibhasikas say that only Svalaksanas exist and they arc
 objective. Cf. Dharma.!18 They say that the Svalaksanas are
 directly perceived.

 Sautrantika : Relying upon Suttas. The Sautrantikas say that
 Svalaksanas    are       perceived     not   directly    but   indirectly.


 116 “But meanwhile from the tradition, represented by the teaching
      of the Pali Canon, there were growing out many limbs of
      divergent doctrines and sects. The story of these growths, told
      only in Commentaries, is obscure, but it points to a calamitous
      schism, in which the upholders of the older teaching were
      expelled by the new majority, entitled for             the occasion,
      Vibhajjavadins, or Analysts. The expelled upholders, who
      refused to deny the persisting reality of the man or soul, found
      their way to China, and came Jater to be called Mahayanists.
      The Analysts, represented then mainly by Ceylon, were called
      Hinayanists, 1.8. men of the Great and the Low Vehicle
      respectively” (Buddhism, 1934, P. 26).

Psychological analogy: Between stimulus and perception there
is impression.119 Vaibhasikas do not recognise impression.
Yogacara : Relying upon Yoga. The Yogacaravadins or
Vijnanavadins use Vijiiana 1.6. idea for Svalaksana or Dharma
(Stcherbatsky) only. They are floating ideas. Cf. Berkeley. They
advance the following arguments for their Idealism. (i) The
Svalaksana is objective, Vijiina is subjective.!20 (ii) Analogy
of the dream.121 (iii) Triplet of knowledge, knower and known.
The latter two go away and Jiiana (knowledge) alone remains.122
Disproof of inference or Vyapti. All inferences and judgements
are wrong. Because both of them deal with matters of fact which
do not exist. Hence inference cannot exist. (iv) Relativism. Cf.
Protagoras.!23 Thorns are good for the camel and bad for man.

Madhyamika : Relying upon cight-fold middle path.125
Alayavijiiana,!26 Bhitatathaté.127 They say that even ideas do
not exist. Nagarjuna says nothing exists. Cf. Zeno.128 This is

     another. Hence judgements like, ‘a thing is good’ or ‘a thing is

124 In his Republic, Plato gives a fitting reply to Herakicitos’
     antinomianism.     Herakleitos   had    said   that   no   absolute
     judgement is possible because sea water is good for fish and
     bad for men; or the beginning of a circle and the end of a circle
     are the same; or again, night and day are the same. This
      antinomianism evoked a counter-antinomianism from Plato
     when he said, ‘A man and no man, seeing and not seeing, a
      bird and no bird, sitting upon wood and no wood, struck it and
      did not strike it, with a stone and no stone...’ (See R. D. Ranade,
      Philosophical and Other Essays, Part I, Pp. 11-12).
 125 This is the description of the famous middle path of the eight

      pluarality (Ananartham), (7) no coming in (Anagamam) and
      (8) no goint out (Anirgamam), (Nagarjuna, Milamadhyama-

129   "The real Buddha must be perceived directly by intuition..."
      (T. Stcherbatsky, The Conception of Buddhist Nirvana, 1977,
      P. 52). "The Buddhist saint is supposed, in a moment of mystic
      illumination, suddenly to perceive the whole construction, with
      its gross and mystic worlds, as vividly as if it were a direct sense
      perception. As a psychological process, it is equally taught in
      Hinayana and in Mahayana, but its content, the picture which
      reveals itself at this moment, is quite different in both systems.
      It corresponds to their theoretical parts, to the system of
      pluaralism which is taught in Hinayana and to the monist view
      which is the central conception of Mahayana’ (Ibid. P. 19). "The
      supreme moment of illumination is the central point of the
      teaching about the path of salvation. An enormous literature,
      especially in Mahayana, is devoted to this conception of mystic
      intuition” (Ibid. P. 20).

 137    "...dharmadhatu or dharmata or tathata is the word used in

       truth is indicated by ‘thatness’ or tathata; thisness is the truth
       as it appears to us. All agree that “Tathata’ is the higher truth
       and it indicates the essence of a thing but the word Tathata is
       sometimes rendered as ‘thisness’. Inspite of this the implication
       of Tathata is unchanged.

Vaisesikas also are called Ardha-vainasikas.138 Nagarjuna says:
nothing comes into existence. Nothing goes out of existence.
Creation is impossible. Annihilation is impossible. Cause is an
illusion, effect is an illusion. No unity exists, and no plurality
exists.



138 The Vaisesikas hold that nearly half of the objects in the world
     are subject to destruction. Hence they are called Ardha-
     vainasikas (semi-nihilists). The Bauddhas advocate that all
     things in the world are subject to destruction. Therefore, they
     are   called   Sarva-vainadsikas   (universal   nihilists).    Because
     Gaudapiada and Sankara uphold the doctrine of two Realities
     like the   Bauddhas,    critics of Advaitism call them Ardha-
     vainasikas or semi-nihilists.
     "The Buddhistic doctrine of total annihilation... is only a further
     and an inevitable development of the Vaisesika doctrine of
     असत्कार्यवाद; while the categories or Padarthas of the latter find
     their counterpart in the five Astikayas or essences of the Jainas.
     The atomic theory moreover is largely adopted by the Jainas,
     and even enters into their legendary mythology. The epithet
     Ardha-vainasikas or Semi-Buddhists, contemptuously bestow-
     ed upon the Vaisesika by Sankaracarya concealed a historical
     truth, if the Vaisesikas were the half-hearted precursors who
     by their materialistic speculations paved the way for the extreme
     radicalism     of Gautam   Buddha"     (Sce Athalye and         Bodas,
     Tarkasangraha of Annambhatta, Introduction, P. xxv).
     "The Bauddhas denied the authority of the Vedas altogether,
     while the Naiyayikas made it dependent on God’s authoriship.
     This may be one reason why the Naiyayikas and Vaisesikas were
     termed by their more orthodox rivals, अर्धवैनाशि क5 or Semi-

     Buddhists" (Ibid. P. 353).

2 (B) Mahayana Buddhism
Three chief conceptions in Mahayana Buddhism. Mahayanists
expelled believers in soul.145 (i) The Tathata or Thatness. (ii)
Vijnana or Idea (11) Siinya or void. (Viyjnanavadins, Siinyavadins:
See Das Gupta).146 The first is stressed by ASvaghosa, 117 the
second    by     the    Lankavatara-sitral48,           and      the   third    by
Nagarjuna.!49 All the three really are interrelated. ASvaghosa


     human     form,        He   conjures    up   a   phantom        body    called
     Nirmanakaya. It is through this that He incarnates as a Buddha,
     as a saviour of mankind. The actual physical body of the Buddha
     is the Rupakaya. It will thus be seen that Buddhism is not a
     historical religion like Christianity. The Buddha is not the
     founder of a religion. He only transmits Dharma which is
     eternal. There have been many Buddhas before, and there will
     be   many    Buddhas        in   the   future.   (त.     Stcherbatsky,    The
     Conception        of   Buddhist    Nirvana,      Prof.    Jai   Dev    Singh’s
     Introduction, Pp. 48-49).
     Dharmakiaya is also known as Swabhavika-kaya or the essential,
     natural kaya of the Buddha. The Nirmana-kaya is assumed for
     the time being for a specific purpose. The Ripa-kaya or the
     actual physical body of the Buddha is visible to every one. The
     Nirmana-kaya is visible only to adepts (Ibid. Prof. Jai Dev
     Singh’s Introduction, P. 49). See also Foot Note 54 above.

Tathagata-garbha is the womb of such. Considered in such a
way, the Tathata Philosophy of Asvaghosa comes dangerously
near to Vedantism.152 The Buddha says that all things forever
abide in Nirvana. The Tathata of ASvaghosa comes nearer to the
Vedantic Absolute. ASvaghosa’s approach to the Vedantic
Absolute in his philosophy of Tathataé may be explained by his
early Brahmanical nurture.
Vijhanavada : Vijhanavada is a philosophy ofideas. Conscious-
ness creeps in only surreptitiously. In Berkeley consciousness
is the very foundation. Alayavijiiana which is another name for
mind is merely the habitat of ideas. The Vijfanas are to the
Alayavijiiana as the waves are to the ocean. In the Alayavijnana
are produced the ripples of sense-experience or ideas. They are
what we may call the modes. The relation between Vijiana and
Vijfadnalaya is both that of identity and difference. The ocean is
both identical with and different from waves. What causes these
waves? The Vijfadna philosophy tells us that Avidya causes it,
just as the wind is responsible for the waves in the ocean. This
principle of Avajja in Vijianavada comes again perilously near
to the Vedantic doctrine of Maya. What exists essentially is
merely the idea. In the all-conserving mind, ignorance manifests
itself in the shape of the perceiver and the perceived. Various
grades ofindividuation due to ignorance, more than the number
of the sands on the Ganges. Buddha’s answer to Ravana in the
Lankavatara-sutra. Buddha does not teach the Nairatmya or
Nihsvabhava doctrine, lest his followers should be scared away.
The external world is merely an expression ofthese ideas. Hence
there is no objective world as such. Cf. Berkeley and Johnson’s

     which is transcendent, beyond all pairs or ineffable.

Siinyavada has seven modes of voidness: (i) interdependence of

                              in Space, Time, Cause, Mind,
Gorgias downwards.!56 Disbelief


     reference to it, is the distinctive activity of the ego" (Edna
     Heidbreder, Seven Psychologies, Appleton Century-Crofts Inc.
     New York, 1933, P. 398). The ego permits of adaptation to
     reality and supports the reality principle. Freud compared it
     with the rider who "must curb the superior strength of his horse
     and must borrow the means to do so". The ego tries to curb
     the blind demands of the id, and hence the conflict between
     the   two.   According to   Freud    the death    instinct   is the
     counterpart of the life instinct or love instinct (libido). Freud
     postulates that the organism has an innate tendency to revert
     to its initial state. This instinct, which would lead to self-
     destruction, has to be diverted outward by the developing
     organism. The life instinct is largely synonymous with energy.
     It is responsible for love, friendship, co-operation, constructive
     activity and so on. The death instinct, on the other hand, is
     responsible for enmity, ill-will, envy or destruction. The conflict
     between them goes on.
     C.G. Jung, a follower of Freud, later disagreed with him on
     many counts. For him the primary drive in animals is life energy.
     It expresses itself
                       in many ways in life. As ego principle in man,
     it has to face the external circumstances — the reality. When
     the ego principle is not powerful enough to deal successfully
     with the reality principle, mental disorders arise, says Jung. In
     Freud also there is polarity between Pleasure-principle and
     Reality-principle.                                       ,

VI. Badarayana’s Criticism of Buddhism
Badarayana’s criticisms of Buddhism           fall       into two Adhi-
karanas,164 The first is supposed to be devoted to the realists,
including the Vaibhasikas and the Sautrantikas, the second either
to the Vijiianavadins (Belvalkar) or to the Siinyavadins (Jacobi).
Jacobi does not seem to be wrong; on the other hand, he seems
to be right on account of the reference in the Sttras to Abhava.
In any case, it is a criticism of the doctrine of the void by
whomsoever it may have been preached, and at any rate, the
Siinyavadins preached it more than the Vijiianavadins.
Points in Sankara’s criticism: (i) Samudaya (=Sanghita, cf.
Stcherbatsky),    an    impossible      concept      :    conglomeration
 impossible.165 (ii) Santana (cf. Stcherbatsky), or continuity, an



      Paramarthohi aryanim      tisnim bhavah"       (To the   saints   the
      Absolute is just silence, i.e. it is inexpressible).       (See T.
      Stcherbatsky, The Conception of Buddhist Nirvana, 1977, Prof.
      Jai Dey Singh’s Introduction, P. 51). Cf. also: "A long discussion
      about the essence of the absolute is given in the Vimalakirti-
      50178. The question is tackled from different sides and when
      Vimalakirti is at length asked to summarise, he remains silent,
      where upon Bodhisattva Manjusri exclaims, "Well donc, Well
      done, non-duality is truly above words!" (Ibid. P. 27).

VII. Dialectical Buddhism! 75

173   Ibid. Pp. 115-116.
174   Sanya is not equal to Void. See Foot Note 160 above.
175   Dialectic is a way of argument which scrutinises all sides of a
      question and shows that reason can not explain all problems
      but that one has to go beyond reason in search of the solution
      of the problem. Reason can work only in the empirical world.
      It gets stuck up if it tries to enter the transcendental realm
      which is out of bounds for it. Hence one has to take resort to
      dialectic. The origin of Buddhist dialectic lies in Nagarjuna’s
      attempt to find out why Buddha was silent on certain questions.
      Nagarjuna enumerated fourteen such questions, arranged them
      in four sets — the first three sets consisting of four questions
      and the last consisting of two. The first two sets refer to the
      World (its eternity and infinitude), the third to Tathagata (his
      existence after death) and the fourth to the soul (its relation
      with body). In each set the question is dealt with by giving four
      alternatives, i.e. ‘is’, ‘is not’, ‘both is and is not’ and ‘neither is,
      nor is not’. One example will suffice. The first set of questions
      runs: (a) the world is eternal (b) The world is not eternal (c)
      The world is both eternal and non-eternal (d) The world is
      neither eternal nor non-eternal.
      Nagarjuna showed that the way out of the impasse was not to
      get entangled in such wranglings but to go beyond reason. In
      all this, Nagarjuna, as if, foreshadowed how Kant, centuries
      later, was to argue. Kant also gave four antinomies — two
      concerning the world, the third to self and the last to God.




                                    137
Studies in Indian Philosophy

Santaraksita and Kamalasila (Typed separately).176                      a
Vill. An Exfoliation of the Different Conceptions of Nirvana! ५
(1) Psychological: (i) Rooting out of Desire, (ii) Cessation of
Suffering (especially Adhyatmika).
(2) Epistemological: (i) Discovery of the Law of Causality. (ii)
Destruction of Avidya or ignorance. Snapping of the first link


     Nagarjuna examined the views of his opponents by applying
     such dialectic and showed that they were logically absurd. The
     purpose of Nagarjuna’s dialectic was to disprove the views of
     opponents, and not to prove his own thesis because he had no
      thesis to put forth. The purpose of the destructive endeavour
      was to attack the dogmatism of his opponents who depended
      only on reason. Nagarjuna wanted to take resort to Prajna or
      intuition. The problems of causality, motion and rest, or Nirvana
      were subjected to a searching examination with the help of the
      dialectic (See T. Stcherbatsky, The Conception of Buddhist
      Nirvana, 1977, Prof. Jai Dev Singh’s Introduction, Pp. 14 ff).
      See also T. Stcherbatsky, Buddhistic Logic, Vol. 1. P. 44 and
       Pp. 444 ff.
  176 Santaraksita is a leading figure of the Sautrantika school. So
       are Kamalasila, Dharmakirti and Dharmottara. The dialectics
       of Santaraksita and Kamalasila against Carvakas is famous. "It
       may be said in passing that Santaraksita and Kamalasila
       represent     a   syncretism   of    the   Madhyamaka   system   and
       Vijnanavada and can not be strictly called Madhyamikas.
       Santaraksita flourished in the 8th century A. D. His great work
       was Tattva-sangraha (Compendium of Reality). His celebrated
       pupil Kamalasila wrote the Tattva-saigraha-panjika, a learned
       commentary on Tattva-sangraha." (Ibid. P. 14). (Prof. Ranade’s

      typed notes on this head could not be found).

                                                                 to
      Indian Philosophy’, University of Poona, 1987, Pp. 25-27.




                                      138
                                                        Buddhism


in the chain of the twelve Nidanas (by knowledge).
(3) Ontological: (i) Annihilation of being, and perpetuation of
Being. Realisation of essencelessness, negative; realisation of
Tathata, positive. (ii) Going beyond Being and not-Being.
Transcending duality.

(4) Ethical: (i) Equanimity, cooling away or peace. The ocean
becomes still. Cf. apathy and ataraxy.!78 (ii) Sanctification. Cf.
The Mahayana conception of consecration.

(5) Mystical: (i) Realization of supreme bliss. The different
conceptions of Nirvana are summarised very beautifully by Mr.
Rhys Davids in his book, Early Buddhism, with a wealth of
epithets such as, "the harbour of refuge, the cool cave, the island
amidst the floods, the place of bliss, emancipation, liberation,
safety, the supreme, the transcendental, the uncreated, the
tranquil, the home of case, the calm, the end of suffering, the
medicine for all evil, the unshaken, the ambrosia, the immaterial,
the imperishable, the abiding, the further shore, the unending,
the bliss ofeffort, the supreme joy, the ineffable, the detachment,
the holy city".179 (ii) Living in Dharmakaya here and now.
Dharmakaya is a logico-metaphysical, as well as an ethico-
religious conception. This last conception of Nirvana may be
compared to the Vedantie Jivanmukti, the Pari-nirvana being
comparable to the Vedantic Videhamukti. The Mahaparinirvana
of the Buddha may be something higher!

183   Two Brahmins Vasistha and Bharadwaja went to Buddha to
      know the right path leading to union with the Brahma. When
      asked, they replied that no Brahmin versed in the Vedas had
      seen Brahma face to face. Buddha remarked that if Brahma
      can not be known or seen, those who search for him search in
      vain. It is like making a stair-case to a mansion about which no
      one has the faintest idea. The implication of Buddha’s answer
      in this story is that apprehending God face to face is impossible.
      This distrust of mystical experience is probably because of
      Canonical influence. There is an unmistakable clement of
      mystical intuition in early Buddhism. (8८९ Paul Carus. The

  187    (i) The first stage is Duraroha, "hard to reach". In this stage




                                      142
                                                        Buddhism




Bodhisattvas are at first unconverted. They exercise the eight
good qualities like liberality, compassion and so on. Through
certain vices (sensual pleasure, sloth and so on), they may fail
to reach the next stage. They are reborn again and again till
the vices are eradicated. (ii) Baddhamana, "bound". Here they
have a dislike for existences. On account of 28 vices (love of
gain and praise etc.) they fail to reach the next stage. (iii)
Puspamandita, "adorned with flowers". They sacrifice their
wealth and themselves to obtain a single "well-spoken" verse of
the Docirine. Because of 14 vices, they fail to reach the next
stage. (iv) Rucira, "Beautiful". The Bodhisattvas avoid certain
great crimes and reccive births as gods or in high positions.
For sins such as corrupting nuns they fail to reach the next
stage. (४) In the fifth stage, Citravistara, "of varied expanse’,
they look on all existence as on fire with lust, hate and delusion.
(vi) They arrive at the next stage, Riipavati, "of fair form", with
the thought, "few delights has this terrible whirlpool of the
world". Here there are innumerable Buddhas cach ina       separate
field or universe. (vii) Durjaya, "hard to conquer". Their minds
turn to self-control. Through the thought of great compassion
they pass to the next stage. Now onward there is no turning
back. (viii) Janma-nidega, "discussion of birth". A list of
Buddhas is given, under whom in the first seven births
Sakyamuni made the roots of goodness to grow. Here
Bodhisattvas become Buddhas. They are great teachers. (ix)
Yauvarajya,   "Viceroyship".   The   list   of such   Buddhas    15
continued. (x) Tenth stage is Abhiseka, "Consecration as King".
The Bodaisattvas are born in the Tusita heaven, and then they
have a desire for a human existence. They do not indulge in
sensual pleasures. "Although Bodhisattvas develop into beings
in whom human action is a mere appearance, yet they have
begun as ordinary fallible creatures and it is through their great

                                 150
                       CHAPTER IV

                            JAINISM

I. Pan-psychism (a better word than Monadism)!
1. More consistent Atomism than that of the Vaisesikas. All


1   Jainism believes that all matter is ensouled. So did Leibnitz,
    the German philosopher who gave the doctrine of Monadism.
    His monads, like the Jaina atoms, are psychical in nature; they
    are spiritual centres of force. But Leibnitz taught another
    doctrine — the Identity of Indiscernibles: if two things can not
    be discerned from each other, i.c. ifthey are same in all respects,
    they are not really two but are identical. Monads are discernible
    from one another; they all mirror the world differently, in their
    own particular way. Each monad is different from all other
    monads and there is no possibility of one being transmuted into
    another. Qualitative transformation is thus ruled out from
    monadism. On the other hand, it is an essential feature of
    Jainism. As per Leibnitz, the monads develop internally and
    represent different grades. They give a hierarchy. The gradation
    reaches the highest point in the central monad or the Monad
    of monads. This Monad is equivalent to God. On the contrary,
    Jainism is atheistic; it allows no God. Another difference
    between Jainism and Leibnitz is, that for Leibnitz the lowest
    matter (materia prima) has force and it represents a grade of
    spirit though the lowest one. The Jainas make a clear cut
    distinction between spirit and matter (Jiva and Ajiva). It is
     therefore not proper to call Jaina doctrine monadism. The
    better word to describe it is pan-psychism (pan = all, psyche
      = mind) according to which everything has a soul or mind.
     (See also, R. D. Ranade, Vedanta the Culmination of Indian
     Thought, Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Dr. K. M. Munshi Marg,
     Bombay 7, 1970, Pp. 10-11). See also Foot Note 21 below.
Studies in Indian Philosophy


atoms qualitatively alike.2 Transmutation of metals into gold,
therefore, not chimerical.3 The Jainas did not make sufficient
use of Dharma or motion (Anaxagoras) in their cosmology.*


2    Scientifically all atoms are qualitatively alike. All elements
     consist of atoms. All atoms contain three main particles —
     protons, electrons and neutrons. Protons are positively charged
     particles, electrons are negatively charged particles, while
     neutrons have no charge. Protons and neutrons are in the core,
     called nucleus. Electrons revolve round the nucleus. In an atom,
      protons and electrons are the same in number. The chemical
      properties of an atom depend on the number of protons in the
      nucleus. Each element has a specific number of protons in its
      atom.   Thus   all   the   atoms      are   qualitatively   alike   but
      quantitatively different. By quality is meant the type of particles
      present and by quantity, the number of particles present. See
      Foot Note 3 below.

      According to Jainism, all atoms are of the same kind and can
      not be categorised. They all have touch, taste, smell and colour.
      By   developing      any   of   these   characteristics,    qualitative
      differentiation can take place. Thus, matter which is quite
      indefinite, can be transmuted. One metal, say iron, can be
      transmuted into another, say gold. To turn metals into gold was
       a dream of the alchemist. On the Jaina view, this is very much
       possible. See Foot Note 2 above and Foot Note 14 below.
       Contrast, however, Nyaya view in Foot Note 13 in Chapter V.
      The Jainas explained combination of atoms as due to humidity
       of atoms. Anaxagoras (a Greek philosopher, died about 429
       B.C.) introduced the concept of an intelligent force, nous, which
       arranges and organises everything with a definite design. It scts
       atoms in motion. Likewise the Jainas could well have attributed
       the power of motion to the ensouled atoms.

2. Every material atom ensouled by Jiva. Hylozoism7 (Jacobi)

5   Democritus, the atomist (Greek philosopher, about 460-370
    B.C.), transferred motion to the primary substance itself. The
    atoms are in a state of rotary motion. By this motion, atoms
    are brought together or are separated. Out of this motion the
    worlds are created. Thus formation of things depends on the
    twin principles of matter and motion (E. Zeller, Outlines of
    the History of Greek Philosophy, Pp. 66-67). Empedocles
     (Greek philosopher, about 495—435 B.C.) had used love and
    hate as principles which are responsible for motion. Anaxagoras
     introduced nous (mind) for the purpose. But Democritus does
     not need any outside force. He is reported to have said, ‘Give
     me matter and motion and I will explain the whole world to
     you’. See also Foot Note 112 in Chapter VJ, and Foot Note 100
     in Chapter V.
6    Kundakundacarya is a Jaina teacher. He is the author of
     Pancastikaya-sangraha-siittra. According to him atoms have
     different degrees of humidity. Some are dry, some are viscous.
     "Two atoms form a compound (Skandha), when 010 is viscous
     and the other dry or both are of different degrees of viscocity

     or dryness" (S.N. Das Gupta, A History of Indian Philosophy,
     Vol. I, P. 196). See also Foot Notes 16 and 17 below.
7    —Hylozoism is a doctrine according to which all matter has life.

4 द्व्यणुक, others follow themeselves.!7            (त) While Democritus


13   According to Jainism, matter is ultimately indefinite, having no
     specific nature. Atoms have no size and qualitative changes are
     very much possible. Atoms are divisible. In Democritus, atoms
     (dense bodies) are indivisible. Unlike in Jainism they are
     distinguishable by their shape and size and are absolutely
     incapable of qualitative change.
14   Alchemy — an carly form of chemistry, with philosophical and
     magic associations, studied in the Middle Ages. Its chief aims
     were to change baser metals into gold and to discover the elixir
     of perpetual youth. (Sce also Foot Note 3 above).
15   In Jainism Dharma is the principle of motion, and Adharma
     the principle of rest. Dharma helps matter to move as water
     helps fish to move. On the other hand, Adharma helps matter
     to remain stable. Hence Dharma and Adharma are physical
     principles as contrasted with moral ones. They do not mean
     merit and demerit, but mean motion and rest.
16   According to Democritus atoms are in perpetual motion.
     Because of the motion they are combined or are separated.
     The combination takes place on the basis of size and shape of
     atoms. Some atoms have even surfaces, some have rough

     surfaces, some have sharp or hooked surfaces. On account of
     this they catch hold of each other. Sce also Foot Notes 6 above
     and 17 below.
17   The Tarka-dipika states that motion is first produced in the
     atoms   as   a   result   of   God’s   will.   This   motion   produces

3. Gradation of Jivas according to development of sensations
(Is this biologically sound?)!9 One sense: minerals; touch. Two
senses: worms; touch + taste. Three senses: ants; (Do not the
ants see?); touch + taste + smell. Four senses: bees; touch + taste
+ smell + sight. Five senses: men; touch + taste + smell + sight
+ hearing. Hearing seems to be the highest sense according to




      conjunction of two atoms which is called a दुव्यणुक (Diad).

      Athalye and Bodas, Tarka-sangraha of Annambhatta, P. 118).
      See Foot Note 6 above for Jaina view.
 18   Things differ in their qualities because of the difference of
      shape and size as well as position and arrangement of atoms
      out of which they are constituted. Democritus does not treat
      all qualities of things alike. He says that weight, density or
      hardness are qualities which belong to things themselves. Other
      qualities like colour, depend on the perceiver. Qualities which
      belong to objects themselves, were called primary qualities by
      John Locke (an English philosopher, known as father of
      modern empiricism, 1632-1704). Those depending on the
      perceiver are called secondary qualities. Thus we find that the
      distinction between primary and secondary qualities was very
      much present in Democritus. In Jainism, Pudgala or matter has
      some inalienable qualities like touch or taste. By a development
      of these qualities, objects acquire newer qualities. The new
      qualities do not depend upon the perceiver. As such the
      question of primary and secondary qualities docs not arise in
      Jainism,
 19   The distribution of sensations right from minerals upto man is
      not scientifically justifiable.

also have not the power of creation which God alone possesses).

zoism or monadism, though with cither it has got analogies.21

20   See Section VI. (ii) (a) below.
21   There is agreement between hylozoism and Jainism. Hylo-

     zoism gives life to everything; so does Jainism. But atoms in
     Jainism have certain qualities like touch, taste, colour and so

     on. Thus their atoms have not only life but souls. Therefore,
     inspite of agreement between Hylozoism         and Jainism   in
     attributing life to everything, Jainism is better described as
     pan-psychism.
     Now Jainism and monadism agree in maintaining that all

     substances are psychical in nature, and agree again in holding
     that atoms are indistinguishable from one another and are
     shapcless. Notwithstanding these common points, monadism is
     not a fitting description of the Jaina theory. (6९८ Foot Note 1
     above).

Studies in Indian Philosophy


Syadvada.22 The Syadvada-maiijari, which is a standard epitome
of Jaina doctrine, discusses both the Nayas as well as the
Syadvada (which is itself called Sapta-bhangi naya). For the
discussion of the Nayas vide also ViSesavasyaka Bhasya on the
Niryuktis of Bhadrabahu.23 (a) There are infinite Nayas (philo-
sophical standpoints or doctrines) possible. Anckanta- vada.24

22     Naya means ‘a point of view’ or ‘a standpoint’. A thing has
       infinite qualities. But to take any early, familiar or otherwise
       accidental characterisation of a thing as definitive is a fallacy.
       R. 8. Perry calls it a fallacy of definition by initial predication.
       For example, the letter ‘a’ is the second letter in the word man,
       no doubt; but to define the letter ‘a’ as a second letter in the
       word man is wrong. The letter appears in innumerable ways in
       innumerable words. The fallacy occurs because              only   an
       exclusive character out of a multiple characters is taken to
       describe a thing. (See, Present Philosophical Tendencies,
       Longmans, Green and Co., London, 1929, Pp. 127-128). The
       typical Jaina example is the story of blind men describing an
        elephant. Each of them thinks that his exclusive standpoint is
        definitive. He who touches the foot of the elephant calls the
        elephant a pillar and so on with different parts of the elephant’s
        body. This, according to the Jainas, is wrong. To describe a
        thing only from one standpoint is a fallacy (Nayabhasa). In
        order to avoid the fallacy, every statement should be prefixed
        by the word ‘Syad’ (probably). No judgement is absolutely true;
        and no judgement is absolutely false; hence all judgements are
        relative or probable.

        an Arhat (433-357 B.C.).

        Ekanta-vada (fallacy of exclusive particularity); to recognise
        that there are infinite points of view is Anckanta-vada (doctrine
