                                                               Jainism


But the Jainas consider only four Nayas as prominent.25
The Sangrahanaya (synoptic) of Vedanta, all-inclusiveness. The
Vyavahara-naya of Sankhya, the practical (pragmatic) or
scientific standpoint. The Naigama-naya of Nyaya. This refers
to the Nigamana of Naiyayikas. And the Rju-sitra-naya of
Buddhism (see Das Gupta).26 The Jainas approve of the
straightness of the Buddhists!27 (Radhakrishnan, however, says


     of multiple predication).
25    The four Nayas are: (i) Sangraha-naya, (ii) Vyavahara-naya,
     (iii) Naigama-naya and (iv) Rju-sitra-naya. (i) Sangraha-naya
     consists in including things under the widest class; for example,
     a book is included under ‘entity. The book may have specific
     qualities, but its most general character of being an entity 15
     here referred to. The Jainas ascribe this view to Vedanta
     because according to Vedanta everything, whether animate or
     inanimate is subsumed under Brahman. (ii) The Vyavahara-
     naya takes into consideration both the general and specific
     characteristics of a thing; for instance, the book is a useful
     entity. Along with being an entity its specific character of being
     useful is also mentioned. As per the Jaina opinion, Vyavahara-
     naya is held by the Sankhyas. (iii) Naigama-naya is an empirical
     view. Here not much stress is laid on either general or specific
     characters. Talking about a book, we may call it an entity, or
     we may Call it a useful thing as well. The Jainas lay this view at
     the door of the Nyadya-Vaisesika schools. (iv) Rju-sitra-naya
     takes a thing as a mere conglomeration of qualitics with no
     underlying substratum. The book is a bundle of qualities like
     size, shape, weight or colour. According to the Jainas, this view
     is held by the Buddhists.
26   A History of Indian Philosophy, Vol. 1, Cambridge, At the
     University Press, 1957, Pp. 196 ff.
27   All the four Nayas mentioned above are, say the Jainas, only



                                 159
Studies in Indian Philosophy


that Sangraha-naya belongs to Sankhya and Vyavahara-naya
belongs to Vedanta (Indian Philosophy, Vol. I, P. 301). Is this
right?28 Just because all these Nayas look at Reality cach from
its special point of view, they can not apprehend Reality at all.
Hence arise Nayabhasas or antinomics.29            Four antinomies as


      partial stand-points and hence fallacious (Nayabhasas). Now,
      if the Jainas regard the Buddhist view as erroncous, how can
      they call it Rju (straight or upright)?
28    The Vedanta takes a synoptic or all-inclusive view. Brahman is
      the most general or universal category in which everything is
      included. Hence Vedanta view must be described as the
      Sangraha-naya, as the Jainas rightly do. Dr. Radhakrishnan’s
      inclusion of Vedanta under Vyavahiara-naya does not sccm to
      be right. Likewise, the Sankhya view is scientific and pragmatic;
      and Vyavahara-naya is its proper description. Dr. Radha-
      krishnan’s inclusion of the Sankhya view under Sangraha-naya
      does not seem to be correct.
 29   The Jaina stand is that all the four Nayas, Sangraha-naya,
      Vyavahara-naya,      Naigama-naya      and    Rju-sitra-naya     are
      dogmatic statements. Now, one dogma is always countered by
      another dogma. Dogmas contain only partial truth. To treat it
      as the absolute truth, is Nayadbhasa, a fallacy. This is exactly
      how Kant criticised the two theories of rationalism              and
      empiricism. Both were one-sided according to him. Edward
      Caird describes this stand in his monumental volume on Kant.
      We may quote Prof. Ranade’s summary of Caird’s exposition:
      "Such a one-sidedness, however, calls forth an opposite one-
      sidedness; this half truth, according to the law of Nemesis,
       another half truth. A one-sided dogmatism has the opposite
      dogmatism latent in itself. And the history of metaphysics is a
      splendid    record   of   the    perpetual   conflict   of   opposite
      dogmatisms.”

in Kant.30  (©) Or, as in Radhakrishnan, the Sankhyas believe
in Satkarya-vada, effect in cause; the Vaisesikas believe in

these views are only probable. (०) Or again, the Upanisads say
that the self exists and the Buddhists say that it does not exist
(See Das Gupta)31; both views again are only probable. It
follows, therefore, that not absolute knowledge but Relativity
alone is the truth. Cf. Sophists.32_ What we call knowledge is
only probable knowledge.

(ii) Hence, the seven-fold formulation of Syad-vada or as it is



30   An antinomy consists in putting forth a thesis which is
     confronted by its opposite or the anti-thesis. Kant gives the
     following four antinomies: (i) Thesis: The world is limited in
     space and time. Antithesis: The world is not limited in space
     and time. (ii) Thesis: Things are divisible into simple parts.
     Antithesis: Things are not divisible into simple parts. (iii)
     Thesis: There is freedom in nature. Antithesis: There is no
     freedom in nature. (iv) Thesis: The absolutely necessary Being
     exists. Antithesis: There is no absolutely necessary Being.
     It may be noted that Kant does provide a reconciliation or
     synthesis of all the four antinomies.

32   Sophists,   generally   speaking,   shifted   their   interest   from
     philosophy of nature to philosophy of man. There was also a
     slight distrust of human powers to probe the depths of natural
     phenomena. The most important representative of the Sophists,
     Protogoras, gave the maxim — man is the measure of things, he
     is the judge of things. What appears good to one man may not
     be so to another man. There is no absolute religion, no absolute
     morality and no absolute justice and so on. This fits in the
     mould of the Jaina concept of Nayabhasa.

36   See Foot Note 113 in Chapter III.
37   The moral law is a Categorical Imperative. It is unconditional
     and admits of no exception. It is to be observed not for gaining
     anything, but simply because it is obligatory. Hence a probable
     moral law is an absurdity. Moksa which implies freedom from
     clinging to matter must be absolute. If the highest ideal or
     Kaivalya is itself probable, the whole striving for its achievement
     would be an exercise in futility. The Tirthankara is an
     embodiment of perfect knowledge. If we were to give up the
     idea of perfect knowledge, we have likewise to give up the idea
     of Tirthankara and with this the Jainas will never agree. For
     them Tirthankara is very much a reality.
     Hence probability is not applicable to the moral law, to Moksa
     and to Tirthankara.

40   Sankara’s criticism (contained in his Bhasya on Vedanta-sutra
     II. 2.33) states that if the Jaina doctrine of relativity is accepted,
     the Tirthankara’s teaching would itself be relative. It is just an
     application of a general statement to a particular case. So far
     so good. It is beating the enemy on his own ground. But a couple
     of observations which Sankara makes before he comes to the
     above-mentioned conclusion, are not sound. Sankara puts to
      the Jaina philosopher that his unlimited (नि रंकुश) assertion viz.,
      ‘all knowledge is relative’ should be applied to itself. If so
      applied, contends Sankara, it loses certainty and the Jaina
      thinker’s claim of the relativity of knowledge just vanishes. But
      this can not be granted to Sankara. The Jaina statement of the
      relativity of knowledge can not be applied to itself. Here is a
      parallel. "A similar paradox has become classic in the history
      of philosophy, the paradox of Epimenides the Cretan, who said:
      all Cretans are liars. Now if what he says is true his assertion
      is a lie and so Cretans are not liars. But if Cretans are not liars,
      his first assertion is true and so Cretans are liars. Thus, when
      he tells the truth, he lies and when he lies he tells the truth,
      which is aself-contradiction. The self-contradiction disappears,
      according to Tarski if we make a distinction between language
      and meta-language.     He points out, that a sentence asserting
      that some sentence S is a true sentence of some language L,
      can not itself be a sentence of the language L, but must belong
      to a meta-language in which the sentences of L are not used

      but are mentioned and discussed" (5.5. Barlingay and Padma
      Kulkarni, A Critical Survey of Western            Philosophy, The



                                   164
                                                                      Jainism


pivot of Jainism would come to an end. May it not be, however,
that, as recently in the case of Buddhism,

the founder and the dogma might be sharply distinguished?41
Yes. Cf. पूज्यपाद'$ समाधि शतक which is perfectly spiritual.42 It is


     Macmillan Company of India, Ltd., Delhi, 1980, P. 52). So in
     the sentence: ‘A Cretan says All Cretans are liars’, the part: ‘A
     Cretan says’ does not belong to the same language L but to a

     meta-language M. So when a              self-reference is eliminated the

     contradiction disappears. Similarly, the Jaina statement that

45   Herbert Spencer’s contention is that the First Cause can not
     be known but its positive existence is a necessary datum of
     consciousness. To put it simply, Spencer’s position is that he
     knows that the First Cause exists, though not what it is. He calls
     that principle Incomprchensible Power. James Ward’s criticism
     is   that   once      you        attribute      Causality or   Power   to   the
     Unknowable, it no longer remains unknowable. Another
     contention of Spencer is that strict knowledge is confined only
     to appearances and the ultimate reality can by no means be
     called an appearance, and hence it remains unknowable.
     Ward’s objection to such a position is : if there is appearance,
     there must be, behind it, some reality that appears. But how,
     "can reality appear, shine forth, and yet remain totally and for
     ever beyond the knowledge of those to whom it appears?” Why
     not take appearance to be a manifestation of a Supreme Mind?
     Why aitach to it the bad sense of concealing rather than the
     good sense of revealing, queries Ward. Therefore if you know
     the appearances, to that extent you do know reality; its
     unknowability melts away                    (See, J. Ward, Naturalism and
     Agnoticism, Vol. II, Adam and Charles Black, London, second
     edition, 1903, Pp. 267-276).
46   The Isavasya Mantra (5) runs as :
     तदेजति   तन्नैजति   तदूरे तद्वन्ति   के
     तदन्तरस्य सर्वस्य तदु सर्वस्यास्य बाह्यतः |
     (Jt moves, it moves not. It is far and it is near. It is within all
     this, and it is outside of all this). This is the experience of saints
     and sages and is very much real. Discursive intellect which
     operates under the banner of the Law of Contradiction can not

     explain this; and therefore, calls it ineffable.

 48   वक्तव्य and अवक्तव्य are contrary terms. Therefore वक्तव्य 15 not
      the same as न अवक्तव्य. The contradictory of वक्तव्य is TAHA.

 49   Change without a permanent identity is absurd, because there
      would be nothing to change. If there is transition of independent
      things, to talk of change is meaningless. If A changes into B,
      A must have some sensible duration and not merely momentary
      existence (See A.E, Taylor, Elements of Metaphysics, Methuen
      and Co. Ltd., London, 13th Edition, 1952, Pp. 160 ff). A thing
      is relative to some other thing. That other thing would be
      relative to yet another thing and so on. This leads to infinite
      regress. To overcome this we have to stop somewhere and posit
      some entity as absolute.

50   Bosanquet holds that no negation is possible without a positive
     basis. ‘A is not B’ would be a significant negation only on the
     basis of some positive background. A is not B means A is
     something else (Ibid. P. 19 and Foot Note 1 where this view is
     supported by mentioning Bosanquet). See also Foot Note 10
     in Chapter V.
51   Traditional logic gives twelve kinds of judgements out of which
     six are mentioned here. (a) The categorical judgment
     unconditionally attributes a predicate to a subject, e.g., God is
     just. (b) The hypothetical judgement expresses some kind of
     dependence or a condition, e.g. If God is just, He will punish
     the wicked. (c) The disjunctive judgement gives different
     alternatives, ५.९. he is either a fool or a knave.       (ध) The
     problematic judgement gives only possibility, e.g. Mars may be
     inhabited. (€) Assertoric judgement expresses reality. e.g. The
     earth is round. (f) The apodictic judgment spells out necessity,
     e.g. God must be just.
52   Kant makes a distinction between Categorical, Hypothetical
     and Disjunctive judgments. In the first we consider only two
     concepts — substance and quality. A categorical judgment
     attributes a predicate to a subject. In the second or hypothetical
     judgment, a relation of ground and consequence is given. This
     judgment is an expression of the causal law. Kant gives an
     example: If there is perfect justice, the-abstinately wicked are
     punished. The hypothetical may be said to mark an advance



                                 169
Studies in Indian Philosophy


Bosanquet is not right in giving greater and greater reality from
categorical to hypothetical and from hypothetical to disjunctive.
Ultimately there    must be     a categorical basis for the hypo-
thetical   and the disjunctive.      Tattvamasi     is   a   categorical
proposition. Bosanquet puts a premium upon the knowledge of
conditions or upon doubt, which the Vedantin does not allow.53


      over the categorical, in that it asserts a logical sequence. In the
      third kind of judgment, namely the disjunctive, two or more
      alternatives are given. These alternatives exclude one another.
      There is logical opposition among these different alternatives.
      Again the alternatives taken together occupy the whole sphere
      of knowledge in question. Kant gives the example: The world
      exists either through blind chance, or through inner necessity,
      or through an external cause. The more the alternatives, the
      wider the sphere of knowledge. Taken together, the alternatives
      exhaust the sphere of knowledge at the moment. Thus the
      disjunctive judgment evidently steals a march over the first two
      types of jugments — namely categorical and hypothetical. See
      also, B. Bosanquet, Logic or The Morphology of Knowledge,
      Vol. I, Oxford, 1911, Chapter VI.
 53    Prof. Ranade gives a central place to the categorical judgment.
       Unless there is some positive basis,        mere expression     of
       conditions or putting forward various alternatives, does not
       take us far. The hypothetical may express a causal law; though
       it does not do so in every case. Again, it must be remembered
       that the causal law is only a postulate of science. It does not
       have the status of an axiom. The ‘ifs’ and the ‘in order to’s’
      make the statement of truth weak. Again, the various
      alternatives in the disjunctive proposition are only so many
      doubts. Theoretically there may be infinite alternatives. In order
      to assert one alternative, the host of other alternatives will have
      to be annulled. This being an almost impossible task, truth

(9) Spinoza said, Determination was Negation; the Jainas say

                                  Not—A is not—B

Inverse does not follow. Some non-A is non-B should be the
immediate inference. If Spinoza is right, Jainas are wrong.>4


     escapes. Only the categorical judgment possesses the
     adamantine certainty. Tattvamasi (Chandogya Upanisad, VI.
     8. 7) loses its Olympian height if it is reduced to hypothetical
     or disjunctive judgment. The fundamental unity of Self and God
     does not depend upon fulfilment of conditions as in the
     hypothetical judgment or elimination of doubts as in the
     disjunctive.

     symbolical expression itself would be right only if determination
     and indetermination (as also negation and Reality) are taken
     to be contradictory terms. Assuming for the sake of argument
     that they are, and assuming again that Spinoza is right, 10 us

(10) Relativism good only as a tentative stage. Relativism as a
necessary ancillary to Mysticism is what Gaudapada himself
would     teach.55        Cf.     Bradley:       blend    of    scepticism     and
mysticism.56 But to dissect relativism and mysticism is not


     Some indetermi-nation is reality. The upshot is that philosophy
     can not survive on the assumption that all knowledge is
     indeterminate or probable.
55   The day to day experience is relativistic. From this stand-point
     there is multiplicity. This relative experience is both affirmative
     and negative. In it there is affirmation of the world but the
     world exists only on the background of the non-dual Reality
     which is absolute. The relative experience again has a negative
     element as it is sublated on the realisation of the non-dual
     Brahman which is free from affirmation or negation. There is
     no    distinction          between    the    waking       state   and   dream
     (Gaudapada-karika, 111, 29); both are unreal. But a common
     man takes for granted the reality of the waking world; he
     experiences joy and suffers pain. At the same time he refutes
     the reality of the dream. This is because he has created a false
     relative   standard          for   himself   which    affirms     the   waking
     experience but denies the dream experience which is in fact
     unreal.    It   is   only    by the     development        of the   power   of
     discrimination that he learns to treat the waking experience
     also as unreal. It is from relativism that a man attains to the
     experience of non-duality. Relativism is thus a tentative stage,
     a training ground for mystically realising the ultimate Reality
     or Brahman. Only because man is caught in relativism, he seeks
     the Absolute.

     mysticism. His philosophy is sceptical in the sense that hc
                                                                 sets

                                                             of
     conceptions. He is against relativism and ruthlessly
                                                                         denies the

IL. Doctrine of Two Truths?’
Two degrees of truth: Vyavahara and Niscaya. Cf. two degrees
of reality in Nagarjuna: Samvrti-satya and Parinispanna-satya,
also two degrees of Reality in Sankara. The Atmapravada-piirva
which is certainly carlier than the Angas and therefore may
express the opinion of Mahavira himself,98 states two different
points of view, namely, those of Vyavahara and Niscaya,
common       sense   and philosophy, which correspond to           the
phenomenal and the noumenal. (1) From the common sense point
of view, the self does good or bad actions (Karta); from the
philosophical point ofview, it remains absolute in its own nature.
(2) In common sense, it enjoys the fruits ofits actions (Bhokta);



     tenability of the categories like space, time or the laws of
     causality and uniformity. He believes in the non-spatial, non-
     temporal Reality which is free from the reign of any empirical
     law. The ultimate Reality is known not by reason, but is got

     only in the immediate experience, where the individual is totally
     merged and dissolved in the Absolute. Here enters the mystical
     element in Bradley. Here also the attainment of the Absolute
     proceeds on denying the relativistic standpoint.

58   "According to the Jainas, there were originally two kinds of
     sacred books, the fourteen Pirvas and the eleven Angas. The
     Purvas continued to be transmitted for some time but were
     gradually lost. The works known as the eleven Angas are now
     the oldest parts of the existing Jaina canon.... The Digambaras,
     however, assert that these original works have all been lost, and
     that the present works which pass by the old names are
     spurious” (S.N. Das Gupta, A History of Indian Philosophy,
     Vol. I, P. 171).

in reality it enjoys nothing. (3) In common sense, it absorbs the

body: in reality it fills the whole world and is therefore called
Visnu (See Jaini).

V The Ethical Code ofJainism
(i) Five chief virtues: Ahimsa, Satya, Asteya, Brahmacarya and
Aparigraha. Close analogy with the virtues of the Bhagavad-gita.
The story of five grains of rice given by a father-in-law to his
four daughters-in-law. The first throws them away, the second
neglects them, the third preserves them and the fourth sows them
and propagates them (See Wintemitz, Vol. ग, P. 446).
Preservation and propagation of the Faith.64 (ii) Other virtues :
(1) Gandhi and the diseased calf. Unity oflife. "Thou art thyself
the animal thou wishest to kill" (See Winternitz, Vol. Il, P.


64   A merchant had four daughters-in-law. In order to put them
     to the test, he gives each of them five grains of rice with orders
     to preserve them carefully until he shall ask for them back
      again. The first daughter-in-law throws the grains away, and
      thinks to herself : "There are plenty of grains of rice in the
      larder, I shall give him others instead." The second thinks in
      the same way, and cats the grains. The third daughter-in-law
      preserves them carefully in her jewel-casket. But the fourth one
      plants the grains, and reaps; she again sows the harvest and
      reaps again, until at the end of five years she has accumulated
      a large store of rice. Then the merchant returns and punishes
      the first two daughters-in-law, assigning them the meanest tasks
      in the houschold; he entrusts the third one with the guarding
      of the entire property; but he gives the entire management of
      the large household into the hands of the fourth daughter-in-
      law. (These four women represent the monks some of whom
      do not keep the five great vows at all, others neglect them, the
      better ones observe them conscientiously, but the best of whom
      are not content with observing them, but propagate them also).

      (See M. Winternitz, A History of Indian Literature, Vol. I,
      translated from the original German by Mrs. S. Ketkar and
      Miss H. Khon, B.A., University of Calcutta, 1934, P. 446).

65   "You yourself are the (being) which you intend to kill; you
     yourself are the (being) which you intend to ill-use;.... Therefore
     the righteous one, who has awakened to this knowledge, and
     lives according to it, will neither kill nor cause to kill’, Ayaranga-

     of Indian Literature, Vol. II, P. 436).
     We may quote here an expcrience of a disciple of Prof. Ranade
     as it was narrated to us. The disciple writes, "It was about 1955.
     I had a passion for hunting. My friends tried to dissuade me
     from that habit, but I did not listen. One day I had a dream
     where I had killed a deer. I was holding the animal by its leg
     and was reaching for a knife to cut the creature. As I turned
     my face for getting hold of the knife, I felt that the animal was
     moving. I directed my gaze towards the deer and lo, there was
     no deer. Instead it was my son. Words came out from his mouth,
     ‘Of, father, what you are doing?’ I was stunned. It was a
     revelation for me. Never again did I go a-hunting. By God’s
     grace, in a fraction of a second, I left hunting for good".

 85   Samvara is total obscuration while Bandha is partial obscura-
      tion. But it is obscuration nonetheless, It is an attack of Karma

 86   The second, the third and the fourth stages in the process of
      the liberation of the soul are: Bandha, Samvara and Nirjara. In
      Bandha there is influx of Karma in the soul; in Samvara the
      influx of fresh Karma is stopped and the Jiva is completely
      obscured by Karmic matter which has already infiltrated. In
      Nirjara the shedding of Karmas begins. These stages can be
      compared with the three stages, namely, penumbra, umbra and
      again penumbra of the solar eclipse. In the first, light is partially
      obscured; in the second there is perfect darkness or obscuration
      when no light is received from the sun. In the third light begins
      to appear.

propound the Truth during their life-time, which is a higher thing.
The Tirthankaras are Asamanya-siddhas; the others are
Samanya-siddhas. The Tirthankaras, however are like a society
of gods (Me Taggart).89 The Holy Malli, a Tirthankarini. They
sit on the Siddhasilatala. Tirthankaras limited? Probably not.90
It is possible for every man to reach the highest state or divinity.
The Tirthankara is the only lone being in a world of possibles.91

                         CHAPTER V

A CRITICAL ESTIMATE OF NYAYA-VAISESIKA
I. Reduction of all Categories (Vaisesika) to One

1. (i) History of the doctrine of the categories. Hegel’st


1   A category is a class. This view is shared byAristotle, Kant and

    Hegel. "The term ‘category’ means literally ‘predicate’. Its most
    gencral signification — common to Aristotle, Kant and Hegel
    alike — is ‘fundamentally important class’. The main difference
    between Kant and Aristotle in their enumeration of categories
    is to be found in the fact that Kant starts from the subjective
    side, considering the categories first as forms of conscious
    judging, whereas Aristotle — herein followed by Hegel —regards
    the categories as relations of the objects of knowledge" (M.W.
    Calkins,   The     Persistent      Problems    of   Philosophy,     The
    Macmillan Company, London, 1921, Pp. 525-526). Put simply,
    it means that under category, Kant discussed the relation of
    mind to objects, whereas Aristotle and Hegel discussed the
    relation   of    objects     to    one   another.   The    former      is
    epistemological while the latter is ontological.
    "Categories,    especially    in   the   Aristotelian   sense,   are   a
    classification of predicates only, and not an enumeration of all
    namable things (including both subjects and predicates) as the
    Padarthas
          of the Vaisesikasare. In this respect, the classification
    of the Vaisesikas is superior to that of Aristotle. The latter
    enumerated ten categories, 1. Substance, 2. Quantity, 3. Quality,
    4. Relation, 5. Place. 6. Time, 7. Posture, 8. Appurtenance or
    Property, 9. Activity and 10. Passivity" (Athalye and Bodas,
    Tarka-sangraha of Annambhatta, P. 73).
    According to Hegel, categories "............ are not only modes of
    thinking things; they are the modes of being of the things
    themselves. They are not empty frames, which receive their
Studies in Indian Philosophy




     contents from without; they are substantial forms, as the Middle
     Ages used to say; they give themselves their own content; they
     are creative acts of divine and human reason. They are both
     the forms which mould my thought and the stages of eternal
     creation" (Weber and Perry, History of Philosophy, Charles
     Scribner’s Sons, New York, 1925, P. 407).
     According to Kant, a category is a pure concept without any
     empirical content. A crow as a concept is a category. It docs
     not refer to any particular crow, but crow as such. Categories
     are connective concepts. Kant recognises twelve categories.
     Traditional Logic considers propositions under four heads —
     Quantity, Quality, Relation and Modality, each having three
     sub-heads. Thus there are twelve forms of judgement. Taking
     this table as a guide, Kant gave twelve categories. They are:
     1    Under Quantity — Unity, Plurality, Totality;
     1]   Under Quality — Reality, Negation, Limitation;
     1    Under Relation — Subsistence and Inherence (Substance
     and Accident), Causality and Dependence (Cause and Effect),
     Community (Reciprocity between the Active and the Passive);
     1४    Under Modality — Possibility-Impossibility, Existence-
     Non-existence, Necessity-Contingency.
      Categories are results of mind’s activity unlike a sensation
     where the mind is passive. We have knowledge when we get
      data from the external world and systematise the data by
      contributing something from the mind. This contribution of the
      mind to the knowledge-process is a category.
     The aim of the Upanisadic seers was to find out the ultimate
      existence or the ultimate category under which all things were
     subsumed. Prof. Ranade has traced the development of the
     concept    through the Upanisads.    Cosmological     categories,
     namely, elements like fire or water were supposed to be
     fundamental. Finding them wanting, physiological categories



                                 188
                                                            Nyaya-vaisesika


categories. Kanada recognises only six, and does not recognise
Abhava.2     Abhava comes to be added later 07.3 (ii) There are
four kinds of Abhavas: (a) Pragabhava, anterior negation which
implies    anterior     eternity.4 (b)      Pradhvansabhava,         posterior
negation which implies posterior eternity.) The mecting point
of prior eternity and posterior eternity is the "specious present".6


     like the eye or the ear were substituted. These made way to
     psychological categories like consciousness and ultimately to
     God or Atman (See A Constructive Survey of Upanishadic
     Philosophy, Pp. 181 ff).
     According to Vaisesika-sttra I.1.4, the six categories recognised
     by Kanada are: (1) Dravya (substance), (2) Guna (quality), (3)
     Karma        (action),   (4)   Samanya    (Universal),        (5)   Visesa
     (individual)     and     (6)   Samavaya   (inherence     or     necessary
     relation).
     The seventh category, namely, Abhava (non-existence) was
     added by commentators. Nyaya recognises 16 categories. Their
     second category viz. प्रमेय 07 ‘Knowables’ includes all the seven
     categories of the वैशेषि क5,
     प्रागभाव means negation of a thing before it comes into existence.
     This negation has. anterior eternity in the sense that it is
     beginningless; this negation was always there till the thing came
     into existence. But it comes to an end as soon as the thing is
     created.
     प्रध्वंसाभाव means negation of a thing afier the thing is destroyed.
     It is posterior negation; it has posterior eternity; for, the non-
     existence of a thing after it is destroyed, is endless.
     Prior eternity comes to an end when the thing comes into
     existence. Posterior eternity starts after the thing is destroyed.
     Obviously their meeting place is the duration of the thing’s
     existence. This duration may be either short or long; its span
     can be as small as a mere moment; but even then it has some

11   If अभाव is absolutely nothing, it can not imply any thing what-
     soever. The very fact that it implies prior and posterior eternity,
     means that it has some capacity and is of some positive nature.
12   For Bergson pure negation is unimaginable and inconceivable.
     Reality does not mean ‘that which exists but which may as well
     not exist’. The absence of reality is not nothingness, but
     presence of some other reality. Such a positive interpretation
     of negation has a pragmatic value. It has solved many a problem
     in philosophy. It has done away with the dualism between
     positive and negative existence as all existence is now only
     positive. As void is dismissed, problems like creation out of
     nothing or existence of formless matter do not remain at all.
     (See H.W. Carr, Henri Bergson, T.C. and E.C. Jack, Ltd.,
     London and Edinburgh, 1919, Pp, 69-74).

(2) An indefinite regress in ViSesas. "Qualitative" distinction
between atoms of the different elements. Visesas among atoms
even of the same clement (Svatovyavartaka).13 Consequent
merger of Vaisesika in Nyaya. Contrast Jainism, where all atoms
of any element whatsoever are always alike.14 The particular
and the general together constitute the Definition of Aristotle.15
(3) (1) The Nyaya conception of Samanya attacked by Vedantins,

carya : Divisible or plural?!6 (ii) The Vaisesikas retort by saying



13    Atoms of different elements are qualitatively different. An
      air-atom will never be a water-atom. But how to distinguish
      between one water-atom and another water-atom? Here the
      Vaisesikas say that it is the Visesa which distinguishes them.
      Now the question arises how to distinguish between the Visesa
      of one atom and the Visesa of another atom. Logically we have
      to posit additional Visesas to do that job, and this will lead to
      an idefinite regress. The Vaisesikas, however try to escape from
      the impasse by holding that the Viscsas 976 स्वतोव्यावर्तक, 1.७.
      they differentiate themselves from other Visesas. A वि शेष
      performs a two-fold function of distinguishing an eternal
      substance from all others as well as distinguishing itself from
      other वि शेष. We may observe that if two वि शेषऽ can be self-
      differentiating, why not two different atoms of the same element
      themselves be so? See also Foot Note 2 in Chapter IV.

characteristic and (b) a class concept (Jati). In the first, it is a
correlative of Visesas, involving quality; in the second, it is a
correlative of Vyakti involving number.20

     distinguished from all other घटऽ. So Samanya is a universal

     घट. So ina    single घट, both the Samanya and the Visesa reside,
     the   former    as   a   general   character     and   the   latter   as   a
     distinguishing mark. The difference between them refers to
     characteristics and is obviously only qualitative. As per the
     second meaning, Samanya is Jati or a class-concept, and serves
     as a distinguishing character of a group of things from other
     groups. घटत्व as a Jati distinguishes all घटऽ from Ys or from
     वृक्षऽ and so on. It stands for the whole group comprising of a
     number of individuals who can be counted as different Afths.

brought together. The relation of above-ness is in the book and
that of below-ness is in the table; they can not be brought together.
Infinite regress in these cases also. To relate relata and the relation
between them, new relations must be found out, and so on ad

27   Objects are in Akaga or space. The relation between objects
     and 61258 is संयोग. Objects may disappear; but आकाश remains.
     So is the relation between Atman and body.
28   (i) संयुक्त-समवाय is contact-cum-inherence. In the cognition of
     entities like qualities, the contact is of the form of संयुक्त-समवाय.
     For example when I see a red rose, the rose is cognised by a
     simple contact (संयोग) with the rose. But the quality redness is
     cognised as inhering in the rose. (ii) In the cognition of a quality
     of a quality (for example, cognition of pleasantness of the
     redness and redness of a rose where pleasantness inheres in
     the redness and redness inheres in the rose) the contact is of
     the nature of संयुक्त-समवेत-समवाय, 1.९., contact-cum-inherence-
     cum-inherence. (iii) The cognition of sound is of the nature of
     समवेत समवाय i.c., inherence-cum-inherence. Sound is a quality
     and is congnised only through inherence. But Akaga, though a
     substance, is supposed to be cognised not through contact of
     the ear with Akasa, but only through inherence. Hence the
      cognition of sound is through inherence-cum-inherence. (See
     The Cultural Heritage of India, Vol. III, Ramakrishna Mission,
      Calcutta, 1969, P. 600).
      All this shows how simple facts are made complicated.
      The combination 0 संयुक्त and समवाय in different things, takes
      different forms and can be extended ad infinitum. Again, it is
      not known why Akasa is supposed to be cognised not through
      direct contact but through inherence. Akasa being a Dravya,
      should be known through simple contact. If light waves are
      directly contacted, why not sound waves?

29   The Absolute transcends all relations. Relations like time and

 ,   applied to the Absolute (See Foot Note 26 above).
30   Citsukha, a post-Sankara Advaitin, refutes Nydya-Vaisesika
     categories and establishes that Brahman alone is the ultimate
     Reality. Time and space are not real as they are only relative.
     Atoms are a contradiction because the process of self-division
     can not stop. The universal can not be proved either by
     perception or by inference.
31   ‘By substance I understand that which is in itself and is
     conceived by means of itself, i.c., that the conception of which
     can be formed without the aid of the conception of any other
     thing.’ Thus runs the third Definition in Spinoza’s Ethics.
     Independence is the essence of substantiality and so the
     substance does not require anything either for its being or for
     its knowledge.

34    Sriharsa’s खंडनखंडखाद्य aims at establishing the pure self-shining
      consciousness (Brahman) as the only reality by refuting ali
      arguments put forward by the Naiyayikas and the Vaisesikas to
      support   the   reality of the   Pramanas (the   means   of valid
      cognition) and the Prameyas (the objects of valid experience).
      Sriharsa attempts to prove that all empirical experiences and
      their objects are but conventional relative truths, having no
      ultimate reality in them. This negative method of destructive
      criticism (Khandana), originally started by nihilistic Buddhist
      philosophers like Nagarjuna, Candrakirti and others, was first
      avowedly applied by Sriharsa in the field of Advaita Vedanta
      and was followed by Citsukha, Anandagiri and others” (See,
      The Cultural Heritage of India, Vol. ता, ए. 271).

(1) The three-fold definition of Karana as Samavayi, Asamavayi
and Nimitta, not tenable.       The     Samavayi   and    the   Nimitta
compared to Aristotle’s material and efficient causes. As the
formal or the final cause ofAristotle is not a real cause, similarly
the Asamavayi in Nyaya philosophy is not a cause.47 The Final
cause useless in Science — Bacon.48 As illustrations of Samavayi
or the material cause we have earth for pot, and atoms for the
world   (the   word Samavaya       has    got   nothing   to    do   with
Samavayi),49     and as illustrations of Nimitta or the efficient
cause, the rod, the wheel and the man. Also the ass. The ass
plays such an important part in Indian Logic.59 Nimitta = exciting



 47   The तंतुसंयोग 15 said to be the असमवायि कारण of पट. It roughly
      corresponds to the shape or plan of the cloth and is comparable
      to the formal cause of Aristotle. Both the final and the formal
      causes can be dispensed with. So too the असमवायि कारण of the
      Naiyayika (See Foot Note 52 below).
 48   Bacon was an empiricist to the core, and recommended the
      methods of observation and experiment in science. The habit
      of human mind to attribute purposes or final causes to nature,
      was decried by him. He called it an idol or a prejudice of the
      mind. This particular idol of attributing final causes, is named
      Idol of the Tribe. It results in anthropo-morphism, i.c. applying
      human qualities and motives to the non-human entities.
 49   See Foot Note 52 below.
 50   There is a plurality of instrumental or efficient causes in Nyaya.
      For a pot, earth is the material cause; rod and wheel constitute
      the efficient causes; so docs the potter. The ass bringing the
      clay, too, becomes an efficient cause; the potter’s father
      becomes an efficient cause and so on. (This account we get

52   Neither Guna (quality) nor Karma (motion) can serve as a
     cause because, as per the Nyaya theory, they can not exist
     without Dravya (substance). Far less, then, can they operate
     independently. Obviously here Dravya becomes the real cause.
     But Dravya is said to be the Samavayi karana in Nyaya. The
     Asamavayi karana is thus reduced to Samavayi karana. It is
     important to remember that Asamavayi in this context does not
     mean    ‘not-samavayi’   (non-intimate),   or that    which   is   not
     connected by Samavaya relation. It means something else. The
     Naiyayika classifies causes into separable and inseparable ones.
     The former are instrumental causes. The latter are divided into
     material and non-material. Ofthese, the material cause समवायि

     are present and which is absolutely indispensable for the
     production of the effect. The original notion of a करण scems to
     be to find out the cause par excellence. There are many causes

     special designation, करण. The distinction between कारण and

58    The preceminance of the करण is said to consist in its activily or

      contact of the axe with the wood is Vyapara or process. Here
      the process itself becomes the cause (Ibid. P. 37; Pp. 186—187).

II Proofs for God’s Existence in Nyaya
(1) Cosmological Proof. (a) All production requires perception
of materials by agent. God so required for perceiving the
supersensuous atoms. (b) God is required for the original motion:
Prime    Mover.       (c)    God    is   required    for creating the first
binary    compound: 2!             Why       not    bring     in    God       in
between       all   stages     from      2! to 71263 Occasionalism.64



62   Indriya is the करण 0! प्रत्यक्ष (तर्कसंग्रह Section 43). Paramarsa is
     the करण of अनुमान (तर्कसंग्रह Section 44). साइश्यज्ञान 15 the करण of
     उपमान (तर्कसंग्रह Section 58) and शब्द is the करण of शाब्दज्ञान (तर्कसंग्रह
     Section 63) (See, Athalye and Bods, Tarka-sangraha of
     Annambhatta, P. 190 and P. 348).
63   God is brought in to create a binary compound, i.e., 2! (Factorial
     2). 2! = 2x 1 or the binary composed of two atoms. The
     argument refers to the stage of creating the binary compound.
     But creation does not stop at the binary compound or aT Fs;
     it goes on (0 त्र्यणुक5, चतुरणुकऽ and so on. Stages between 2! and
     n! are: 1x 2x3>x 4.... x (1-1) x n. Why not bring God for the
     creation of all these later stages? That amounts to bringing in
     God for every act of creation, and this would result in
     occasionalism.
64    "Occasionalism denotes the theory of occasional causes... It
     is God who, "on the occasion" of the physical motion (of the
     air and nerves) produces the sensation (of sound), and “at the
     instance" of the determination of the will, produces the
     movement of the arms" (R. Falckenberg, History of Modern
     Philosophy, P. 111). God intervenes on every occasion of
     interaction between mind and body. He is the necessary and
     only mediator between matter and soul. This is the view advo-
     cated by Geulincx and Malebranche in Western philosophy.

IV. Nyaya Doctrine of Epigenesis or Qualitative Novelty
(1) General Theory of Epigenesis. The Arambhavada or the
 Asatkaryavada of the Naiyayikas is a doctrine of epigenesis or
 new creation. The Parinadmavada or the Satkaryavada of the
 Sankhyas is the doctrine of equilibration or evolution.83 The
 Naiyayikas suppose that the effect was not present formerly,
 viz., in the cause; hence epigenesis. The Sankhyas suppose that
 the effect was present formerly, viz.,              in   the cause;     hence
 equilibration.

 (2) Epigenesis in Atomism. (i) Differences of quality among
 atoms. Nyaya philosophy lays stress on differences of quality
 between atoms, not merely of the different elements, but even
 of the same element, not so Jainism which recognises only


       translation with 5101165 in brief and notes, by Narada Thera,
       published by Buddhist Missionary Society, Kaulalumpur, III
       Edition, 1978).
 80    See Foot Note 167 in Chapter VI.
 81    योगज प्रत्यक्ष 15 a variety of अलौकि क प्रत्यक्ष or super-sensuous
       perception.
 82    Sce Section IV in Chapter IV.
 83    See Foot Notes 38-39 in Chapter I.

                                                        Nyaya-vaisesika


differences of quantity. To use another terminology, Nyaya
considers the differences as physical, Jainism considers the
differences as mathematical. Physical differences or differences
of quality are epigenetic in character. (ii) Formation of ays.
It is strange how from the अणु which, according to the Naiyayikas,
enjoys only infinitesimality (पारि मांडल्य) or a mere ideal content,
8 दुव्यणुक should ever be formed. The rise of 16 दुव्यणुक is an
illustration of epigenesis.84 (iii) Factorial n, an epigenetic
product. The general formula for successive atomistic formations
or patterns is:     1x2x3....xnorn! The दुव्यणुक५, the A
                                                      7s, the
चतुरणुक5 and the rest are all epigenetic formations as they involve
the process of multiplication and not of summation.85 The
Mimamsa differs from Nyaya in regarding the त्यणुक as the
minimum visible (minimum perceptible) and not 116 दव्सणुक as



84    The dimension of an atom is अणुत्व (infinitesimality); that of the
      binary or FAY is also अणुत्व. The अणुत्व of an atom has 8
      special name पारि मांडल्य, which is not applied to the binary. From
      three binaries when compounded, arises the Tryanuka or the
      tertiary. This last is the minimum visible particle. Now the
      binarics have अणुत्व or subtleness and from subtleness, no
      magnitude can ever arise. The Nyaya argument is that
      magnitude is a result of three factors बहुत्व (number), महत्त्व
     ` (size)   and प्रचय (arrangement), number being the most
      important. So the cause of the magnitude in a AF is not the
      अणुत्व or subtleness in the <4, but their number. This makes
      it clear that magnitude does not come out of subtleness; for, it
      is a different species and it must be a new product. Thus does
      the Naiyayika harp on new creation or epigenesis.
85    All genesis is multiplication; when things are just put together
      it is summation. When they interact, it is multiplication. At such
      times, there is a new product, 1.6. it is epigenesis.




                                   213
Studies in Indian Philosophy


the Nyaya supposes.86
(3) Epigenesis in Generation: (i) Generation of a product.
Generation, destruction. The doctrine of momentary existence
of the Buddhists is a hobgoblin87 to the Indian logicians. Two
moments instead of one. Momentary existence according to them
can   explain    neither generation     nor   destruction.   For   both
generation and destruction, more than one moment is necessary.
Thus they hold that the Ghata comes into existence one moment
after the Kapalas are united, thus involving epigenesis; also they
hold that the Ghata is destroyed one moment after the Kap4las
are destroyed.
Criticism. This latter doctrine as applied to an organism would
be manifestly absurd because the cells would have to die first
before the body dies. Now it is a fact of experience that the cells
 live even after the body dies. The most that we can say is that
 the body and the cells die together. Also the action of a
 decapitated frog or a decapitated warrior shows that the parts
 are active even though the whole may be dead, e.g., Murarbaji
 Deshpande. (11) Emergence ofqualities. The Indian logicians are
 not content with explaining generation and destruction, only as
 occupying more than a moment’s span. They say that every
 product, e.g., a pitcher at the moment ofproduction, is a neutral
 entity, and that qualities, e.g., red colour in the pitcher or smell
 in the rose, emerge later, say, in the second moment, and which

 (4) Epigenesis in Causation. We have also the doctrine of
` epigenesis in the concept of the Asamavayi karana ofthe Indian

                                 is unique and sui generis. As
  compared with the Tantu-nipas, therefore, the Pata-riipa is an
  epigenetic formation.92

  In Prabhakara, the soul is always Jada. In Ny@ya the soul is Jada
  in Susupti, but in Jagrti it gets the attribute of knowledge.
  Knowledge is thus, according to the Naiyayikas, adventitious; a
  quality of the self (Cf. Buddhi in the चतुर्वि शति गुण५)93 and not its

V. Nyaya Criticism of Buddhism
(1) (i) Vatsyayana criticises the Buddhistic Relativism in his
Bhasya on IV. 1. 39 and IV. 1. 40.94 He shows that relativism
is self-contradictory. The long and the short must be regarded
as self-existent; otherwise no relation can be established between
them, and relativism would be impossible. (ii) He criticises the
Buddhist doctrine of Vijiiana or knowledge alone as real, in his
Bhasya on IV. 2. 26 and IV. 2. 27. He tells us that the doctrine
is self-destructive, in as much as the Buddhist, in separating
things from thoughts, is giving reality to them. (iii) The Buddhist
doctrine of momentariness is criticised in his Bhasya on III. 2.
11. We must always suppose that there is a connecting link
between the origination of an entity and its cessation.
(2) Udyotakara, in his Nyaya-vartika, III. 1.1, criticises the
Buddhist doctrine of soul-less-ness. He tells us that the Bhadanta
teaches that there is a burden and there is a burden-bearer. The
five Skandhas are the burden, and the burden-bearer must,
therefore, be the soul.       (ii)     In his Vartika on the Nyaya-sutra.

I. 1. 37 Udyotakara criticises a Buddhist logician who says that
the middle term and the major term co-abide in a syllogism.
Udyotakara passes two criticisms: (a) How can a Buddhist, a
holder of the doctrine of momentariness, teach the doctrine of
co-abidance and (b) if we take so much a syllogism as Sabdah
anityah   krtakatvat    ghatatvat,   Anityatva   which    designates
posterior non-eternity, and krtakatva which designates prior
non-eternity will have to co-abide in the Ghata which according
to the Buddhists exists for a moment.95

(3) Udyayana’s Kusumafijali is devoted to the refutation of
Kalyana Raksita’s ISvara-bhanga-karika.           Udayana     in    1115
 Atma-tattva-viveka refutes the four Buddhist theories:             (i)
 Ksanabhanga, (ii) Bahyartha-bhanga, (111) Guna-guni-bheda-
 bhanga, and (iv) Anupalamba. The last doctrine tells us that a
 substance exists in relation to its qualities only, and the qualities
 exist in relation to the former. In this net-work of relations which
 constitutes the veil of Samvrti, the world is verily void, says the
 Buddhist.
 (4) Jayanta, in his Nyaya-mafijari, says that the Buddhists hold


 95   The fact that the Ghata exists at the present moment implies
      that there is at least one moment prior to its creation. This is
      prior non-eternity. The word krtakatva or production implies
      this prior non-eternity, or non-existence before creation.
      Similarly, the word Anityatva or      perishability implies    15
      posterior non-eternity. It will be destroyed and will not exist
      thereafter. If Krtakatva and Anityatva are to be applied to one
      and the same Ghata, it will have to be related at least to three
      moments: one moment of prior non-eternity, one moment of

      existence and one moment of posterior non-eternity. This
      contradicts the Buddhist contention that the Ghata exists only
      for one moment.

VI. Doctrine of Perception

(1) The doctrine of atomicity of sense, also this sense exists at
the tip of the respective sense-organ, नासाग्रवर्ती27 and जि ह्नाग्रवर्ती 20
ete. (अणुपरि माणमि ंद्रि यम्|‌ अतींद्रि यम्‌).

(2) Perception requires actual contact between sense and object.
This is to be effected by the further artifice of the motion in
sense-organ. Doctrine of three-fold contact; contact between soul
and mind, mind and sense, and sense and object.99 This is a
truly crass materialistic conception. (Cf. Windelband).100

(3) 0812658 criticises this ortginal doctrine of perception, saying
that it is both too wide and too narrow. It is too wide because it
includes recollection, which is the contact between the mind and
the image; and too narrow because it excludes perception by
God. Has God, for example, mind and the senses? The question
whether God perceives or not, can be solved by the Naiyayikas
only by taking an anthropomorphic view of God.

(4) With this Naiyayika theory of perception, we may contrast
the Vedantic theory as well as the Buddhistic theory. The
 Buddhist theory of perception is presentationistic in the extreme,
 so their theory of perception consists in the succession of one
 presentation by another, not involving the mind at all.101 The
 Nalyayika’s theory involves mind, but states that, perception
 consists in the physical contact of the mind and the object through
 the   senses.   The Vedantic     theory   takes   a   via   media,    the
 अंत: करणवृत्ति , taking the form of the object, and it is this form
 which is known (cf. Dharmarajadhvarindra).102               The   present




       mechanical motion. Matter in its form and motion, is regarded
       as the true reality. With such ideas, the system of Democritus
       assumes the character of conscious, outspoken materialism.

Studies in Indian Philosophy


with their realistic bias fall into the mistake of supposing that
there is a ‘perception of the Universal’. Dinnaga had said that
perception concerns itself with the individual and not with the
universal. The Naiyayikas answer that we must be supposed to
perceive the universal, otherwise we can not communicate it to
our fellow men (cf. Russel and his doctrine of the perception of
the universal).106 Stout’s doctrine takes, however, a via media
between idealism and realism.107 All categories of the
understanding are latent in sense, and there is only a difference
of degree and not of kind. This is a doctrine to which the
Naiyayikas should have subscribed in respect oftheir distinction
 between sensation and perception.

(7) GangeSa however tries to evade the difficulty by making a

we have an associational perception of fragrance, when we see
a piece of sandal-wood; according to the third, we have an
intuitional perception of the supreme Being, of atoms, of duty,
of things past and future, and so on. In this last doctrine of Yogaja

 VII. Judgment, Inference and Induction
 (1) The Naiyayikas are scientists and realists and not idealists.
 Hence they start with perception and conception and not with
 judgment as the unit of thought. Jayanta alone ofIndian logicians,
 regards the sentence or the judgment as the beginning of all
 thought ; ‘words’ he says, ‘are parts of a sentence’. This is a
 position very similar to that of Bosanquct who regards the judg-


      They hold another peculiar view regarding the apprehension
      of all cows — past, present and future. "...with the aid of this
      knowledge of the universal cowness we are able to apprehend
      directly,.... but in a transcendental manner all the particular
      cows that exist now or ever existed or are going to exist, though
      only as belonging to that class. This knowledge of all the
      particulars falling under a universal... is regarded as a case of
      Alaukika Pratyaksa." (Ibid. Pp. 249-250).
 111 The योगज प्रत्यक्ष which is necessarily अलौकि क is nothing but the
      अपरोक्षानुभूति   (intuitive apprehension) of the Vedantins.



                                    224
                                                                Nyaya-vaisesika


ment as the unit of all thought.112 As against the position of
Bosanquet, one might ask, why we might not start with the
percept or concept on the one hand, or with inference on the
other? Jayanta alone takes the idealistic view of judgment. His
theory is like that of the Anvitabhidhana-vadins, who take a
representational view, as against the Abhihitanvaya-vadins, who
take a presentational view. The first view depends upon meaning,
the second upon fact.113

(2) Nature of inference. We can not understand the nature of
inference in Nyaya without contrasting it with that of the
Buddhists or of Bosanquet. (i) In the first place, inference as
with Dinnaga, moves from particular to particular, and yet
Dinnaga feels the necessity of taking recourse to the law of
causation. Herein he commits the same fallacy as Mill, who says
that   inference       moves     from particular to particular and yet
recognises the law of causation. Causality among particulars is
a contradiction in terms. Hume was thus more consistent than

Mill for he recognises the particulars but denied the law of
causation.!14        (ii)   Bosanquet          also   denies   causation,   but


112 See Foot Note 36 in Chapter VI.

113 For अभि हि तान्वयवाद words have independent meaning. They
       refer to facts directly and so the view is presentationism. Words
       are   uttered    together    in   a sentence. This view is     held by

       Kumirila. It implies realism. The contrary opinion 15 that words
       convey meanings not directly but only when joined in asentence.
       This is indirect or representational view. There is no reference
       to facts. This is अन्वि   ताभि धानवाद of the idealists. It is advocated
       by Prabhakara.
114 For Mill, inference is from particular to particular and the

       intervention of the universal is not necessary. Now causation
       is a universal law and need not have any place in Mill’s thought.

substitutes the law of ground and consequent, as the basis of
inference. No time element is involved in inference.115 When
Reality is the subject of all judgments, there is no reason why
causality should exist.116 (iii) The Naiyayikas, on the other
hand, recognise causation. They, however, over-hit the mark in
that, they recognise all cases of inseparable accident also as cases
of causation (for example, the green colour of parrots). It is only
our ignorance ofthe real nature of things which makes us suppose
that there is anything like accident.117


     But inconsistently enough, he recognises the law of causation.
     In fact his definition of a cause stands as a point of reference
     in the history of Logic. Causation is a universal relation and to
     take recourse to it, in the world of particulars, is contradictory.
     Hume also opted for particulars, but does not recognise any
      universal relation like causality; he is here consistent, though
     wrong. ‘Two things are conjoined, but not connected’; ‘there is
      sequence but no consequence’ are his favourite utterances.
 115 In causality, there is     a necessary reference to         time. The

      Mill’s adjective ‘antecedent’ are enough to prove this. Time is
      always associated with particulars. This cow means the cow
      which I see ‘now’. Cowness on the other hand is universal and
      is above time. The basis of a rope-serpent-illusion is the rope;
      there is no question of time reference, for the rope serves as
      the ground (अधि ष्ठान) of the serpent for all time. The relation is
      timeless as time has no function when ground and consequent
      are substituted for     cause   and   effect.   Here   time    is   non-
      operational and withers away.

(3) Nature of Induction. The chief problem is — how do we arrive
at scientific laws, how is it that we are able to formulate the
major premise? What, in short, is the ‘Vyaptigrahopaya’ or the
means of attainment of the knowledge of induction, as Gangesa
puts it. (i) According to the Buddhists, there can be no scientific
induction as logic deals merely with perception. Inference, they
take as invalid. As against this, GangeSa tells us that those who
try to prove the invalidity of inference can not do so without
themselves invoking the aid of inference.118 Mere percepts
without concepts are beads without a thread. (ii) Udyotakara, an
earliar logician, however, says that the Vyaptigrahopaya can be
explained by the combination of perception and remembrance:
perception of the minor term, and remembrance of the concomit-
ance of the major term and the middle.119 (iii) Even against`

       question is — how can you have memory of the concomitance
       unless you have previous knowledge of it? In all subsequent

123 Tarka-sangraha, Section 56. A condition or उपाधि          is defined as
     that which is greater in extent than the thing to be proved (साध्य)
     but is not more extensive than the reason (साधन). (See Athalye
     and Bodas, Tarka-sangraha of Annambhatta, P. 116). Unless
     the condition is fulfilled, the conclusion is not valid. The topic
     of Saf comes in, in the discussion of fallacies. For this purpose

      always give rise to smoke, 1.७. does not pervade smoke (साध्य).
      It gives rise to smoke on the fulfilment of an extra condition,

      gives rise to smoke, so it pervades smoke धूमव्यापक,               i.c.,

      for the presence of fire which can exist in an iron ball without

      साधनाव्यापक;    hence the definition     of a   condition    as   more

      extensive than साध्य (साध्यव्यापकत्वे सति ) but less extensive than
      साधन (साधनव्यापकत्व). Wet fuel, which is a condition, pervades
      smoke but does not pervade fire; and so presence of fire does
      not assure us of presence of smoke. Such a          syllogism which
      takes recourse to a condition is fallacious because the usual
      procedure of pervading the साध्य ७४ हेतु 15 not observed and so

124 असि द्ध    is   inconclusive      or   unproved reason. The            fallacy

      in either of the three terms of the syllogism, viz., पक्ष (minor

      (i) आश्रयासि द्ध - the fallacy when the middle term is not present
      in the minor term, e.g.
           All lotuses are fragrant.
           Sky-lotus is a lotus.
          Sky-lotus is fragrant.
      Here the middle term, lotus, is not present in the sky-lotus at
      all, for there exists nothing like a sky-lotus. So the conclusion
      is wrong. (ii) स्वरूपासि द्ध is absence of association between the
      middle term and the minor term.
           Where there is smoke there is fire.
           There is smoke in the lake.
          There is fire in the lake.
      Here, there is absence of smoke in the lake, and it makes the
     conclusion, incorrect. (17) व्याप्यत्वासि द्ध, Here the middle term
     is not invariably associated with the major term.

           The mountain has golden smoke.
           The mountain has fire.
     There is no invariable association between golden smoke and
     fire. Therefore, the conclusion is wrong.

125 The Joint Method of Agreement and Difference is the third
      method mentioned by Mill. It is the combination of the first
      two methods, viz., the Method of Agreement and the Method
      of Difference. If all people who ate stale and fermented sweets
      fell ill and none of those who did not eat stale and fermented
      sweets fell ill, sweets are likely to be the cause of illness. If a
      causal connection is thus established, there can be inductive
      generalisation, namely, cating stale and fermented sweets leads
      to illness (See Foot Note 120 above.)

7. Problem of Validity in Nyaya and Mimamsa
(a) The 1५६५४ theory of knowledge is known as Paratah-
pramanya,127 while the Mimamsa theory of knowledge is known
as Svatah-pramanya. The Nyaya theory of error is known as
Anyatha-khyati, while there are two Mimamsa theories of error,
the Akhyati of Prabhakara and the Viparita-khyati (which is
almost equivalent to the Anyatha-khyati of Nyaya) of Kumarila.
The difference between the two Mimansa {11607168 of error, viz.,
the Akhyati and the Viparita-khyati, we shall notice under
"Prabhakara and Kuméarila" in the next chapter).128 At present
we   shall   consider the    Mimamsa      and   Nydya     theorics   of
knowledge, viz., Svatah-pramanya and Paratah-pramanya, as
well as the very famous discussion by Vdacaspati, of the five
 theories of error (See S.C. Vidyabhusan, History of Indian Logic,
 Pp. 138-139). The five theories of error here enumerated are:—
 (i) The Atma-khyati of the Yogicara Buddhists (Vi{77118 ४4115),
 (ii) The Asat-khyati of the Madhyamika Buddhists (Siinya-



      preparation, incubation, illumination and verification (See R.
      9. Woodworth, Experimental Psychology, Methuen & Co. Ltd.,
      London, Reprinted 1951, Pp. 818 ff). In the first, data are
      collected; in the second, there is conscious work or even
      unconscious work (work going on in the brain inspite of the
      subject not being in the centre of attention); in the third, there
      is illumination or inspiration and in the fourth, hypotheses put
      forward are verified. It is the third stage of inspiration which
      is akin to intuition. Rational work precedes it, but the actual
      spark is not explained by reason. This flash leads to inductive
      inference.
 127 Svatah-pramanya means self-proved. Paratah-pramanya means

(5) (i) Corresponding to the Svatah-pramanya and Paratah-
pramanya of Mimaimsa and Nydaya (theories of intrinsic and
extrinsic validity) we have, so far as the authorship of the Vedas
is concerned, their theories of Apauruseya and Pauruseya-vada.
According to Mimamsa, knowledge has its criterion within itself.
According to Nyaya, knowledge has its criterion outside itself.
Mimamsa inclines towards internal coherence, Nyaya towards
external reference.

129 See Section VI. 5 in Chapter VI.
130 See Foot Note 147 in Chapter VI.

131   Coherence is internal consistency. Two statements can not be
      true together if they are inconsistent. For example, A is accused
      of murdering B. Suppossing, it is proved that A was not present
      in the town on the day of murder, his being away and the
      accusation of murder are not coherent. Consistency or harmony
      is a mark of truth. Bradley would say that the smallest of the
      small or the meanest of the mean can not be dispensed with as
      it has its appointed place in the harmony of the Absolute. When



                                  235
Studies in Indian Philosophy


would be valid, (a) provided the source from which it springs is
perfect, i.c., the cause from which it proceeds is real (Keith, P.
18), (b) provided the sense-organs are not deficient or (c)
provided that it leads to some useful purpose. Thus, e.g., the
dream-consciousness would be invalid because there is no
.perfection in its cause; the yellowness of the conch would be
invalid because there is jaundice in the eye; and again, the water
of the mirage would be unreal, because it does not lead to the
quenching of thirst. The realism of Nyaya, however, is tinged
with Pragmatism.!32 Thus according to Naiyayikas, knowledge

 132 Pragmatism holds that truth is that which works or that which

 133 According to Nyaya, truth is correspondence with reality. But
      there is no way to ascertain the correspondence unless you
      previously know the fact. Truth is then sought to be established
      by successful activity. "So the doctrine proposes an indirect
      test— fruitful activity (Samvadi-pravrtti). What serves as the test
      is thus really another experience — that of thirst being
      quenched....." (See M. Hiriyanna, Outlines of Indian Philo-

                                                             Nyaya-vaisesika


thirst. Cf. the Arthakriyakaritva of the Buddhists.134 Thus Nyaya


      sophy, P. 308). We believe that there is water. This gives rise
      to a desire in us to partake of it. When actually the water is
      drunk and the thirst is quenched there is satisfaction of desire.
      This however is an extrancous condition. The pragmatic test,
      it must be borne in mind, is only for verification and does not
      constitute the definition of truth, which is correspondence with

                           तत्मकारकज्ञानत्वं प्रामाण्यम्‌|| (Valid know-
      ledge is that knowledge regarding something possessing a
      particular attribute, which has that attribute as its feature
      (prakara), which is conducive to successful effort, and which
      includes recollection as well as fresh experience). Vedanta-
      paribhasa, of Dharmarajadhvarindra, Tr. Swami Madhava-
      nanda, Pp. 143-144. The explanation is that "valid knowledge
      is knowing a thing as it is, and not as something else, which
      would be error. For instance, the sentence, "The lake has fire",
      does not give us valid knowledge, for a lake contains water and
      not fire" (Ibid. P. 145, Note 4). Again, when we mistake a conch
      for silver our effort to take it, is not successful (Ibid. P. 145,
      Note 5).
134   अर्थक्रि याकारि त्व is a term used by modern Naiyayikas in the
      discussion of import of words. When a word like घट is uttered,
      the hearer should understand what entity is referred to. The
      घट primarily denotes घटव्यक्ति     , [16 particular object. Hence the
      capacity of being the ‘subject of any act’ (अर्थक्रि याकारि त्व) resides
      in the concrete object घट. Thus according to the Modern
      Naiyayikas, the import of word is केवलव्यक्ति     . The Mimamsakas
      hold that a word implies a class-concept (जाति ); घट implies
      घटत्व (केवलजाति ). The ancient Naiyayikas avoid these two

     extremes and hold that the word घट implies a particular object

     word घट we do not understand an external object, becuase we
      never know external objects, nor the जाति           because जाति      is
      nothing more than a conception formed by us and imposed on
      what we call external objects. By घट we understand that a thing
      possesses certain peculiaritics that tell it to be different from
      other things. Thus घट does not tell us what a thing is but what
      it is not. It is negative knowledge and not a positive definition
      (See Athalye and Bodas, Tarka-sangraha of Annambhatta, Pp.

 135 See Foot Notes 133 and 134 above.
 136 It was John Locke who made the distinction between primary
      and secondary qualities. Primary qualities are objective i.e. they
      belong to the object. Locke names figure, extension, etc. as
      primary qualities. Secondary qualities, on the other hand, are
      powers in the substance to produce various sensations in us by
      the primary qualities. These are colour, sound, taste and so on.
     They can change; and their removal does not affect the



                                    238
                                                          Nyaya-vaisesika


Cr:    the    tertiary    qualities      in   Neo-realism.137      Holt’s


      existerce of the substance. Now according to Nyaya-Vaisesika
      philosophy, secondary qualities are also objective, i.e. primary.
      They belong to the object. They are sensed by us because there
      is likeness between the object and the sense, e.g. the colour is
      in the object and is made up of Tejas atoms, so is the eye made
      up of Tejas atoms; and so the eye knows the colour. The realism
      of Nyaya does not allow the colour to be contributed by the
      knowing mind. It is in the object itself.
137   Neo-realism began as a polemic against idealism. Then there
      arose a desire to spell out in a cooperative spirit the constructive
      doctrines of neo-realism. Consequently, ‘The Program and
      First Platform of Six Realists’ by the advocates of the new
      philosophy — E. B. Holt, W. T. Marvin, W. P. Montague, 1९. 8.
      Perry, W. B. Pitkin, and E. G. Spaulding was published in
      Journal of Philosophy, Psychology and Scientific Methods, Vol.
      VII, 1910. A volume entitled ‘The New Realism’ by the same
      writers also indicates the direction of their thinking.
      According to Neo-realism all relations are external. Objects
      may enter into new and new relations; but they do not alter the
      structure of the object. The relation is an added character. An
      object may enter into a relation with a. knowing subject. A
      person’s consciousness of a thing is made up of (1) the thing
      and (ii) its relation to consciousness. For instance, in “a person
      knows the book’, the person’s consciousness of the book
      consists of (i) the book and (ii) the relation of the book to him.
      The latter is a character which is added to the person’s
      consciousness. In Idealism, it is the mind which contributes to
      the knowledge process. In Neo-realism on the contrary, the
      object is the contributor. Whenever a thing enters into a relation
      with another entity, it acquires a new character which may be
      termed tertiary (third) quality. Perry does not use the term



                                   239
Studies in Indian Philosophy


physiologism.138      (ii) Realistic theory of error. The illusory



     tertiary qualities but his description of ‘relations as an added
     character’ seems to imply that relations are a ‘third quality’,
     distinguishable from the traditional primary and secondary
     qualities. See R. B. Perry, Present Philosophical Tendencies,
     Chapter XIII. See also Foot Note 136 above.
138 Some physiological aspects are specially emphasised by E. B.
     Holt. Neo-realism has its own philosophy of mind. Mind to
     them is not exclusively available to the method of introspection.
     It   is available,   equally   easily to   the   method   of general
     observation. This leads further to the assertion that a man’s
     mind is known not solely to himself but also to others. Common
     place behaviour of a man is a window to his mind. From his
     buying a thing in the market we know what he must be thinking.
     His verbal reports also help an outsider to follow the speaker’s
      mind. So bodily behaviour is a mirror to a man’s mind. It is
      true to say that a person has his own internal sensations (called
      proprioceptive sensations) that only he can know. For example,
      pressures and strains from internal organs are peculiarly his
      own. But even then, an outsider, though he cannot experience

      these with others, can know them. A doctor may even know
      them better by his study and a little imagination. So perhaps a
      physiologist would be in a better position to report on my mental
      processes. A man’s mental content is part of the environment

      on which his organism acts — 11 is his interested behaviour. A
      cat’s mind is its interested behaviour towards a rat. Mind is
      thus reduced by the neo-realists to physiological elements.
      Holt’s two articles
                        — ‘Concept of Consciousness’ and ‘Response
      and Cognition’, in Journal of Philosophy, Psychology and
      Scientific Methods, Vol. XII, Nos. 14 and 15, constitute an able
      statement of his ‘physiologism’ ($6९ R. B. Perry, Present
      Philosophical Tendencies, Chapter XI).

yellow colour itself objective. The Naiyayikas hold that when a

is Apanastha. Also, no quenching ofthirst follows. Hence, fusion
of realism and pragmatism.
(त)   In   regard to   Pramanya-vada or validity of knowledge,
GangeSa tells us that two questions arise: (i) Whence is the
validity of knowledge derived and (ii) How are we conscious of
it? (In our opinion these two questions reduce themselves to the
same thing).!40 The Mimamsakas answer the question by saying



139 All the factors in a judgment may be severally true; but the
      whole complex may not have a corresponding complex in the
      objective   world   and   is therefore, false. The conch,     the
      yellowness and the Samavaya are not present together in one
      single complex whole; and so itis error (See Hiriyanna, Outlines
      of Indian Philosophy, P. 252).
140 Prof. Ranade reduces the two questions to one from the
      idealistic point of view. The question of validity assumes
      significance only when it enters a knower’s consciousness. Till
      then he may be blissfully unaware of whether any piece of
      knowledge is valid or not. Thus the two questions would be
      reduced to one question, viz. How are we conscious of the
      source of validity? But the questions will very much remain two
      if we accept the stark realistic position. This latter would be
      interested in validity as such irrespective of any knower
      whatsoever. This can be compared with the definition of truth-
      in-itself (Satz an sich) given by Bernard Bolzano : ‘By a "Satz
      an sich" I mean any statement whatever to the effect that some
      thing is or is not, irrespective of whether the statement be true

Studies in Indian Philosophy


that knowledge is self-evident. If so, says GangeSa, invalid
knowledge would be identical with valid knowledge. It follows
therefore that knowledge is not self-evident, but is inferred from
the fruitful correspondence between the knowledge and the
activity. After a Ghata is known, a quality called Jiatata is
produced in Ghata according to Mimamsakas and a quality called
17811५8 or संवि ति   is produced in Atman (knower) according to
the Naiyayikas. As regards Aprama or invalid knowledge, the
Mimamsakas (Prabhakara?) do not admit invalid knowledge or
error at all,141 All knowledge according to them is valid.
According to GangesSa, Aprama is that knowledge which does
not abide in its own subject but in the subject of another generic
nature (Anyathakhyati). Moreover, in our consciousness ‘this is
silver’, says GangeSa, we must suppose that there is only one
kind of knowledge, and it is cumbrous to assume two like the
 Mimamsakas, viz., perception and recollection.            See Vidya-

Y. V. Athalye and M. R. 80085 (Editors) :   "Tarka-sangraha".
The Introduction which is historical in character should be read.
The notes are very good, every chief topic being discussed
separately in a different note. The notes are of much philosophical
importance. The topics of the notes are concerned with separate
philosophical problems.

A. 3. Keith: Indian Logic and Atomism — Part | is a historical
 Introduction, containing in two Chapters the old school and the
 new school respectively. The notes are philosophical. In Part II,
 the philosophy of the Nyaya-Vaisesika. The following Chapters
 might be read. Chapter I is epistemological and discusses the
 nature    of knowledge   and   error according    to    the   School,
 contrasting it with the standpoints of Buddhism and Mimamsa.
 Chapters II and III 4150058 प्रत्यक्ष and अनुमान (Perception and
 Inference) respectively. Chapter VII discusses the categories of
 Nyaya and Vaisesika respectively. Chapter VIII discusses the
 Atomic Theory. Chapter IX is Psychological and Chapter X
 Theistic. On the whole, a valuable book.

 translated by Satish Chandra Vidyabhushan (Sacred Books of
 the Hindus) Vol. VIII. Typography excellent. A very fine copy.

 of Udayana (University copy, Sanskrit 120/33) contains at the

 6 + 19). Nyadya arguments for the existence of God.
