                    PURVA-MIMAMSA
I. Reduction of all Pramanas to one!
Progressive delineation of the six Pramanas.2
Carvaka     —   Pratyaksa
Vaisesika   —   Pratyaksa + Anumana
Sankhya —       Pratyaksa + Anumana + Sabda
Nyaya     —     Pratyaksa + Anumana + Sabda + Upamana
Prabhakara—     Pratyaksa + Anumana + Sabda + Upamana
                                                        + Arthapatti
Kumarila    —   Pratyaksa + Anumana + Sabda + Upamdna
                                       + Arthapatti + Anupalabdhi
Bauddhas do regard Anumana as a Pramana but their Anumana
is invalid.3 Criticism of these Pramanas in the light of the
Vedantic "Anubhava".

A. (1) Anupalabdhi or non-perception cannot be a          criterion at
all. It is invented to explain our apprehension of "Abhava" or

     a general statement relating two things to be true, when it is
     made the ground of practical activity. Their acceptance of
     relation is only pragmatic. But their inference is invalid because
     theoretically all relations according to them aré false and as
     inference is necessarily based on relations, their view of
     inference can not be valid.

Ja    If अभाव is something negative or that which does not exist, 20
      criterion is, in fact, necessary to apprehend it; a negative
      criterion like अनुपलब्धि       is moreover least fitted to apprehend
      अभाव.     However, if अभाव is भावरूप i.e. positive or something
      existing, प्रत्यक्ष can be used to apprehend it. Thus in both the
      cases, अनुपलब्धि     is driven out of court.
5     The quotation is from Ramanuja’s Sribhasya on Vedanta-siitra
       1.1.1. When a flame is burning, it appears to be one and the

      same to Pratyaksa or perception. But inference will stultify the
      oneness of the flame by proving that there are many flames
       भि न्नज्वालाः), as different particles are burning one after the
      other. Which of the two — perception or inference — has greater
      validity? Ramanuja says that inference here sublates percep-
      tion, because what is given by Pratyaksa can be accounted for
      otherwise i.e. the one-ness of the flame is due to the very rapid
      succession of different flames. (See, Sribhasya, published by
      Shri Nirnbarkacarya Pith, 12 Mahajani Tola, Prayag, Samvat
      2030, P. 31). Whatever the explanation, the very fact that Rama-
      nuja, a realist, degrades Pratyaksa, goes against his realism.
6     For example, a गो will resemble a गवय in respect of horns, and



                                         247
Studies in Indian Philosophy


such as the "counting" of points of resemblance between Earth
and Mars in respect of their suitability as habitations for life.
Mars is a planet like the Earth. Therefore, it must have life on
it. Does not "weigh" but only count. Analogical inference cannot
be valid inference. It only leads to probability. (4) (i) Arthapatti
or"Implication" (which is the soul of Inference). It can, therefore,

     2 गवय will resemble a गो in respect of hoofs and so on, so that
     there never will arrive a point where all the similarities are
     exhausted and a valid conclusion is arrived at.
7    "Characteristics which attract general notice only when full

     grown, are traceable far back when we come to look for them;
     and further, they are frequently implied by the nature of an
      individual long before any scrutiny can detect them.... The
      labour of genuine science is to disentangle the true continuity
      of process, limiting it only by modifications which are certainly
      traced or inevitably implied; never assuming the existence of a
      highly developed attribute or function where it is not seen in
      operation, or shown to be implied to the exclusion of all
      conceivable    alternatives."    (B.   Bosanquet,     Logic    or   the
      Morphology of Knowledge, Vol. I, Pp. 15-16). What Bosanquct
      means is that science should always trace back the obvious
      characteristics to their source where they are not so obvious
      but are only potential; nevertheless they imply the obvious.
      Again the characteristic which we say is "implied" must be the
      only alternative which explains the situation, e.g. पीनो देवदत्तः
     दि वान भुंक्ते | (The fat Devadatta does not eat during day). The
      fatness can be explained only on the hypothesis of his cating
      at night. Therefore, रात्री भुंक्ते (cats at night) is clearly implied.

Kumarila     says    there   is   contradiction    which    is   ultimately
reconciled. Both recognise Arthapatti. Sansaya is required as an
initial stage even in Anumana नानुपलब्धे न नि र्णति र्र्थ न्यायः प्रवर्तते.8
(iii) While the Mimamsakas contend that we must recognise
Arthapatti to be a new Pramana, as we cannot assign a Linga or
Hetu or Midle term which alone would justify its being classed
under Anum§ana, the Naiyayikas hold that we cannot regard
Arthapatti as a new Pram4na, but that it can very well be shown
to be included under Anumana: and २७६५३ is right. The formula
for Arthapatti can very well be exhibited in the form of "Barbara"
in the First Figure as follows:
All fat non-eaters by day must eat at night.
S. N. is a fat non-cater by day.
Therefore, he must eat at night. (Kevalanvayi type).

The "non-eater" here, however, is a negative expression. Or,
alternatively, the formula could be exhibited as follows in EAE
in the First Figure:

No fat man non-eating by day is a non-eater at night.




8     The expression occurs in वात्स्यायन भाष्य on गौतमन्यायसूत्र 1.1.1.
      The latter portion of the sentence is: कि ं तर्हि संशयि तेऽ्थ' (What,
      if there is doubt?). This indicates the exact subject-matter of
      न्याय. न्याय can not proceed where no data are available
      (अनुपलब्ध) nor does it proceed where an indubitable conclusion
      is arrived at (नि र्णी त). In the first case, it is impossible; in the
      second it is superfluous. केशव मि श्र in his THATS adds a positive
      sense by quoting a commentator’s expression— अपि तु संदि ग्धे
      (त्याय proceeds when there is ambiguity about the conclusion).
      He refers to the expression twice — once in अतुमान प्रकरण and
      another time in हेत्वाभास प्रकरण.

Formally, this Anumana would be correct; also the "implication"
of it would cover what is intended to be proved by Arthapatti.
Arth4patti is thus implicative inference. (5) The Anum4na gives
us only a partial grasp of reality. Judgment and Inference are
only appearances and not reality. Truth, as Bradley says, always
falls short of Reality. Therefore, Anumana invalid. (See Buddhist
criticism of Anumana in Santaraksita, as well as Vedantic
Criticism in Sriharsa). Wecan not bring all the Tarkikas together;

16    Anubhava means direct, mystical or spiritual experience. The
      faculty involved in this experience is intuition. Hence it is
      termed intuitive experience. It can be classed under perception
      because it is direct experience. However, it is not sensuous
      perception or Laukika Pratyaksa as no sense-organ is involved
      in it. Ordinary or sensuous perception does not give indubitable
      knowledge whereas intuitive experience gives absolutely certain
      knowledge, like deductive inference. Thus it shares directness
      with   perception      and   certainty   with   inference.   Intuitive
      experience is the same as mystical experience.

21     शब्द (sound) is a quality of आकाश (ether) which is the substance.

       Now a quality or adjective has a dependent existence. Word is
       not thus supreme according to the Naiyayikas.
22     The phrase means begging the question or taking for granted
       what is to be proved. When Nyaya philosophy gives arguments
       for the existence of God, it states that God exists because Vedas
       say so. Now to say that God created Vedas and again to say
       that God exists because the Vedas say so, appears to beg the
       question in as much as the existence of Vedas depends on God
       and the proof of God’s existence depends on the Vedas. The
       apparent circularity of the argument is resolved by recognising
       two orders, viz. order of existence, ratio essendi, and order of
       knowledge, ratio cognoscendi. In the order of existence, God
       comes first and creates the Vedas. It is only in the order of
       knowledge that the Vedas come first because it is through the
       Vedas that we know God.
23     "The true eternity of the Vedas consists, according to Gautama,
       in   the   unbroken   continuity of their   tradition, study and
       employment, both in the Manvantaras and the Yugas, which
       are past and those that are still to come, whilst their authority

       depends on the authority of the most competent persons." (Max
       Muller, The Six systems of Indian Philosophy, p. 414).

36   "As mere internal movements, the states which follow external
     irritants may exist side by side in us.... but if they are to admit
     of combination in the definite form of a thought, they each
     require some previous shaping to make them into logical
     building stones." (Lotze, Logic, English translation (by many
     authors), Edited by B. Bosanquet, Oxford, P. 10). "Without
     doubt.... pure logic must place the form of the concept before
     that of the judgment" (Ibid. P. 18). For Lotze concepts are
     prior, they are moulded, shaped and then are combined into
     judgments. His view is clementaristic.
     Bosanquet on the other hand, holds a Gestaltist view. He does
     not   agree’ that   ideas are subsequent      to impressions, that
     concepts     are subsequent     to ideas,   or that judgments are
     subsequent to simpler concepts. For him mere concepts are
     blindwithout a judgment. Knowledge does not consist in laying
     concept-stones      to   concept-stones.    Judgment   is   a   unitary
     function of the mind. Concepts are not prior to judgments, they
     are the result of analysis of a judgment. Bosanquet says, "I have
     never been able to doubt that the central function of the
     intellect,   I would even say of consciousness, is one from
     beginning to end.... In speaking of the connection between
     impressions and ideas and again between concepts and the
     judgment, I am obliged to reject the casy partition into distinct
     operations." (B. Bosanquet, Logic or The Morphology of
     Knowledge, P. 29). "Judgment is not, in relation cither to
     impressions, ideas or concepts, a mechanical combination of
     parts which remain outside each other. It is an expression of
     the unity in which consciousness consists." (Ibid. P. 31).

38    "Existence being divided, as found in cows and so on, is called

      Tva, Tal and so on, which form abstract nouns, such as Go-tva,

(Max Muller, P. 532). Compare all ideas as species of the Idea

     nature of a term). One rule dealing with हृद compound runs as—
     लघ्वक्षरं पूर्व ति ष्ठति (the term consisting of लघु letters comes first
     in the compound). So when we take the two terms, वाक्य and
     पद, the && compound would be पदवाक्य. And after adding the
     ईय suffix to indicate the name of a book, the compound would
     be पदवाक्यीय. Hence grammatically the expression should have
     been पदवाक्यीय and not वाक्यपदीय. However, there is another
     rule, अभ्यर्हि ते पूर्व नि पतति , 1.6७. the term which is more important
     comes first. According to Bhartrhari, a वाक्य or judgment has
     logical priority over a पद or term, the latter being only thé result
     of analysis of a judgment. In this case, the compound would be
     वाक्यपदीय. Here we have two different rules regarding the
     compound, the first making the compound पदवाक्यीय and the
     second making it वाक्यपदीय. In such a case of conflict, there is
     yet one more rule, वि प्रति षेधे परं कार्यम्‌. In the case of a conflict
     of two rules, the subsequent rule supersedes the earlier one.
     Hence the title of Bhartrhari’s work is वाक्यपदीय and rightly so.
     The observation in the text, that वाक्यपदीय is ungrammatical’
     seems to be interpolated as it has no connection cither with
     the preceding or succeeding sentence. In fact it breaks the flow
     of the argument. If it is excised, the para reads well. It might
     have been suggested by somebody when the notes were read
     out. Perhaps Prof. Ranade would not have allowed it in the
     final draft.
40   In a classificatory series, species are subsumed under a genus.
     That genus is further subsumed under a higher genus till we
     reach the summum genus. The whole presents a hierarchy. This
     is how Plato represents the world of Ideas. Ideas of less general
     things are subsumed under more general things and the process
     continues till we rise to the apex, namely, the Idea of the Good.

all, words ultimately mean God, the Logos (Max Muller, ए.

      "The world of sense is the copy of the world of Ideas; and
      conversely, the world of Ideas resembles its image; it forms a
      hierarchy. In our visible world there is a gradation of being
      from the most imperfect creature to the perfect, sensible being,
      or the universe. The same holds true of the intelligible realm
      or the pattern of the world; the Ideas are joined together by
      means of other Ideas of a higher order; the latter, in turn, are
      embraced under others still more exalted, and so on; the Ideas
      constantly increase in generality and force, until we reach the
      top, the last, the highest, the most powerful Idea or the Good,
      which comprehends, contains or summarises the entire system,
      just as the visible universe, its copy, comprehends, contains, or
      summarises all creatures’ (Weber and Perry, History of
      Philosophy, P. 62).
 41   The philosophy of Sanskrit grammar puts forth this view. As
      all that exists is but the manifestation of Brahman, each word
      (though it stands for an object like a mountain or a river), sub
      specie eternitatis, means Brahman only. Brahman alone is the
      referent (पति पाद्य) of every word. Word and its denotation
       (द्रव्यरूप) are eternal. In this connection Kaiyata says,

Three points of view, therefore: Epistemological, Logical and
Philosophical. From any one of these approaches, Sabda or
Sphota becomes identical with absolute reality. All these
approaches, however, are merely intellectual and not mystical.
Panini alone saw the mystical significance of it. "The eternal
Word", says Madhava in his Sarva-darsana-sangraha, "which is
called Sphota, and which is without parts, is the true cause of
the world." Badarayana in the Sutra I. 3.28 and Sankara in
criticising the doctrine of "Sphota", failed to sympathise with
the mystical significance of it. Both Badarayana and Sankara
say that the world comes from the "Word" (and not Sphota), and
this Word is Brahman (Max Muller, P. 521). Contrast जन्माद्यस्य
यत: 144 यतः = from God. In his criticism of the doctrine of
Sphota,      Sankara45       particularly     quotes     Upavarsa,      117९
Mimamsaka-Vedantist, according to whom the word is nothing
but the aggregate of its constitutive letters (वर्णसमुदायः शब्दः) as
against the grammarians who teach that over and above the
aggregate of the letters there exists a super-sensuous entity called
Sphota which is the direct cause of the apprehension of the
meaning of a-word (Thibaut, P. xxxvii).46 (1) उचरि त शब्द and (2)

      in a particular manner becomes a वर्ण; for example, one of the
      sounds pronounced from the throat, is कू. By combining वर्ण
      we get a letter (अक्षर). For example, + अ = क. These letters,
      pronounced at a particular time, particular place, and having
      regard to the intention of the speaker and the hearer, become
      2 शब्द, for example, कमल. The meaning is conveyed not because
      of the aggregate of letters but because of the supersensuous
      power (स्फोट) which is different from the letters. The meaning
      bursts forth. This is Sphota. From words uttered, a meaning is
      suggested. On similar lines, वाच्य (literal or primary meaning
      of a word) manifests an implication (suggestion or Dhwani).
      For example, the sun sets. This simple sentence primarily refers
      to an event. But it suggests (ध्वनि ) different things to different
      people. A cowherd takes it as the time to take the cows home.
      For a thief it is time to begin his activity and so on. Sce also
      Foot Note 55 below.

54       5९९ Foot Note 25 above.
55       Sphotavada has both warm friends and bitter enemies. Some
         raise the doctrine to the skies while others are not prepared to
         touch it even with a pair of tongs. A speaker utters a word, a
         hearer understands its meaning. Wherein lies the power to
         express the meaning? The linguistico-metaphysical view is that
         it is Sphota. It is the vehicle of the idea which flashes on the
         mind or the impression produced on the mind on hearing the
         sound. When a word is uttered, the individual constituent letters
         disappear as soon as uttered. Each letter comes after its
         predecessor. The      letters are never simultaously present.
         Therefore a ‘group of sounds’ can not express a meaning. Nor
         does an individual letter by itself convey the meaning, for, in
         that case, other letters in the word will be superfluous. The
         Sphotavadins attribute this function of conveying the meaning
         to Sphota, the Word, which is independent of uttered letters
         and which lies at the back of them all. This Word is eternal
         (नि त्य), indivisible (अखंड), not involving sequence (अक्रम). The
         uttered letters (वर्णसमूह) are just occasions of the expression‘of
         meaning    (अभि व्यंजक)   while Sphota is what bursts       forth
         (अभि व्यंग्य), when words are uttered. As such the uttered letters
         (वर्णसमूह) are secondary (गौण), while स्फोट is primary (प्रधान).
         On the same lines the Alankarikas or rhetoricians say that the

111. Metaphysical Doctrines of the Mimamsa~°
1. The Reality of the World. (a) Critical: Criticism of Buddhistic
unreality. The external world is not a product of mere intellect
(idea). Also no momentariness like that of an idea.’ (b)
Epistemological : Object exists because the report of one sense
is corroborated by that of another. Hence the world, objective
and real.58 (©) Cosmological. The world is also eternal.

59   Therefore the theory of Creation and Dissolution must be

     admitted to resemble the present every day processes (of
     production and destruction); and any particular idea of these
     with regard to the production and destruction of the whole
     universe cannot be established, for want of proofs (113). Even
     the existence of
                    a Creator is to be rejected in the same manner
     as an omniscient person. Any such Creator cannot differ from
     ordinary people, except through (an excess of) Dharma; nor is
     Dharma possible without performance (of actions); and
     performance is not possible without an idea (of the action to
     be performed); this idea is not possible except from the Veda;
     nor   is   (a    knowledge of)          the Veda possible without (a
     comprehension of) words, and so on. Therefore it must be
     admitted that all these (Words and so on), existed before the
     Creator. And again, such a creator may be proved to have been
     preceded by the Veda, on account of His being an intelligent
     being like ourselves (who are preceded by the Veda) (114-116).
     It is impossible to give an adequate reply to the people who
     assert (the fact of the Creator being preceded by the Veda) on
     the ground of these reasonings explained in 114-116. Therefore
     the followers of the Veda must explain the usage (of Words)
     as being without a beginning (1.०, eternal) (117). (Sloka-vartika
     by Kumarilabhatta, Tr. Ganga Nath Jha, The Asiatic Society,
     1 Park Street, Calcutta, 1985, Pp. 368-369).

Sloka-vartika.82 (i) We must posit the reality of the external
world; of virtue and vice; of teacher and pupil. Even in dream
cognition, we must posit a real substratum. The external world
is not a product of mere intellect.    Our ideas are imposed upon
us by an external reality. (ii) It is wrong to say that "cognition"
alone exists, to the exclusion of cogniser and cognised. (iii) No
idea can comprehend itself; nor any one idea be comprehended
by another. It is wrong to say that one idea as it originates,
becomes known to the first. (iv) The unity and continuity of our
mental life cannot be explained without the recognition of a




     which evoked replies from Nyaya and Vedanta that sought to
     refute his heresies. The reply of Mimamsa is given in Vrttikara
     as cited in the Bhasya. There is a subtle dialectic between the
     Vrttikara and the Buddhist opponent on the reality of the
     external world, knowledge of the external world or knowledge

     of the idea.                  1
     There are some differences of opinion whether the criticism is
     against शून्यवाद or वि ज्ञानवाद. The value of Kumarila’s refutation
     of the Buddhist schools is not inconsiderable; he brings out
     fully the grave difficultics which meet any effort to account for
     the facts of life without accepting some permanent entity. This
     criticism is regarding the existence of the external world. Then
     there is the criticism against the Buddhistic idea of no-self
     (covered in points (५) to (VIII) above). The Mimamsa
     conclusion is that the soul, with the aid of the mind, is the
     enjoyer of all experience; the sense organs are the instruments;
     and the body is the abode of the sense organs and the mind,
     through whose instrumentality the soul has experience (A.B.
     Keith, The Karma Mimamsi, Association Press, 1921).
82   For नि रालंबनवाद see verses 1-197 and for शून्यवाद verses 1-264
     in श्लोकवार्ति क.


                                 270
                                                    Pirva Mimamsa


substantival unity ofthe self. In Hume and Buddhism one idea
contiguous with and yet separate from another. In James the next
idea envelopes the first. (v) The law of retribution requires that
the sower and reaper of actions must be the same. (vi) The
Buddhist cannot explain "transmigration".        A momentary idea
cannot transmigrate itself. It is the soul alone which trans-
migrates. "The sea remains, despite the movements ofits waves;
the serpent uncoils, without change of essence." (vii) The fact
that I fee! with my hand what I see with my eyes shows that
there exists something beyond sense-organs. (vili) Other souls
also exist. The "perception" of another soul is impossible, but
one can "infer" another soul from the activity ofits body.


18. Metaphysics: World, Self, God
(1) The World eternally existent :

       Tasmadadyavadeyvatra sarga-pralaya-kalpana,
       Samastaksayajanmabhyam na sidhyatyapramanika
       Tasmat-pragapi sarvemi srastuh asan padadayah
       Anvesyovyavaharoyamanadirvedavadibhih

       ( Slokavartika, Sambandhaksepa-parihara, Verses 113-117).

Neither creation nor destniction of the world. No creator required.

(2) Jaimini gives no Sutras for the establishment ofthe Self. The
Self is only to be inferred: Svargakamo yajeta. Though Jaimini
is silent, Sabara enters into an elaborate proof
                                             of the soul which
is independent of the body and senses, and beyond pleasure and
pain. According to Prabhakara and Kuméarila, there are many
souls, all eternal: Nityah sarvagatah pum4n (Sloka-vartika,
Atma-vada, verse 73). Cf. also Salikanatha.
       BuddhindriyaSarirebhyo bhinna atma vibhurdhruvah
       Nanabhitah pratiksetramarthavittisu bhasate

                      (Prakarana-paicika, Atma-tattva, Pp. 141 ff).
                                271
Studies in Indian Philosophy


(3) God is not required in Mimamsa for giving rewards and
punishments. The Apurva or the invisible potency alone can do
so. Jaimini says that the deity is Guna, or subsidiary in a sacrifice.
Havis is more important than Devata, cf. Havisa niyamyeta
(Purva Mimamsa VIII. 1. 32), and Gunatve devatasrutih (Purva
Mimamsa IX. 1. 9). The deity has no body. The sacrifice cannot
be intended to please the deity. The deity is not the Lord of all
things. The fruit of the sacrifice does not proceed from the deity.
The deities of the sacrifices are merely symbols and objects to
whom Suktas are addressed: Suktabhajo havirbhajasca devatah.
They are to be distinguished from the deities of the Puranas,
which occupy a higher position. No synonym can be employed
for Devatis. We cannot substitute the word Pavaka or Suci for
Agni. No personal god required for creating the world. God is
not omniscient. As there can be no object of knowledge, God
has neither knowledge nor omniscience. He is only the sum total
of all privation of knowledge. Jianabhava-niscayah (see Kane).

For the arguments which Mimamsa uses against God, sce Keith,
Pp. 61-64; also Sloka-vartika. Cf. Jainistic arguments against
God.   The   arguments    can   be summed      up   as   follows:   (1)
Impossibility of God having a body. If he has a body, then there
must be a matter which must have existed before him. (ii) What
can be his motive in creation? Why does he feel a desire to
multiply? (111) Why should God create a world full of misery, if
he is all good? Hence God is not benevolent. Vaisamya-
nairghmmya-prasangat.83 (iv) If there is moral evil in the world,
God is either not omniscient or not omnipotent. (v) He has also
no fore-knowledge. Did God fore-know the Bamrauli disaster?
God’s knowledge and power conflict with his goodness; his


83   See Sankara-bhasya on Vedanta-sitra II. 1. 34.


                                 272
                                                      Parva Mimamsa


fore-knowledge conflicts with man’s freedom.
                    Here the rough draft ends].

IV. Contribution to the Science ofInterpretation

The Tarkapada of Jaimini, viz., 1.1, is dedicated to a discussion
of philosophical topics. The remaining Padas of the first, and
the other Adhyayas upto the twelfth are devoted to a discussion
of the principles of ritual interpretation.
The "Arthavada" discussed in I. 2 has its value in extolling
desirable or censuring      forbidden acts; it thus comes into
immediate connection with injunctions or prohibitions.

1.3. discusses the relation of Smrti to Veda. Below Smrti in value
comes the practice of good men. 1. 4. discusses the subject of
Names or Namadheya. It seems that the Mimamsa felt the
necessity of dealing wth a number of-obscure sacrificial terms.

Mimamsa (I. 1) develops such principles as Ekavakyata84
(syntactical unity), Vakyabheda85 (syntactical split) and Anu-
sanga86 1.९. the principle of extension.

Vidhis or injunctions are encitements to actions. Action may be
either positive or negative, or may partake of both characters.


84   एकवाक्यता   means    consistency in   meaning,   unanimity,    or
     reconciling different statements. Short, simple sentences may
     by themselves be meaningful and independent. However, a
     complex sentence may be formed out of them which expresses
     a wider meaning. All statements are brought together in a
     consistent way to have a larger application. This is एकवाक्यता.
     See Foot Notes 96 and 97 below.
85   वाक्यभेद means a different assertion, a divergent statement.
86   अनुषंग means a mode of interpreting an incomplete sentence by
     supplying the required word or words from the immediate
     context.




                                273
Studies in Indian Philosophy


When it is negative, it is called Pratisedha and when it partakes
of both charaeters, it is called Paryudasa.87
Actions may also be classified as Purusartha88 and Kratvartha8?
done for the sake of the agent and done for the sake of the
offering.
Injunctions or Vidhis are primarily of three kinds : Adhikara-
vidhi    or Vidhi     relating    to   qualification,     Utapatti-vidhi     or
originative    injunction     and      Viniyoga-vidhi       or   injuction   of
application.
Originative injunctions are few in number, relating that they do
to the principal Vedic offering only; they do no more than excite
in the mind of a hearer the desire to perform the action.
Injunctions of application denote the exact manner of procedure
(itikartavyata) by specifying the numerous subsidiary actions.
These subsidiary actions are called Sesas, and the distinction
between Sega and Sesin occupies the greater part ofthe attention
of the Mimamsa.

While dealing with injunctions of application, we may note the
six means by which the relations of subordination of actions

     students of a particular university, say X university, should not
     apply for a post, 15 प्रसज्य-प्रति षेध ठा direct negation. On the other
     hand, to say that students other than those of X university
     should alone apply 15 पर्युदास 0 indirect negation. The import
     of प्रसज्य-प्रति षेघ is prohibition, that ० पर्युदास is exclusion.

may be determined. These are : Sruti or express declaration;

Prakarana or context; Krama or order which is the same thing

these means is deemed to be of"less" value than the preceding.

90   कात्यायनवार्ति क I. 3. 11. This maxim teaches that when two rules
     are equally weighty, the grammatical operation should proceed
     according to the rule which comes later. पर (later rule) is
     enjoined in preference to the Y (earlier rule). But if the carlier
     rule deals with regularly prescribed action (नि त्य) or with an
     essential part of the base of a word’ (अंतरंग) or with a special
     rule or order or command (अपवाद), the grammatical operation
     should proceed as per the earlier rule. Here पूर्वं (earlier rule)
     is preferred to पर (later rule). This is the practice of grammar,
     and the quotation under reference comes from a grammarian.
     Thus मीमांसा and व्याकरण hold opposite views. In मीमांसा there
     is उत्तरोत्तर दौर्बल्य; in grammar on the other hand, (1९1015 उत्तरोत्तर
     प्राबल्य.   However, in the realm of मीमांसा, मीमांसा-11391115
     (वेदोक्तवि धि 5) are honoured. In the realm of grammar, grammati-
     cal maxims (पाणि नि सूत्र5) carry the day. The upshot seems to be:
     Render therefore unto Caesar the things which are Caesar’s
     and unto God the things that are God’s (Matthew 22.21). अंतरंग
     should prevail over नि त्य, and अपवाद over अंतरंग. पूर्व, पर, नि त्य,
     अंतरंग and अपवाद constitute the grades of priority.



                                    275
Studies in Indian Philosophy


Vidhis again, Apurva-vidhi or original injunction, Niyama-vidhi
or restrictive injunction, and Parisankhya-vidhi or injunction of
limitation or exclusion. Over against these Vidhis or injunctions
which direct a positive act, there ar¢ prohibitions or Nisedhas,
e.g., the Brahmacarin must not look at the sun.

The above are the main topics with which Padas 2-4 of Adhyaya
I and Adhyayas II—VI of the Mimamsa-sttras deal. Adhyayas
VII and VIII discuss Atidesa or transference. The process of
transference, however, involves modifications (Uhas) which are
discussed in Adhyaya IX. In other cases, the transference must
be accompanied by the annulment of details, which are discussed
 in Adhyaya X. Adhyaya XI discusses the reiation of subsidiary
 to principal offerings, and Adhyaya XII discusses the topic of
 options or Vikalpa. These last might be लाला Vyavasthita, fixed
 or limited and Avyavasthita, that is to say, unlimited (see Keith.
 Chapter V).
 The rules of Interpretation which the Mimansa laid down were
 later utilised by the Dharma-sastras for the interpretation of their
 texts, and they may also be found to harmonise with the rules
 of   interpretation   which   modern   Law    employs.    Compare
 Maxwell.?!
 The first rule of the interpretation of Statutes is that words and
 sentences   must be construed     in their ordinary    and   natural
 meaning, which is the same as the Mimaimsa rule of Sruti.
 Another rule is that conflict between the statutes is not to be
 readily assumed, which is the same as laid down in Mimamsa,



 91   Maxwell’s book, On Interpretation of Statutes is a well known,

      indeed a classic work on law. The fourth impression of the 12th
      edition (1976) is published by N. M. Tripathi Pvt. Ltd., 164
      Samaldas Gandhi Marg, Bombay 400 002, 1981.



                                 276
                                                      Puirva Mimamsa


that an option is to be declared only in the last resort. Another
tule is that we must take into account the history ofthe causes
ofan enactment corresponding to the mle of Prakarana or context
in Mimamsa. Another rule is that all the parts of a statute are to
be construed together and each part is not to be construed
separately, which corresponds to the rule of Vakya or syntactical
connection in Mimamsa.92

The Mimamsa tells us that the principal word in a sentence is

the verb, and chat the subject is, therefore subordinate; and hence
the attributes or Visesanas ofthe subject are not to be supposed
to be intended as a necessary part ofthe rule or proposition. The
king is entitled to the tax on land, and not to the land itself. The
sovereign ruler is not an absolute owner of his kingdom, and
can not make a    gift of it. In the Visvajit sacrifice where every

thing is to be given away, the sacrificer can not make a gift of
the things deposited with him, or of his wife, or of his children.

Then again there is arguing from Similars; thus for example, if
a NisAda is allowed to offer a Rudrayaga, if follows that a Sidra
ean also do so.

An Arthavada93 is not a positive command like Vidhi. When
Mitaksara says that one should kill without deliberation even a

Studies in Indian Philosophy


Guru, a child, an old man, or a learned Brahmin if he comes as
an Atatayin, this is merely an Arthavada and not a Vidhi or
command. That sons have not the competence to divide in their
father’s   life-time,   is   merely   an   Arthavada   extolling   the
prohibition to do so.

Kratvartha Vidhis are those which are of absolute obligation,
and Purusartha Vidhis are those which are addressed to the
conscience of the agent. A breach of the first brings on sin, of
the second only blame or censure. Compare Duties of perfect
and imperfect obligation in Kant.94 When Yajiiavalkya says that
one should marry a girl who is free from disease, who has a
brother, and who is not of the same Gotra or Pravara, the last is
 Kratvartha and the first two are Purusartha. When Manu tells us
 that the son to be adopted is to be given by the mother or father
 "in distress", the "distress" is not Kratvartha but Purusartha.

 Krama is illustrated in allowing, e.g. a mother a prior claim, for
 the word pitarau is explained in grammatical treatises as Mata
 pitarau which means mother and father and not father and mother.
 Sometimes, however, there is difficulty about the understanding


 94   Duties of perfect obligation are (i) perfection of oneself and
      (ii) happiness of others. We should avoid perfection of others
      because if we try to perfect others it would be restraining their
      freedom. Again we need not pursue our happiness as a duty
      because we naturally seek it. Now duties of imperfect obligation
      are less strict. If we do them they bring on merit; but if we do
      not do them they do not bring on sin. Doing such acts is a virtue

      but not doing it, is not a vice but moral weakness. If you save
      a drowning man it is virtue; if you drown him more it is vice;

      but if you do not do either, it is moral weakness (See E. Caird,
      The Critical Philosophy of Kant, Vol. II, Glassgow, James,
      Maclehose and Sons, second edition, 1909, Pp. 352-355).



                                  278
                                                   Piirva Mimamsa


of this Krama, e.g. when Yajiiavalkya says that the heirs of a
hermit are the teacher, the good pupil, and a fellow student in
order, the Mitaksara says that the order is to be reversed as
common sense would require it. We may not, however, agree
with Mitaksara.

Options are always an improper procedure, as both texts are
पला nullified. Hence if possible an endeavour should be made
to explain the negative proposition as an exception (Paryudasa)?9
or as an Anuvada.

The Anuvada is merely a variety of Arthavada, as in the Vedic
sentence ‘Fire should not be consecrated on the bare ground, nor
in the sky, nor in heaven.’ Every one knows that no one can
consecrate fire in the sky, and therefore this sentence is a mere
Anuvada for praising the Vidhi that fire must be consecrated on
ground with gold. As an illustration of the Nityanuvada we can
give the instance that a man can donate what he owns, but there
is no ownership ina deposit, or in wife, or children, ¢.g., Dharma
and Draupadi. This prohibition repeats only what is well-known.
When, again, Manu apparently both allows and condemns
Niyoga, the Mitaksara tells us that this is to be understood in 8
"limited" sense, viz., that a maiden may marry the deceased’s
brother, if the intended husband dies before actual marriage.
Manusmrti (LX. 59) defines Nioyoga as follows:
       Devaradva sapindadva striya samyan-niyuktaya

      Prajepsitadhigantavya santanasya pariksaye.
In this way Pandu and Dhrtarastra were born from Vyasa from
the widows of Vicitravirya.

plural, the Mimamsi illustrates this by enjoining that when it is
said that a vessel is to be cleansed, it is implied that all vessels
are to be cleansed.

Finally, the fault called Vakyabheda occurs when one and the
same sentence is construed as laying down two different rules
or Vidhis, vide Sabarabhasya P. 196 and ए. 4497 for examples
of Vakyabheda (see Kane).
V. The Ethical Ideal of theMimamsa
1. Jaimini’s division of the Veda in five parts. The mass of the
Vedas being vast, Jaimini divides it into five parts: Vidhi,
Arthavada, Namadheya, Pratisedha and Mantra.

Relations of ideas and matters of fact.98 Sometimes Arthavada
may not be a fact. ०.४. (i) Rama says to Cara: अर्थवादः खल्वेषः दोषं
तु मे कि ंचि त्‌कथय ७८. (उत्तररामचरि त).99        (11) वर्हि षि रजतं न देयम्‌, says

98    "The distinction is not merely introduced by Hume but is
      emphasized by him. Leibnitz has talked of necessary truths and
      contingent truths; Hume talks of relations of ideas and relations
      of objects (matters of fact). The first is the way in which
      consistency is demonstrated, it is the deductive way of reasoning
      and is now called ‘formal implication’. The second is the
      argument from probability or an inductive way of reasoning.
      The former gives us formal validity, the latter, material truth.
      By the first, Hume argues, we will never reach matters of fact,
      it always takes the form of hypothetical reasoning; if P were so
      and so, q would be so and so; it will never tell us what P or q
      exactly are, or whether they actually are at all... The second
      way talks of actual things. Whatever is treated in the former
      way is only an epistemological existence but the latter claims
      an ontological being." (S. 5. Barlingay and Padma Kulkarni, A
      Critical Survey of Western Philosophy, P. 186).

3. Dharma: the Ideal in earlier Mimamsi. It is the Vidhis which
can lay down Dharma. The Ideal in earlier Mimamsa is Dharma,
Artha and Kama being subordinated to it. Nitya and Kamya-
karmas constitute Dharma and Pratisiddha-karmas constitute
Adharma. The Kamya may be and Pratisiddha Karmas must be
avoided; but Nitya-karmas cannot be so avoided. The Ideal in
later Mimamsa is Moksa.

       offers a chance for the world to be altogether good. Again, as
       a psychological fact, freedom is there, because we feel ourselves
       to be free. So psychologically indeterminism is accepted.
       Pragmatically it holds out the hope for a better future (See R.
       B. Perry, Present Philosophical Tendencies, Pp. 371 ff).
 116    In meting out justice the 474 acts as the supreme authority.
       And as the supreme principle it is equal to God in metaphysics.
 117   When अपूर्वं which is itself an indeterminate principle, is given
       the function of bestowing rewards and punishments, the moral
       theory becomes lame or crippled. And this chance-principle
       discharging moral functions on its own, with little regard for
       God, makes theism lamer or highly ineffective.

119   Prabhakara’s view of error is known as अख्याति or ‘no-know-
      ledge’. It is actually ‘no-knowledge’ of the difference between
      the rope and the serpent. In Kumarila a rope is said to be
      mistaken for a serpent because there is a wrong synthesis of
      the two things. At the same time Kumirila says that it is a
      unitary experience because there is no combination of two
      elements as the single object appears to be what it is 101; it is
      distorted - वि परीत. Kumirila’s view 15 known as वि परीतख्याति .
      The distortion is due to previous संस्कार$ or impressions of the
      serpent. अख्याति is an error of omission because the difference
      between the rope and the serpent is not noticed; it is a fallacy
      of non-observation, On the other hand, वि परीतख्याति     is an error
      of commission because in the place of a rope something else
      (serpent) is seen, though there is no warrant for it; it is a fallacy
      of   mal-observation    (See    Hiriyanna,   Outlines    of   Indian
      Philosophy, Pp. 313-317).
120   The criticism that Vedanta levels against अपूर्व 15 contained in
      Brahma-sitra 11, 2. 38. Sankara in his commentary on this 50078
      points out that as YY is non-intelligent, it cannot operate unless
      there is some initiative from a conscious principle. Moreover,
      there is no criterion or प्रमाण to prove the existence of Apirva.

Apurvamimamsa and Vedanta is really Purvamimamsa as it

5. Moksa, the Ideal in Later Mimamsa. The conception of Moksa
in Mimamsia. For Prabhakara’s and Kum§rila’s opinions on this
head, see next point. "The Mimamsi refutes the Vedantic view
that the physical world is sublated or transcended in the state of
Moksa. The world endures in the same form, even if the soul
becomes free.121 Cf. also Mimamsi criticism of the Vedantic
"Absolute" and of "Nescience."

VI. Prabhakara and Kumarila
The     two    great     Mimamsa       philosophers        :    Prabhakara      and
Kumirila, one inclining, on the whole, more towards Nyaya, the
other inclining, on the whole, more towards Vedanta. The
chronological relationship between Prabhakara and Kumiarila :
according to some, Prabhakara is earlier; while according to
others Kumirila is earlier. These latter say that Guru was a title
bestowed       by   Kumarila      on    his   disciple     Prabhakara,        when
Prabhakara intelligently interpreted अत्रापि नोक्तम्

132 "In course of study, the Teacher came by a sentence— अत्रतुनोक्त
      तत्रापि नोक्तं इति द्वि रुक्तम्|‌ the apparent meaning of this was— ‘This
      has not been mentioned here, nor has it been mentioned there,
      so it has been mentioned twice’; and this was an absurdity which
      puzzled the teacher and the pupils.alike; after some cogitation,
      the teacher retired to rest; before he returned, Prabhakara put
      a mark between अत्र and तु and another upon त्रा which altered
      the whole structure of the sentence, अत्र 'तुना' उक्तं तत्र अपि ना"
    उक्तम्‌इति द्वि रुक्तम्‌, which thereby was found to mean— ‘Here this
    idea has been expressed by the particle तु and there the
                                                                              same
    idea has been expressed by the particle अपि , hence
                                                         it has been
    expressed twice’. When the teacher returned, he
                                                     saw the marks
    and found the difficulty easily solved; and having
                                                          found out
    who had supplied the key to it, he was very highly pleased
                                                                 and
    bestowed upon Prabhakara, the title of ‘GuruTM (Ganga Nath

 133 Prabhakara recognises absolute distinctness of one category
      from another. Thus substance, attribute, universal, particular,
      are all distinct. So are the material cause and its effect. There
      is thus no possibility of the cause transforming into the effect. ,
      And hence Parinama-vada (transformationism) is excluded.
      Prabhakara does not accept Abhaya or negation because his
      contention   is   that Abhava can always be        represented   as
      something positive. Absence of a book on a           table can be
      expressed positively as bare table. If Abava as a category does
      not exist, there is no necessity of an independent criterion to
      know Abhava. So Anupalabdhi as a criterion has no raison
      d'etre.
 134 According to Kumiarila the relation between the material cause

      and its effect is viewed as Bhedabheda or identity-in-difference.
     The effect is not a new creation, hence there is Abheda. But
     the effect is a transformation; hence there is Bheda. Milk and

      curds are different; so there is Bheda. But milk itself is

      doctrine    of Satkaryavada.          Again Kumiarila’s doctrine of
      Bhedabheda is also advocated by Nimbarka. He interprets the
      Brahma-sitra III. 2.27 to imply Bhedabheda. The serpent looks
      different (Bheda) from its coils; yet it is the same (Abheda) as
      the coils. "The above discussion seems to prove convincingly
      that Badarayana’s philosophy was some kind of Bhedabheda-
      vada or a theory of transcendence and immanence of God
      (Brahman) - even in the light of Sahkara’s own commentary"

      category.    As   none    of    the    Pramanas,    namely    Pratyaksa,
      Anumina, Upamana, Sabda or Arthapatti is competent to
      cognise Abhava, a special criterion, namely Anupalabdhi is
      requisitioned by Kumiarila. (For Foot Notes 133 and 134 see

136   While dealing ७(॥ सर्वज्ञात्ममुनि ७ doctrine in the class-room,
      Prof. Ranade observed that this was a heresy of the ultra-
      Sankarites. Brahman is not so cheap as to be had in सुषुत्ति . Here
      the bliss (the so called bliss) is of unconsciousness and not of
      self-consciousness, which alone is real bliss. Bliss is experienced
      in the ecstatic state and not in deep sleep. In deep sleep there
      is happiness and not bliss; and this happiness is inferred when
      one wakes up. Actually it means that there was no pain during
      sleep. Bliss is qualitatively different from mere happiness. Prof.
      Ranade deals with this topic in Pathway to God in Hindi
      Literature, Pp. 212—213.
 137 According to Prabhakara, the Atman or self is never an object

      of knowledge; 1.6. it is not knowable. The self is always the
      subject of knowledge and is revealed simultaneously with the
      object. This self-revelation does not mean that Atman is self-
      luminous. It is Jada or unconscious. See also Hiriyanna,
      Outlines of Indian Philosophy, Pp. 302-307.
 138 Kumiarila holds that the self acts as both subject and object of

      knowledge. He believes in Parinama i.e. modal change in the
      self. Jnana is said to be a mode of the self. When modal change
      is accepted, the self can as well be an object of knowledge. The
      self is known through Aham-pratyaya or ‘I-notion’. It arises
      whenever the self knows an object. It must be remembered,
      however, that though Aham-pratyaya is there, the Aham or self
      is not known as the subject; it is known as an object. In any

139 The expression ‘I am I’ is traceable to Kant. It means the unity

     of self-consciousness. The ideal of knowledge would consist in
     the object being one with the self. The object-self is one with
     the subject-self in pure self-consciousness. This is the unity of
     the subject and the object as in Kumarila. Now as Kumiarila
    says that the self to be known at all must at the same time be
    aware of some object, i.e. the self knows itself only when
    knowing an object, Kant also contends that, an empty ‘I am’
    —a judgment of self-consciousness which did not involve at the
    same time a consciousness of a world of objects - would be

    impossible (See E. Caird, the Critical Philosophy of Kant, Vol.
    1, James Maclehose and Sons, Glasgow; Second edition, 1909,
    P. 373). The consciousness of objects is essential for the
    consciousness of self. Ibid. Pp. 368-374; also M. Hiriyanna,
    Outlines of Indian Philosophy, Pp. 305-306. This is comparable
    to अस्मि   ता (bare unqualified awareness of oneself) in Yoga

146 "Error is a qualification of a reality in such a way that in the
      result it has an inconsistent content, which for that reason is
      rejected” (ह. H. Bradley, Appearance and Reality, Oxford, At
      the Clarendon Press, 9th Impression, 1930, P. 167). "Error is
      truth, it is partial truth, that is false only because’ partial and
      left incomplete" (Ibid. P. 169). "We have pointed out that it is
      at least possible for errors to correct themselves, and, as such,
      to disappear in a higher experience. But, if so, we must affirm
      that they are thus absorbed and made good. For what is
      possible, and what a general principle compels us to say must
      be, that certainly is" (Ibid. P. 173). "But with this we have arrived
      at the meeting ground of error and truth. There willl be no
      truth which is entirely true, just as there will be no error which
      is totally false.... truth and error, measured by the Absolute,

Satkhyati (Ramanuja).147 Macbeth’s dagger real.148 Cf. the


    must each be subject always to degree" (Ibid. Pp. 320-321).
147 Sat-khyati means that Sat (that which exists) is alone cognised
    (khyati). There can be no knowledge if there is no real object.
     For knowledge there must be a corresponding object. All
     knowledge is thus true. A difficulty however arises because
     errors do occur. Ramanuja’s theory of Satkhyati must give
     explanation of error. A conch is mistaken for silver because
     there is similarity between the two as regards lustre (चाकचक्य).
     This similarity is due to the real presence of particles of silver
     in the conch. The particles may be extremely smail in number
      (अल्प) but they are there and their presence gives rise to error
     — viz. seeing silver where there is only a conch. The explanation
     given about the yellowness wrongly seen in the white conch js
     different. Ramanuja says that the yellowness situated in the eye
     of the jaundiced person travels to the conch in the process of
     seeing and therefore the conch is scen as yellow. The yellowness
      obscures the whiteness ofthe conch. See M. Hiriyanna, Outlines
      of Indian Philosophy, Pp. 390 ff. (See 8150 श्रीभाष्य, published
      by श्री नि ंबार्का चार्य पीठ, Pp. 172-176).
 148 In Macbeth we have Macbeth’s soliloquy where he is addressing

      a dagger. In fact the dagger is nothing but a hallucination:
      Is this a dagger which I see before me,..
      I have thee not, and yet I 5९९ thee still
      >> Or art thou but
      A dagger of the mind, a false creation,
      Proceeding from the heat-oppressed brain?
      I see thee yet, in form as palpable
     As this which now I draw.                      (Act Two Scene 1).
     Macbeth sees the dagger as a stark reality. Ramanuja’s realism
     will have to explain the vision of the dagger which has no
     corresponding object. The dagger, Ramanuja would say, is not

152 Asatkhyati means knowledge of the unreal. When there are no
      external objects there is neither knowledge nor error. A self
      knowing an object is meaningless because the self is a void and
      the object is a void.
153 The expression Atmakhyati (knowledge of the self) is a
      misnomer, because Buddhism does not accept the self (Atman)
      at all. The Vijnanavadin’s theory of error is based on their
      metaphysics that the external world does not exist. The silver
      in the shell-silver does not exist in the external world. It is an
      idea in the mind of the perceiver which appears to be external.
154 See Foot Note 119 above.
155 Anyaihikhyati means knowing otherwise. This is the view

      advocated by Nyaya which is realistic. In the shell-silver illusion,



                                   299
Studies in Indian Philosophy


(कुमारि ल). The shell forsakes its nature, and appears as silver.156
Anirvacaniyakhyati of Advaitins. Beyond Being or not-Being.
Neither Real nor Unreal. Unknowable, Inexplicable, Ineffable.
Agnosticism.157     Connected      with:    (i)   Adhyasa     or   super-
imposition of bodily characteristics on self (Ksama-ksina)158 :
of world on God.159 (ii) Vivarta, maltransformation opposed to


     both the silver and the shell are real. The silver is in the shop
     (Apanastha). Error occurs because there is wrong synthesis of
     the shell which is presented and silver which is elsewhere. In
     other words, there is wrong synthesis of the shell which is

     presented and silver which is remembered.
156 See Foot Note 119 above.
157 Anirvacaniyakhyati implies indescribability. We cannot say that
     silver is being 1.6. existent; for, the silver seen on the shell does
     not exist. Nor can we say that the silver does not exist as it is
     actually perceived. It is neither being nor not-being but is
     beyond them. The same is expressed by saying that it is neither
     real nor unreal. Hence no knowledge of a thing can be had.
     When a    thing cannot be categorised as real or unreal it is
     inexplicable and defies expression. This unknowabii.ty leads to
     agnosticism.
158 Anirvacaniyata is a result of Adhyadsa or          superimposition.
     Through Avidya or nescience one thing is superimposed on
     some other thing. By identifying himself with his kith and kin,
     a man takes on their enjoyment or suffering on himself; he
     identifies himself with his body and takes on himself bodily
     characteristics, e.g. he says      I am emaciated and worn out
     (क्षामक्षीण), when as a matter of fact, it is the body which is so,
     the eternal Self being uncontaminated by conditions.
159 By the same process of Adhyasa a man superimposes the world
     on God; he regards the unreal world as real, the real nature cf
     Brahman being concealed by Adhyasa or Maya.

160 Vivarta means unreal appearance caused by Avidya. The object
    undergoes a transformation but the transformation is not real
     as in Parinamavada of the Sankhyas (e.g. milk changing into
     curds) but a mal-transformation or distorted perception. The
     classical examples given are— रज्जुसर्प, the rope appearing as
     serpent or शुक्ति    कारजत, the conch appearing as silver.
161 The Prabhakara school adheres to the dictum: duty for the sake
      of duty. One should perform one’s duty because it is the
      mandate of the Vedas and there is no room here for any
      consideration of pleasure and pain. In other words, it is not the
      thought of pleasure and pain but the consciousness of duty
      (Karyata-jiana) that should motivate a man to action.
162 Contrasted with the teaching of Prabhakara, 1९01181113 holds
    that pleasure and pain motivate a man to action. Acquisition
      of pleasure or Ananda is positive, while destruction of pain
      (Durita-ksaya) is negative in nature. Behind the performance
      of duties, there is the consciousness of satisfying a desire
      (Istasadhanata-jiana). The Karyata-jnana of Prabhakara and
      Istasadhanata-jnana of Kumarila roughly correspond to the
      Bonum Supremum and Bonum Consummatum of Kant. The
      former implies rigid performance of duty with no thought of
      pleasure, the latter recognises the place of happiness in the
      completed good though happiness should not be a motive to
      do duty.

     170 The five points noted under this head refer to the development
           of ideas from Kumarila to Advaita Vedanta. One has to
           remember, however, that the development of doctrines here is
           logical and not chronological. Advaita Vedanta had already
           reached the logical end of doctrines before Kumarila came on
           the scene, with his halting positions. Borrowing a phrase from
           Falckenberg, we can say that history was not obliging enough
           to carry out the development with chronological accuracy; for,
           she had Advaitism complete the doctrines before Kumiarila
           prepared the way.
     171 In Prabhakara there is भेद, (see Foot Note 133 above). In
           Kumiarila there is भेदाभेद, (see Foot Note 134 above). In Advaita
           Vedanta there is अभेद or identity. Hence we 5९९ a process from
           भेद in Prabhakara to अभेद in Advaita Vedanta through the
           intermediate link of भेदाभेद in Kumirila.
     172    Prabhakara holds that the self is जड. In Kumarila the self is a
           fully conscious entity. In Advaita Vedanta the self is illuminator
           and bliss. Here again, there is a passage from a जड self in
           Prabhakara to an आनंदमय self in Advaita Vedanta and Kumarila
           occupies an intermediate position by holding that the self is a
           fully conscious entity.

The tradition related about Kumarila and Sankara has got a great

173 Kumiarila clears the passage from an unconscious self to a
      self-conscious entity
                        by his doctrine of conscious self.
                                                        In Advaita
      Vedanta, there is not merely self-consciousness but self-
      luminousness and self-revelation as well. Here the self
                                                            5 स्वसंवि त्‌
      and स्वप्रकाश, the latter attribute implying that the self not only
      reveals itself but reveals other objects also
174   Kumirila’s ethics is teleological because it recognises an end
      (see Foot Note 162 above). Advaita Vedanta identifies bliss
      with Brahman. The bliss is not only an end but the End. If in
      Kumiarila happiness is only an operational motive, in Advaitism
      bliss is the sole moving force. It may be compared to Aristotle’s
      Theoria, the supreme, thinking and blissful Being. Hence in
      Vedanta, as in Aristotle, Bliss constitutes the metaphysico-

      moral end.
175 As a matter of fact, there is not much difference between

      वि परीतख्याति   and वि वर्तख्याति . 1787118 says that in error there
      is a wrong synthesis of two things (rope and serpent) because
      of which the object appears distorted (वि परीत). The Advaita
      Vedanta puts forth almost the same view by saying that the
      serpent is super-imposed on the rope (अध्यास). See Foot Note
      119 above.                           ~
176 A staunch believer in the Vedas, Kumarila was
                                                    prepared to
    stake his life for establishing their supremacy. Once
                                                           it so
    happened that the declaration of his faith began with
                                                               a
      conditional clause: "If the Vedas are true..." At once a thought




                                     305
Studies in Indian Philosophy




     struck him that his language at least apparently doubted the
     authority of the Vedas. The very utterance of the conditional
     clause implied irreverence for the Vedas. Overcome by deep
     repentance, he decided to immolate himself. At this juncture,
     Sankara who was travelling through the length and breadth of
     India for vindicating Vedanta, came there and threw down the
     gauntlet. Kumarila was undergoing self-imposed Tusanala
     punishment which consisted in twisting dry straw round a
     person’s limbs and setting it on fire. Kumirila said that he was
     on the point of departing and hence there was no time for any
     argument. He suggested that Sankara should go to Mandana
     Misra —    Kumirila’s disciple — who would       represent   the
     Mimamsa stand. How in the argument between Sankara and
     Mandana the latter was won over, is history. The underlying
     meaning of the tradition is that Vedanta could         consume
     Mimamsa.

                     Bibliographical Note



A. 8. Keith: Karma-Mimamsa, Chapter I is historical
containing among other things an account of Prabhakara and
Kumiarila. Chapter II is epistemological containing an account
of स्वतः प्रामाण्य 35 well as an account of the various Pramanas
comparatively with those of Nyaya. The account of अर्था पत्ति ,
                                                      3 or verbal
अनुपलब्धि may be read; but एक्षा10::८:1५ the account of
testimony as the Vedas themselves play such an important part
in the system. Chapter III contains the very celebrated criticism
by Kumarila of Buddhist nihilism and idealism; also an account
of theMimamsa doctrine of categories comparatively with those
of न्याय-वैशेषि क. Chapter IV contains a very good account of a
disproof of God and a proof of the soul. Chapter V deals with
ritual interpretation and Chapter VI with Hindu Law (for this
see Ganga Nath Jha, Prabhakara School).

Ganga Nath Jha: Translation of Kumarila Bhatta’s Tantra-
vartika, Asiatic Society of Bengal, 1924; 2 Vols. with a fine
Introduction "On the Tantravartika and its author Kumarila" by
Pt. Gopinath Kaviraj, Pp. v to xviii. Also a small 4 page
Introduction of Dr. Ganga Nath Jha. The brief Introduction of
Ganga Nath Jha contains reply to Pasupatinatha Shastri’s
criticism on the author’s work on the Prabhakara School.
Gopinath Kaviraj discusses Kumarila and Buddhism; Kumarila’s
last scene; Kumarila’s date etc. in Introduction to Vol. I.

Ganga Nath Jha: Translation of Sloka-vartika, Asiatic Society
of Bengal, 1900 with a very fine Introduction by the author, of
45 pages on the "Parva Mimamsa System", Slokavartika (in
verse) I. i of Kumarila’s work is the Tarkapada, given to
metaphysics and epistemology. The excellent Introduction of
Ganga Nath Jha contains discussions of Dharma (Page iii); the
means of knowing Dharma viz., Veda (Page v); The word as

meaning of Vidhi; अपूर्ववि धि , नि यमवि धि , परि संख्यावि धि   (Pp. xviii and

Ganga Nath Jha:             "Mimamsa Sutras of Jaimini" translated by
 Ganga Nath Jha (Sacred Books of the Hindus Vol. X) with his
 own English commentary. Only Sutras in Chapter 1 to 3 1.6.
 Sloka-vartika and Tantravartika, the remaining 9 chapters being
 Tup Tika). The very brief Introduction contains an account in
 English (P. 11) of the contents of the 12 Adhyayas. The translation

 three Adhyayas.

 Ganga Nath Jha:            Prabhakara   School      of    Putrva-Mimamsa
 (Doctorate Thesis 1911). Very important work on Prabhakara
 particularly, and Prabhakara and Kumarila generally. Chapter I
 is historical and discusses the relation of Prabhakara to Kumarila.

                                             314
If a full exposition of a problem from the
pen of a master is delightful, suggestions
from him for further study are challeng-
ing. Prof. R. D. Ranade’s book is a critical
survey of Sankhya, Yoga, Buddhism,
Jainism, Nyaya-Vaisesika and Purva-
mimamsa. In the present annotated
edition,   the   author’s     aphorism-like
observations are explained for the benefit
of readers, especially students of philo-
sophy. Documentation of references in
the   original   text   are   provided   for
researchers who want to go to the sources.
The work in hand provides ample food
 for thought to any one who wishes to
launch upon a deep study of philosophy
 and religion.
